First Motivation: Removing the Veil of Taqlid
Sanusi states his first motivation: removing the veil of taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Imam al-Sanusi's first concern was removing the veil of blind imitation (taqlid), which he saw as a widespread disease wrongly defended by some.

Core Idea
Imam Sanusi wrote to tear the veil of taqlid, not to defend it.

Before presenting his theological arguments, Imam al-Sanusi reveals his motivations. His first great concern was the epidemic of blind imitation. He considered it a disease that had become widespread, and worse, some had begun to defend the imitators, falsely calling them inheritors of the righteous Salaf. This lesson focuses on that first concern.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the first concern
Read the Arabic passage and translation focusing on the first concern: 'رفع غاشية التقليد'. Notice how the author describes the problem and the misguided defense.
The term 'غاشية' implies a covering or veil that blinds.
2
Understand the metaphor
The Imam calls taqlid a 'ghāshiya' (veil) that has become widespread. Reflect on why he uses such a strong image.
3
Consider the criticism of defenders
The passage criticizes those who defend taqlid, claiming they make the imitators inheritors of the Salaf. Think about why this would be a serious concern for the Imam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه الدندنة سترى لها صدىً في هذا الكتاب الذي بين يديك ، وعناية كبرى من قبل الإمام السنوسي ، وكُتُبه التي خطَّتها يراعتُه لها سعي حثيث في أمورٍ ؛ لعل أجلها أمران رئيسان قد أهما الإمام فلهجَ بذكر النجاة منهما ؛ وهما : الأول : رفع غاشية التقليد التي عم بلاؤها ، وحديثُ هذه الغاشية أخذَ كمّاً كبيراً من سطور ) شرح العقيدة الوسطى » وغيرها من عقائد الإمام ؛ لما رأى من فشو هذا المرض الذي كاد يأتي على صاحبه على مرأى ومسمع من أطباء أهل زمانه ، بل تراخت العناية حتى سُطرَتْ سطور حشوِيَّةٌ في الدفاع عن المقلدين ، واعتبارهم ورثة السلف الصالحين ! الثاني : رفع عنادِ القاسية قلوبهم عن سماع الحقِّ ، هؤلاء الجبناء الذين يخافون من نسمات اليقين ، ويهجرون مجالس العلم متعمدين ، تعرفُهم بسيماهم ؛ لألسنتهم بذاءة الشوقة ، يحجزون أنفسهم وراء سور تجهيل الآخرين ، ويتحذلقون بثرثرة مأفونة قد ملها مَنْ هم على شاكلتهم فضلاً عن غيرهم ، وقل أن تجد في هؤلاء خيراً .
This humming will have an echo in this book which is in your hands, and there is great care from Imam al-Sanusi, and his books which his pens have written have a diligent pursuit in matters; perhaps the greatest of them are two main concerns that worried the Imam, so he frequently mentioned deliverance from them. They are: First: removing the veil of imitation (taqlid) whose affliction has become widespread, and the discussion of this veil occupied a large amount of the lines of 'Sharh al-Aqida al-Wusta' and other creeds of the Imam; because he saw the prevalence of this disease that almost destroyed its patient in plain sight of the physicians of his time, but rather care lagged until verbose lines were written in defense of the imitators (muqallidin), considering them inheritors of the righteous predecessors (salaf)! Second: removing the stubbornness of those whose hearts are hardened against hearing the truth, those cowards who fear the breezes of certainty, and intentionally abandon circles of knowledge; you recognize them by their appearance; their tongues have the foulness of shamelessness; they confine themselves behind a wall of disparaging others, and they play the smart aleck with sickening chatter that even those like them are bored of, let alone others; and rarely will you find good in these.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is the author's preface stating the two main concerns that motivated Imam al-Sanusi (قدس سره) to write this work. The author uses the metaphor of 'dandana' (humming) to refer to the previous discussion, which will have resonance in the book. He then states that Imam al-Sanusi's writings have a diligent pursuit of certain matters, the greatest being two concerns from which he sought deliverance. The first concern is 'raf' ghāshiyat al-taqlīd'—removing the veil of blind imitation that had become widespread. The author notes that this issue occupies many lines in al-Sanusi's 'Sharh al-Aqida al-Wusta' and other creedal works because the Imam saw the disease widespread, and worse, some wrote verbose defenses of the muqallidin, falsely considering them inheritors of the salaf. The second concern is removing the stubbornness of those with hardened hearts who refuse to hear the truth. The author describes them as cowards who fear certainty, intentionally avoid knowledge circles, are recognized by their foul speech, and isolate themselves behind a wall of disparaging others. The passage ends with a harsh judgment: rarely is any good found in such people. This sets the polemical tone of the book.

Key Terms
التقليد
غاشية التقليد
المقلدون
السلف الصالحين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that blind imitation (taqlid) is acceptable and even praiseworthy, considering the imitators as followers of the Salaf.
Correction: Imam Sanusi explicitly rejects this, calling taqlid a disease and criticizing those who defend it as 'verbose lines' written by careless people.

Checkpoint
What does Imam al-Sanusi compare taqlid to in this passage?
A) A strong fortress
B) A disease that destroys its patient
C) A beautiful garment
D) A source of knowledge

Key Takeaway
Imam Sanusi's first goal is to remove the veil of blind imitation, which he considers a destructive disease wrongly defended by some.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your faith have you been content to follow without understanding, trusting that others have done the thinking for you? Commit to uncovering one belief you hold that you cannot defend from the sources.
?
Imagine yourself as one of the 'physicians' Sanusi criticizes—silent or even defending the same disease. When have you excused ignorance in yourself or others rather than seeking clarity? What will you do today to lift the veil?
?
Do you ever feel confident in your faith simply because you associate with scholars or a group, yet you have not personally wrestled with the evidence? This is the very trap Sanusi warns against. Will you remain in it, or take a step toward real understanding?

Second Motivation: Removing Stubbornness
Sanusi states his second motivation: removing stubbornness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Imam al-Sanusi's second concern was removing the stubbornness of those whose hearts are hardened against hearing the truth, describing them with vivid imagery.

Core Idea
The stubborn are cowards who fear certainty and hide behind disparagement.

The second motivation is equally urgent: addressing those who stubbornly refuse the truth. Imam al-Sanusi does not hold back—he describes them as cowards who flee from certainty, avoid knowledge circles, and use foul language to disparage others. This lesson examines that vivid portrait and its purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the second concern
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the second concern: 'رفع عناد القاسية قلوبهم'. Notice the strong language used.
2
Analyze the description
The passage characterizes the stubborn as having hardened hearts, fearing certainty, avoiding scholars, foul speech, and self-isolation through disparagement. List these traits.
3
Reflect on the closing judgment
The passage ends with 'وَقَلَّ أَنْ تَجِدَ فِي هَؤُلَاءِ خَيْرًا'. Consider why the Imam makes such a harsh statement.

Misconception: One might think that stubbornness in religious matters is a sign of strength or conviction.

According to the passage, what do the stubborn people fear?
A) The punishment of the grave
B) The breezes of certainty

D) The opinions of others

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you found yourself avoiding a difficult truth—about yourself, your faith, or your actions? What did you do to protect yourself from it, and what might it cost you to finally face it?
?
Are there areas of your life where you have become stubborn, refusing to listen to someone who disagrees with you? How can you soften your heart today, even just a little, to allow the breezes of certainty to reach you?
?
Do you ever hide behind criticism of others—pointing out their weaknesses or ignorance—to avoid examining your own shortcomings? What would it take for you to step out from behind that wall and let the truth challenge you instead?

Historical Praise for Sanusi's Creedal Works
Historians al-Shafshawani and Ibn al-Qadi praise Sanusi's five creeds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how historians like al-Shafshawani and Ibn al-Qadi praised Imam al-Sanusi's creedal series and why their praise emphasizes the exceptional quality of his works.

Core Idea
Historians acclaim Sanusi's creeds as unmatched in Islamic literature.

This lesson opens with the voices of two historians who declared Imam al-Sanusi's five creedal works among the best ever authored in Islam. Their praise establishes the high esteem in which the Sanusiyyat series has been held since the author's lifetime. Let's examine their exact words and the significance of their testimony.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two historians' statements
Find the direct quotes from al-Shafshawani and Ibn al-Qadi. Note the specific wording of their praise for Sanusi's creeds.
Al-Shafshawani names all five books; Ibn al-Qadi speaks generally.
3
Reflect on why this praise matters
Ask yourself why these historians felt compelled to highlight Sanusi's series. Consider that the passage calls al-Shafshawani's statement a 'golden word' in evaluating the Sanusiyyat.
This connects to the learning objective about the exceptional quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلام في السلسلة العقدية للامام السنوسي قال العلامة المؤرخ الشفشاوني في الإمام السنوسي : ( وعقائده الخمس وشروحاتهـا مِـنْ أفضل ما أُلف في الإسلام ؛ وهي : المقدمة » ، و« الصغرى » ، وصغرى الصغرى » ، و الوسطى و الكبرى ( ) (۱) ، وهي كلمة ذهبية في تقويم ( السنوسيات » واكتفى المؤرخ ابن القاضي المكناسي في التعريف بالإمام السنوسي بقوله : ( صاحب العقائد التي لم يأتِ أحد بمثلها من المتأخرين ((٢) . وسلسلة « السنوسيات ( بشروحها مما تشبّث بذاكرة التاريخ فما كانت لتنسى ، ورغم زحمة التآليف العقدية وتنوعها حجماً ومنهجاً ، ودخولها مكتبة علم الكلام بكل ثقة ورصانة . . كان لـ « السنوسيات » أعظم الأثر في الغرب الإسلامي ثم شرقه ؛ إذ دخلت بقدم راسخة سلسلة التعليم المنهجي لعلم الكلام ؛ بما تميزت عن غيرها من كتب الاعتقاد بالابتداء بمعتصر المختصر الذي تجده في وجيز متونها ، إلى المختصر الذي تراه في كبير المتون ووجيز الشروح ، إلى الوسيط ثم إلى سعة البسيط هذا الترتيب البديع الذي انتهجه إمامنا السنوسي لم يكن بدعاً ؛ فقد سبق إليه من قِبَلِ أعلام علم الكلام ، فلحجة الإسلام الغزالي مثله ؛ في «قواعد العقائد » و « الرسالة القدسية » و« الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد » ، وللعلامة المحقق سعد الدين التفتازاني شِبْهُهُ ؛ في تهذيب المنطق والكلام » و « المقاصد » و « شرح المقاصد » ، وكلاهما من أقصى المشرق الإسلامي ، ولكن البديع في سلسلة الإمام السنوسي هو استحياؤه وكشفه الإلهامي عن تربع هذه السلسلة المباركة مناحي النظر والتدريس في علم العقائد في المستقبل القريب ، بل إنَّ تأليفه هذه عانقتها أنظار العلماء وأقلامهم في حياته قبل موته ، كلُّ هذا كان مدعاة له أن يتفرّس وجوه طلبة المستقبل مُلِمّاً بضعف الضعيف وبلادة ذهنه ، وشرود النصف النصيف في وكدورة وهم الذكي على حدته ونباهته ، واكتفاء الموفق بلمحته وإشارته كل هذا هو بعض ما أدركه الإمام السنوسي لتنفذ همته في إنشاء مدرسة كلامية لها منهاجها وأسلوبها ، تبدأ حدودها بصبيان الكتاب ، وعامَّة أهل الصنائع والحِرَف الذين لا شغل لهم بالعلم ، بل بالنسوة اللاتي همهنَّ بيوتهنَّ وقضاء حوائجهنَّ ، لتصل إلى العالم المحقق
Speech concerning the creedal series of Imam al-Sanusi. The historian al-Shafshawani said concerning Imam al-Sanusi: 'His five creeds and their commentaries are among the best authored in Islam; they are: al-Muqaddimah, al-Sughra, Sughra al-Sughra, al-Wusta, and al-Kubra ( ) (1). And it is a golden word in evaluating the Sanusiyyat.' And the historian Ibn al-Qadi al-Miknasi sufficed in defining Imam al-Sanusi by saying: 'The author of creeds that none among the later scholars has produced the like of.' (2). And the series of the Sanusiyyat with its commentaries clung to the memory of history so that it was not forgotten, and despite the crowding of creedal works and their diversity in size and method, and their entry into the library of kalam with full confidence and sophistication, al-Sanusiyyat had the greatest impact in the Islamic West and then its East; for it entered with a firm foot into the systematic educational series of kalam; by virtue of its distinction from other creedal books by beginning with the most concise of the abridgement which you find in the brief of its texts, to the abridgement which you see in the large texts and the brief of commentaries, to the intermediate, then to the capacity of the extensive. This wonderful arrangement which our Imam al-Sanusi followed was not an innovation; for it had been preceded by the leading figures of kalam, such as the Proof of Islam al-Ghazali in his Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id, al-Risalah al-Qudsiyyah, and al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad; and the verifying scholar Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani had something similar in his Tahdhib al-Mantiq wa al-Kalam, al-Maqasid, and Sharh al-Maqasid, both from the far Islamic East. But the wonderful thing in the series of Imam al-Sanusi is his uncovering and inspired discovery that this blessed series would occupy the domains of consideration and teaching in the science of creeds in the near future. Rather, his authorship of these works was embraced by the eyes and pens of scholars during his lifetime before his death. All of this was a reason for him to discern the faces of future students, taking into account the weakness of the weak and the dullness of his mind, the inattention of the mediocre in perplexity and the suspicion of the intelligent in his acuteness and cleverness, and the satisfaction of the successful one with his glance and gesture. All of this is some of what Imam al-Sanusi realized, to carry out his determination to establish a kalam school with its own methodology and style, whose boundaries begin with the children of the elementary school and the common people of trades and crafts who have no occupation with knowledge, rather with the women whose concern is their houses and fulfilling their needs, to reach the verifying scholar.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is an editorial introduction that presents two main elements: first, historical praise for Imam al-Sanusi's creedal works, and second, a detailed description of the graded pedagogical structure of his series, the 'Sanusiyyat'. The passage begins by quoting two historians. Al-Shafshawani declares that al-Sanusi's five creedal texts (al-Muqaddimah, al-Sughra, Sughra al-Sughra, al-Wusta, al-Kubra) along with their commentaries are among the best works composed in Islam. Ibn al-Qadi al-Miknasi adds that no later scholar has produced creeds equal to al-Sanusi's. This establishes the high regard for al-Sanusi's works. The passage then explains why the Sanusiyyat series became so influential: despite the many other creedal works, the Sanusiyyat entered the systematic curriculum of kalam with a firm presence because of its distinctive method. That method is a graded progression: it begins with the most concise abridgment (al-mukhtasar), then a slightly larger abridgment, then an intermediate work, and finally an extensive commentary. This 'wonderful arrangement' (al-tartib al-badi') was not unprecedented; al-Ghazali and al-Taftazani had similarly structured series. However, the unique feature of al-Sanusi's series is its prophetic insight: he foresaw that this series would become central to teaching future generations. He designed it to cater to all levels of students, from the weak and dull-witted to the intelligent and sharp, and even to women and common people. His goal was to establish a 'kalam school' with a clear methodology, starting from the most basic level (children and artisans) up to the level of the verifying scholar. Thus, the passage emphasizes the pedagogical intentionality behind the Sanusiyyat.

Key Terms
السلسلة العقدية
العقائد الخمس
المقدمة
الصغرى
صغرى الصغرى
الوسطى
الكبرى
السنوسيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Sanusi series is just one book or random writings.
Correction: The historians emphasize it is a deliberate series of five graded works and their commentaries, unmatched by later scholars.

Checkpoint
Which of the following did al-Shafshawani say about Sanusi's creeds?
A) They are too long.
B) They are among the best authored in Islam.
C) They are only for beginners.
D) They were written for the general public.

Key Takeaway
Historians consistently rank Sanusi's five creeds and their commentaries as unmatched in Islamic intellectual history.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Historians declare Sanusi's creeds 'among the best authored in Islam' and 'unmatched by later scholars.' Have you ever studied even one of these five works? If not, what is preventing you from picking up al-Muqaddimah today and starting your journey into precise creed?
?
Ibn al-Qadi says 'none among later scholars has produced the like' of these creeds. Do you treat the texts you study—whether short or long—with the same reverence that the early scholars had for these works? Or do you rush through them without appreciating their status?
?
The series moves from al-Muqaddimah (introduction) to al-Kubra (greatest). Are you arrogant enough to skip the simpler levels, assuming you already know the creed? Will you humble yourself and start from the beginning, as the scholars intended?

The Graded Pedagogical Structure of the Sanusiyyat
Text describes the graded structure of the Sanusiyyat series and its educational purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Sanusi arranged his creedal series in a graded progression from concise to extensive, and why this structure was designed to accommodate students at all levels.

Core Idea
Sanusi's series progresses from concise to extensive, tailored for every learner.

After the historians' praise, the passage explains why the Sanusiyyat series became so influential: its ingenious graded structure. Sanusi designed it to move from the most concise summary to the most detailed commentary, intentionally catering to everyone from beginners to advanced scholars. This lesson unpacks that structure and its pedagogical wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of the graded series
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the sequence of levels from the most concise to the most extensive.
The passage uses terms like mu'tasar al-mukhtasar, mukhtasar, wasit, basit.
2
Compare with similar structures by al-Ghazali and al-Taftazani
Notice that Sanusi's graded arrangement was not new; al-Ghazali and al-Taftazani had similar series. But the text says Sanusi's is unique in its inspired insight.
3
Note the unique insight of Sanusi
Identify what the passage calls 'al-badi'' – the wonderful thing: Sanusi's foresight that the series would become central to teaching. He designed it for all levels.
4
Understand the target audience
The passage lists the types of learners Sanusi had in mind: from children to women to the verifying scholar. Reflect on the inclusivity of his design.

كلام في السلسلة العقدية للامام السنوسي قال العلامة المؤرخ الشفشاوني في الإمام السنوسي : ( وعقائده الخمس وشروحاتهـا مِـنْ أفضل ما أُلف في الإسلام ؛ وهي : المقدمة » ، و« الصغرى » ، وصغرى الصغرى » ، و الوسطى و الكبرى ( ) (۱) ، وهي كلمة ذهبية في تقويم ( السنوسيات » واكتفى المؤرخ ابن القاضي المكناسي في التعريف بالإمام السنوسي بقوله : ( صاحب العقائد التي لم يأتِ أحد بمثلها من المتأخرين ((٢) . وسلسلة « السنوسيات ( بشروحها مما تشبّث بذاكرة التاريخ فما كانت لتنسى ، ورغم زحمة التآليف العقدية وتنوعها حجماً ومنهجاً ، ودخولها مكتبة علم الكلام بكل ثقة ورصانة . . كان لـ « السنوسيات » أعظم الأثر في الغرب الإسلامي ثم شرقه ؛ إذ دخلت بقدم راسخة سلسلة التعليم المنهجي لعلم الكلام ؛ بما تميزت عن غيرها من كتب الاعتقاد بالابتداء بمعتصر المختصر الذي تجده في وجيز متونها ، إلى المختصر الذي تراه في كبير المتون ووجيز الشروح ، إلى الوسيط ثم إلى سعة البسيط هذا الترتيب البديع الذي انتهجه إمامنا السنوسي لم يكن بدعاً ؛ فقد سبق إليه من قِبَلِ أعلام علم الكلام ، فلحجة الإسلام الغزالي مثله ؛ في «قواعد العقائد » و « الرسالة القدسية » و« الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد » ، وللعلامة المحقق سعد الدين التفتازاني شِبْهُهُ ؛ في تهذيب المنطق والكلام » و « المقاصد » و « شرح المقاصد » ، وكلاهما من أقصى المشرق الإسلامي ، ولكن البديع في سلسلة الإمام السنوسي هو استحياؤه وكشفه الإلهامي عن تربع هذه السلسلة المباركة مناحي النظر والتدريس في علم العقائد في المستقبل القريب ، بل إنَّ تأليفه هذه عانقتها أنظار العلماء وأقلامهم في حياته قبل موته ، كلُّ هذا كان مدعاة له أن يتفرّس وجوه طلبة المستقبل مُلِمّاً بضعف الضعيف وبلادة ذهنه ، وشرود النصف النصيف في وكدورة وهم الذكي على حدته ونباهته ، واكتفاء الموفق بلمحته وإشارته كل هذا هو بعض ما أدركه الإمام السنوسي لتنفذ همته في إنشاء مدرسة كلامية لها منهاجها وأسلوبها ، تبدأ حدودها بصبيان الكتاب ، وعامَّة أهل الصنائع والحِرَف الذين لا شغل لهم بالعلم ، بل بالنسوة اللاتي همهنَّ بيوتهنَّ وقضاء حوائجهنَّ ، لتصل إلى العالم المحقق
Speech concerning the creedal series of Imam al-Sanusi. The historian al-Shafshawani said concerning Imam al-Sanusi: 'His five creeds and their commentaries are among the best authored in Islam; they are: al-Muqaddimah, al-Sughra, Sughra al-Sughra, al-Wusta, and al-Kubra ( ) (1). And it is a golden word in evaluating the Sanusiyyat.' And the historian Ibn al-Qadi al-Miknasi sufficed in defining Imam al-Sanusi by saying: 'The author of creeds that none among the later scholars has produced the like of.' (2). And the series of the Sanusiyyat with its commentaries clung to the memory of history so that it was not forgotten, and despite the crowding of creedal works and their diversity in size and method, and their entry into the library of kalam with full confidence and sophistication, al-Sanusiyyat had the greatest impact in the Islamic West and then its East; for it entered with a firm foot into the systematic educational series of kalam; by virtue of its distinction from other creedal books by beginning with the most concise of the abridgement which you find in the brief of its texts, to the abridgement which you see in the large texts and the brief of commentaries, to the intermediate, then to the capacity of the extensive. This wonderful arrangement which our Imam al-Sanusi followed was not an innovation; for it had been preceded by the leading figures of kalam, such as the Proof of Islam al-Ghazali in his Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id, al-Risalah al-Qudsiyyah, and al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad; and the verifying scholar Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani had something similar in his Tahdhib al-Mantiq wa al-Kalam, al-Maqasid, and Sharh al-Maqasid, both from the far Islamic East. But the wonderful thing in the series of Imam al-Sanusi is his uncovering and inspired discovery that this blessed series would occupy the domains of consideration and teaching in the science of creeds in the near future. Rather, his authorship of these works was embraced by the eyes and pens of scholars during his lifetime before his death. All of this was a reason for him to discern the faces of future students, taking into account the weakness of the weak and the dullness of his mind, the inattention of the mediocre in perplexity and the suspicion of the intelligent in his acuteness and cleverness, and the satisfaction of the successful one with his glance and gesture. All of this is some of what Imam al-Sanusi realized, to carry out his determination to establish a kalam school with its own methodology and style, whose boundaries begin with the children of the elementary school and the common people of trades and crafts who have no occupation with knowledge, rather with the women whose concern is their houses and fulfilling their needs, to reach the verifying scholar.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is an editorial introduction that presents two main elements: first, historical praise for Imam al-Sanusi's creedal works, and second, a detailed description of the graded pedagogical structure of his series, the 'Sanusiyyat'. The passage begins by quoting two historians. Al-Shafshawani declares that al-Sanusi's five creedal texts (al-Muqaddimah, al-Sughra, Sughra al-Sughra, al-Wusta, al-Kubra) along with their commentaries are among the best works composed in Islam. Ibn al-Qadi al-Miknasi adds that no later scholar has produced creeds equal to al-Sanusi's. This establishes the high regard for al-Sanusi's works. The passage then explains why the Sanusiyyat series became so influential: despite the many other creedal works, the Sanusiyyat entered the systematic curriculum of kalam with a firm presence because of its distinctive method. That method is a graded progression: it begins with the most concise abridgment (al-mukhtasar), then a slightly larger abridgment, then an intermediate work, and finally an extensive commentary. This 'wonderful arrangement' (al-tartib al-badi') was not unprecedented; al-Ghazali and al-Taftazani had similarly structured series. However, the unique feature of al-Sanusi's series is its prophetic insight: he foresaw that this series would become central to teaching future generations. He designed it to cater to all levels of students, from the weak and dull-witted to the intelligent and sharp, and even to women and common people. His goal was to establish a 'kalam school' with a clear methodology, starting from the most basic level (children and artisans) up to the level of the verifying scholar. Thus, the passage emphasizes the pedagogical intentionality behind the Sanusiyyat.

مدرسة كلامية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Sanusi's series is just a random collection of books with no specific order.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that the series is intentionally arranged in a graded pedagogical order from concise to extensive, designed to meet the needs of diverse learners.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the unique contribution of Imam al-Sanusi's graded series compared to similar works by al-Ghazali and al-Taftazani?
A) He wrote exactly five books.
B) He was the first to write graded creedal works.
C) He had an inspired insight that his series would become central to future teaching.
D) He included women in his audience.

Key Takeaway
The Sanusiyyat series was deliberately designed as a graded pedagogical ladder to make kalam accessible to all, from the simplest minds to the most advanced scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi tailored his series to meet every learner where they were, from the weakest to the brightest. When you share knowledge, do you consider the capacity of your listener, or do you speak only for those at your own level? How will you adjust your conversation today to truly benefit someone who struggles?
?
Sanusi discerned the faces of future students and anticipated their needs. What are you doing right now to ensure that your learning will serve others years from now? Will your efforts bear fruit for those who come after you, or are you only storing knowledge for yourself?
?
The scholars of Sanusi's time embraced his series while he was still alive. If your work were to be evaluated by qualified scholars today, would they find it worthy of teaching? What would need to change for your contribution to be truly valuable to the Ummah?

Sanusi's Graded Ladder of Kalam Works
Sanusi describes his graded series of works from beginner to advanced.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Sanusi designed his series of kalam works as a graded ladder to guide students from the simplest to the most advanced level, and why this approach was both innovative and rooted in tradition.

Core Idea
Sanusi's graded ladder lifts students from blind imitation to lofty verification.

Imam Sanusi didn't just write one book on creed; he created a whole series, carefully sequenced to match the learner's journey. This passage explains that ladder and the pedagogical genius behind it. As you read, notice how he balances innovation with respect for earlier scholars, and how he aims to include everyone, from the beginner to the advanced researcher.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then the English translation. Let the author's description of the progression sink in before analyzing.
Pay attention to the terms: مختصر, وسيط, بسيط.
2
Identify the ladder's rungs
The passage lists a progression: from 'abridgement of the abridgement' to 'abridgement' to 'intermediate' to 'extensive'. Notice that these correspond to different books in Sanusi's corpus.
This ladder is designed to match the student's growing capacity.
3
Recognize the predecessors
Sanusi acknowledges that al-Ghazali and al-Taftazani had similar graded series. This shows that the idea was not new, but Sanusi's implementation was distinctive.
4
Understand the target audience and goal
The passage describes that Sanusi's series caters to all levels: from children and common folk to advanced scholars. The ultimate goal is to move from blind imitation (تقليد أعمى) to lofty verification (تحقيق أسمى).
This inclusive approach was a key feature of his madrasa.

الاعتقاد بالابتداء بمعتصر المختصر الذي تجده في وجيز متونها، إلى المختصر الذي تراه في كبير المتون ووجيز الشروح، إلى الوسيط ثم إلى سعة البسيط. هذا الترتيب البديع الذي انتهجه إمامنا السنوسي لم يكن بدعاً؛ فقد سبق إليه من قبل أعلام علم الكلام، فلحجة الإسلام الغزالي مثله؛ في «قواعد العقائد» و«الرسالة القدسية» و«الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد»، وللعلامة المحقق سعد الدين التفتازاني شِبْهُهُ؛ في «تهذيب المنطق والكلام» و«المقاصد» و«شرح المقاصد»، وكلاهما من أقصى المشرق الإسلامي، ولكن البديع في سلسلة الإمام السنوسي هو استحياؤه [؟] وكشفه الإلهامي عن تربع هذه السلسلة المباركة مناحي النظر والتدريس في علم العقائد في المستقبل القريب، بل إن تأليفه هذه عانقتها أنظار العلماء وأقلامهم في حياته قبل موته، كل هذا كان مدعاة له أن يتفرّس وجوه طلبة المستقبل مُلِمّاً بضعف الضعيف وبلادة ذهنه، وشرود النصف النصيف في وكدورة [؟] وهم الذكي على حدته ونباهته، واكتفاء الموفق عقله بلمحته وإشارته. كل هذا هو بعض ما أدركه الإمام السنوسي لتنفذ همته في إنشاء مدرسة كلامية لها منهاجها وأسلوبها، تبدأ حدودها بصبيان الكتاب، وعامة أهل الصنائع والحِرَف الذين لا شغل لهم بالعلم، بل بالنسوة اللاتي همهنَّ بيوتهنَّ وقضاء حوائجهنَّ، لتصل إلى العالم المحقق المدقق الحريص على نفائس العلم وحُلله، والباحث الغواص في لججه لاستخراج درره؛ فمن صغرى صغرى الصغرى» إلى «شرح العقيدة الكبرى»، ومن تقليد أعمى إلى تحقيق أسمى، فسبحان الفتاح على ما فتح! وله الحمد تعالى على ما أعطى ومنح.
Belief, beginning with the abridgement of the abridgement which you find in the brief of its texts, to the abridgement which you see in the large text and the brief of commentaries, to the intermediate then to the expanse of the extensive. This wonderful arrangement that our Imam Sanusi adopted was not an innovation; for the leading scholars of kalam had preceded him in it. For the Proof of Islam al-Ghazali had something like it in 'The Foundations of Beliefs', 'The Jerusalem Epistle', and 'Moderation in Belief'; and for the investigating scholar Sad al-Din al-Taftazani something similar in 'The Refinement of Logic and Kalam', 'The Aims', and 'The Commentary on the Aims', both from the far East of the Islamic world. But the wonderful aspect in the series of Imam Sanusi is his shyness [؟] and inspired uncovering of the sitting of this blessed series in the realms of consideration and teaching in the science of beliefs in the near future. Rather, his writing of these works was embraced by the looks of scholars and their pens during his life before his death. All of this was a reason for him to discern the faces of future students, taking into account the weakness of the weak and the dullness of his mind, and the distraction of the half-intelligent in fogginess [؟] and the confusion of the intelligent on his sharpness and acumen, and the sufficiency of the successful mind with its glance and signal. All of this is some of what Imam Sanusi perceived so that his resolve could penetrate in establishing a kalam school with its curriculum and style, whose boundaries begin with the children of the book [primary school] and the common people of crafts and trades who have no occupation with knowledge, rather with the women whose concern is their houses and fulfilling their needs, to reach the verifying, scrutinizing scholar who is eager for the precious gems of knowledge and its ornaments, and the diver researcher into its depths to extract its pearls. So from 'The Smallest of the Small' to 'The Commentary on the Great Creed', and from blind imitation to loftiest verification. So glory be to the Opener for what He opened! And praise be to Him, exalted, for what He gave and granted.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage describes the graded structure of Imam Sanusi's series of kalam works, which he designed as a ladder from the simplest to the most advanced. The author begins by outlining the progression: starting with the 'abridgement of the abridgement' (مختصر المختصر) found in the brief texts, moving to the abridgement in larger texts and brief commentaries, then to the intermediate (وسيط), and finally to the extensive (بسيط). He notes that this ordered approach was not new; earlier scholars like al-Ghazali and al-Taftazani had similar series. However, Sanusi's innovation lies in his inspired insight ('الكشف الإلهامي') that his series would occupy a central place in future teaching and scholarship. The author then explains that Sanusi tailored his works to suit students of all levels, from weak beginners to bright but distracted students, and even to women with domestic responsibilities, all the way up to the most advanced researcher. The ultimate goal of this ladder is to elevate students from blind imitation (تقليد أعمى) to lofty verification (تحقيق أسمى). The passage concludes with praise to God for enabling this achievement.

?
Sanusi discerned the different abilities of students. Do you assume your current level is fixed, or do you trust that the ladder can lift you? What is holding you back from climbing—fear, laziness, or pride?

Recognition and Impact of Sanusi's Works
Sanusi's mastery is praised and the widespread influence of his works is described.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi's mastery in kalam was recognized, his works became influential, and the distinctive methodology of superimposed authorship in his Sanusiyyat.

Core Idea
Sanusi's works were so refined that scholars praised his mastery and extensively commented on them.

This passage highlights the remarkable recognition Imam Sanusi received during his lifetime and the enduring impact of his works, especially the Sanusiyyat. It also introduces a unique feature of his authorship: the superimposed structure where each text builds upon previous ones with new insights. As you read, notice the vivid praise and the tangible evidence of influence found in manuscript catalogues.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once without stopping. Pay attention to the flow of the narrative: from the proverbial praise to the joy of seeing his works studied, to the impact in manuscript catalogues, and finally the methodology.
The word 'غربل' (sifted) is key to understanding the nature of Sanusi's contribution.
2
Identify the evidence of impact
The passage lists several indicators of Sanusi's success: the praise from a shaykh, the joy of seeing his works studied, the extraction of 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi' by his teachers, and the extensive commentaries on 'al-Sughra'. List them in your mind.
3
Understand the methodology of superimposed authorship
The passage ends by describing Sanusi's methodology as 'التأليف المتراكب' (superimposed authorship). Reflect on what this means: each text builds on previous ones, offering new useful material, while varying in size for different levels.
This methodology shows Sanusi's pedagogical genius.
4
Answer the checkpoint question
Use the passage to answer the MCQ about what Shaykh Abu Imran said. This reinforces the core praise of Sanusi.

ومكنة الإمام في علم الكلام صارت مضرب مثل، حتى قال الشيخ أبو عمران موسى بن عقدة الأغضاوي إذا ذُكر علم الكلام: (ما رأيتُ مَنْ غربلَ هذا العلم مثل هذا الرجل) يعني: السنوسي. وقبل رحيل إمامنا السنوسي عن هذه الدنيا الفانية... قرت عينه حينما رأى مؤلفاته - وعلى رأسها «سنوسياته» - قد أقبل عليها طلبة العلم، بل إن بعض شيوخه نظر فيها واستخرج ما سُمِّي بكتاب «حقائق السنوسي». أما الناظر في كتب فهارس المخطوطات فسيرى الأثر الكبير الذي أحدثته «السنوسيات»؛ إذ أعملت العلماء أقلامها في شرح متونها والتحشية على شروحها، ولا سيما «الصغرى» و«شرحها»، فقد كانت لهذه العقيدة تحديداً عناية فائقة ندر أن ترى مثلها لكتاب في رحاب المكتبة الإسلامية. والبديع في منهج السنوسي في «سنوسياته» المباركة هو التأليف المتراكب الذي لا بد فيه من جديد مفيد، فالمتون على تفاوت حجمها.
And the position of the Imam in the science of kalam became a proverb, until the Shaykh Abu Imran Musa ibn Uqda al-Aghdawi said when kalam was mentioned: 'I have not seen anyone who sifted this science like this man'—meaning al-Sanusi. And before the departure of our Imam al-Sanusi from this transient world... his eye was comforted when he saw his works—especially his 'Sanusiyyat'—having been eagerly received by students of knowledge; indeed, some of his teachers examined them and extracted what was called the book 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi' (The Truths of al-Sanusi). As for the one who looks in the catalogues of manuscripts, he will see the great impact that the 'Sanusiyyat' produced; for scholars set their pens to work in commenting on its texts and writing glosses on its commentaries, especially 'al-Sughra' and its commentary. This creed in particular received extraordinary attention rarely seen for a book in the realm of the Islamic library. And the wonderful aspect in the methodology of al-Sanusi in his blessed 'Sanusiyyat' is the superimposed authorship which must contain new useful material, for the texts vary in size.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage highlights the recognition and impact of Imam Sanusi's works on kalam. It begins with a statement that Sanusi's mastery became proverbial, quoting Shaykh Abu Imran Musa ibn Uqda al-Aghdawi who praised him as the one who 'sifted' (غربل) the science of kalam like no other, meaning he refined and clarified it. The author then recounts that before Sanusi's death, he had the joy of seeing his works, especially the 'Sanusiyyat', widely studied by students, and even some of his teachers produced a work based on them called 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi'. The passage further notes the enormous impact seen in manuscript catalogues: scholars wrote extensive commentaries and glosses, particularly on 'al-Sughra' (the Small Creed) and its commentary, which received extraordinary attention. Finally, the passage points out another wonderful aspect of Sanusi's methodology: his 'superimposed authorship' (التأليف المتراكب), meaning his works build upon each other with new useful material, and the texts vary in size to suit different levels.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'غربل هذا العلم' means Sanusi invented the science of kalam.
Correction: The metaphor 'غربل' means to sift, refine, and clarify, not to invent. Sanusi's contribution was in organizing, clarifying, and presenting kalam with unprecedented precision.

Key Takeaway
The widespread recognition and lasting impact of Sanusi's works are evident in the praise of scholars, the extensive commentaries on his texts, and his innovative methodology of superimposed authorship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Sanusi's teachers studied his works and extracted 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi'. Do you have the humility to learn from those who were once your students? Or does your ego prevent you from acknowledging the growth of others?
?
The passage describes scholars 'setting their pens to work' on Sanusi's texts for generations. Will the knowledge you acquire today be worthy of such dedication? Are you studying to be a mere consumer or a contributor to the Islamic scholarly tradition?

Sanusi's Layered Composition Method and Its Impact
Describes the impact of Sanusi's works and his unique layered composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's unique method of layered authorship and how it contributed to the enduring scholarly impact of his works.

Core Idea
Sanusi's layered authorship ensures each work adds unique value despite apparent repetition.

This passage from a biographical work on Imam Sanusi highlights the remarkable scholarly reception of his 'Sanusiyyat' series, especially the commentaries. It then explains the innovative compositional method that made these works indispensable for generations. As you read, notice how Sanusi's layered approach allowed each text to serve a distinct purpose while building on shared foundations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow, then read the English translation. Notice how the author first describes the impact, then explains the method.
The passage uses the term 'التأليف المتراكب' (layered composition) as a key concept.
2
Identify the three aspects of novelty
The passage lists three specific ways each new work brings fresh benefit: presentation style, proof/example variation, and unique additions. Find and underline these three items.
These are listed after the phrase 'جديد في أسلوب العرض، وتنويع في الدليل والمثال، وزيادة تنفرد بها'.
3
Reflect on the scholarly practice
Consider why scholars often cite multiple commentaries (al-Kubra, al-Wusta, al-Sughra) rather than relying on one. What does this tell us about the necessity of each work?
The passage states that scholars cannot suffice with just one commentary.

أما الناظر في كتب فهارس المخطوطات فسيرى الأثر الكبير الذي أحدثته السنوسيات ؛ إذ أعملت العلماء أقلامها في شرح متونها والتحشية على شروحها ، ولا سيما الصغرى و شرحها ، فقد كانت لهذه العقيدة تحديداً عناية فائقة ندر أن ترى مثلها لكتاب في رحاب المكتبة الإسلامية . والبديع في منهج السنوسي في سنوسياته المباركة هو التأليف المتراكب الذي لا بد فيه من جديد مفيد ، فالمتون على تفاوت حجمها ترى فيها تكراراً في معالمها العامة ، ولكن مع جديد في أسلوب العرض ، وتنويع في الدليل والمثال ، وزيادة تنفرد بها كل سنوسية لا توجد في غيرها ، وكم ترى للعلماء نقولاً وتحريرات عن إمامنا السنوسي يختمونها بقولهم : كما في شرح العقيدة الكبرى ، أو شرح العقيدة الوسطى ، أو شرح العقيدة الصغرى ولا يمكنهم الاكتفاء بـ شرح العقيدة الكبرى مثلاً عن غيرها.
As for the one who looks into manuscript catalogues, he will see the great impact made by the Sanusiyyat; for scholars have employed their pens in commenting on their texts and writing glosses on their commentaries, especially al-Sughra and its commentary. Indeed, this creed received extraordinary attention rarely seen for a book in the Islamic library. And the novel aspect of Sanusi's method in his blessed Sanusiyyat is the layered composition (al-ta'lif al-mutarakib) which necessarily contains new benefit. For the texts, despite varying in size, you see repetition in their general features, but with novelty in the method of presentation, variation in the proof and example, and an addition unique to each Sanusiyya that is not found in others. And how often you see scholars quoting and writing glosses about our Imam Sanusi, ending them by saying: 'as in the commentary on the Greatest Creed,' or 'commentary on the Middle Creed,' or 'commentary on the Smallest Creed,' and they cannot suffice with the commentary on the Greatest Creed alone to the exclusion of the others.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage discusses the remarkable impact of Sanusi's works, especially the 'Sanusiyyat' series, on the Islamic scholarly tradition. It begins by noting that manuscript catalogues reveal how scholars extensively wrote commentaries and glosses on these texts, particularly the smallest creed (al-Sughra) and its commentary, which received extraordinary attention unparalleled for other books. Then the author explains the innovative method of Sanusi: 'layered composition' (al-ta'lif al-mutarakib). This means that although his works cover the same general topics and thus show repetition, each work introduces new benefit in three ways: a novel presentation style, variation in proofs and examples, and unique additional content not found in the other works. The result is that scholars often refer to multiple commentaries (the Greatest, Middle, and Smallest) in their own citations, demonstrating that each work is indispensable and cannot be replaced by another. This highlights the pedagogical wisdom of Sanusi's approach: each layer serves a distinct purpose for different levels of readers.

Correction: The passage explains that while general features repeat, each work introduces new benefit in presentation, proofs, and unique additions.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's layered composition method ensures that each of his creedal works adds indispensable value, making them collectively essential for scholarship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Sanusi's method required him to create with each work something of unique value, not mere repetition. In your own learning or teaching, are you adding new benefit or just going through the motions?

Sanusi's Method: Inclusive Audience and Sufi Elements
Describes Sanusi's inclusive audience strategy and spiritual elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi tailored his theological writings to diverse audiences and incorporated Sufi elements for spiritual enrichment.

Core Idea
Sanusi made theology accessible to everyone by blending clear language with Sufi wisdom and heartfelt prayers.

Sanusi's theological works are known for their depth, but this passage reveals his remarkable attention to his readers. He didn't write only for scholars; he wrote for the common person, the child, and the specialist alike. Moreover, he infused his texts with Sufi references, prayers, and poetry to touch the heart as well as the mind. Let's explore how he achieved this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's description of Sanusi's method come through.
2
Identify the two main features
The passage highlights two key aspects of Sanusi's approach: addressing diverse audiences and including Sufi elements. Find evidence for each.
The first part discusses audience diversity; the second discusses Sufi references, prayers, and poetry.
3
Reflect on the pedagogical wisdom
Ask yourself why Sanusi adopted this method. How does it make theology more effective and accessible?
Consider the role of emotional and spiritual engagement alongside intellectual clarity.

And every observer knows the diversity of discourse in which diversity of the addressee was taken into account. For Sanusi had a good intention in rescuing the blind followers from all segments; so you see him addressing the commoner and the specialist, the young and the old, the unlettered and the child, in a language keen on understanding and clarification, without preoccupation with embellishing all that. Commentary
This passage describes Sanusi's deliberate consideration of his audience and his inclusion of spiritual and literary elements. It begins by stating that Sanusi took into account the diversity of his readership, aiming to rescue blind followers (muqallidin) from all social segments. He addressed the common layman and the specialist, the young and the old, the illiterate and the child, using clear and explanatory language without excessive embellishment. As a result, his Sanusiyyat are described as sweet in words and elegant in expression. Then the passage highlights another remarkable feature: the intentional blending of gnostic references (isharat ma'rifiyya) in the manner of the Sufi masters, tender prayers at the end of chapters to provide comfort and remind readers of higher purposes, and the inclusion of poetry, wise sayings, and proverbs that soften the dryness of the theological text. The author emphasizes the positive effect of this approach in bringing tranquility to the soul. The passage concludes by noting that Sanusi hinted that this method is faithful to presenting the creed effectively. The commentary underscores the pedagogical strategy of making the text accessible and spiritually uplifting for all levels.

Al-'Attar's Warning: Over-Reliance on the Sanusi Works
The editor quotes al-‘Attar cautioning against making Sanusi's works the sole authority in kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dangers of treating any single scholarly work as the sole authority in kalam, and learn to balance respect for authoritative texts with critical engagement.

Core Idea
A great scholar's works can become an unintended cage if made the only measure of truth.

This passage presents a caution from Shaykh Hasan al-‘Attar, a leading Azhar scholar, about how the Sanusi works—especially the popular al-Sughra—had come to be treated as the exclusive reference in kalam. Al-‘Attar's point is not to disparage these texts but to warn against a narrow, dogmatic approach that rejects any verification found elsewhere. This lesson helps you understand the balance between honoring scholarly tradition and maintaining critical inquiry in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read al-‘Attar's original statement
Begin by reading the quoted statement from Shaykh al-‘Attar in the source passage. Notice his frustration with teachers who refuse to consider any proof outside the Sanusi works.
Al-‘Attar uses strong language: 'if one of them were brought a luminous transmission... he would not deviate.'
2
Identify the core warning
The warning is not against the Sanusi works themselves but against making them the sole authority. Identify the phrase where al-‘Attar clarifies this.
He says: 'لا من باب الاعتراض على ما حوته من علم جم، بل أن يُخطَّاً ما سواها لمجرد مخالفتها'
3
Reflect on the modern extension
The editor then extends al-‘Attar's warning to contemporary issues: quick study methods, deficient abridgements, and partisanship. Consider how this applies today.
4
Understand the misconception
The lesson addresses the common misunderstanding that al-‘Attar was criticizing the Sanusi works. Recognize that he was highlighting a methodological error, not a doctrinal one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عقيدة السنوسي ، يدرسونها في الألواح يجهرون بقراءتها وقد بلغ شأن « السنوسيات » عموماً و « شرح الصغرى » خصوصاً شأواً جعل بعض محققي العلماء يتضجر من ذلك ، لا من باب الاعتراض على ما حوته من علم جم ، بل أن يُخطَّاً ما سواها لمجرد مخالفتها ، حتى قال العلامة الشيخ حسن العطار في حاشيته على شرح المحلي لجمع الجوامع : ( ليت هذا القائل عاش حتى الآن ؛ ليرى ما يقوله المدرسون في دروسهم ، بل ما ينقله المؤلفون في عصرنا ممن يتعلق بعلم الكلام ؛ فإنهم اتخذوا الصغرى ) وما كتب عليها من الحواشي والشروح عمدة وإماماً ، ولم تطمح نفوسهم بما قرره محققو هذا الفن في كتبهم ، حتى إنه لو أتي لواحد منهم بنقل ساطع أو برهان قاطع .. لم يعدل عما استقر في ذهنه مما يخالف الصواب ، وقال : لا أعدل عمَّا رأيته في ذلك الكتاب) . وهذه الكلمة من شيخ الأزهر العلامة العطار يقدر قدرها العالم والموفق ، فحاشا أن يفهم منه الطعن في السنوسيات ، بل هو تنبيه على ألا تجعل - لا سيما الصغرى و شروحها - كلَّ شيء في هذا العلم المترامي الأطراف ، فقد ترى تحقيقاً في مسألة أو أكثر لا تجده أو تجد خلافه فيها ولو أن العلامة العطار عاش حتى الآن ؛ ورأى حال طلبة علم العقائد والكلام ، وما هم عليه من اهتمام بالوجبات العلمية السريعة ، والاختصارات المحدثة المخلَّة وغير المحققة ، والتأليفات الهشة التي داهن فيها مؤلفوها أهل البدع وجانبوا المداراة ، وأخرى تعصّب فيها أصحابها فضيقوا واسعاً ، وجعلوا الجنةَ وَقْفاً على أهل السنة دون غيرهم ، ورأى إلى ذلك أحوال مدرّسي هذا العلم العظيم ، والذين عامتهم تلقلقُ ألسنتهم بمصطلحات هذا العلم دون دراية ، وبعضُهم قد أضمر الخصومة له وهو يدرسه محتسباً بزعمه ليصحح الأخطاء ويردَّ البدع التي انتشرت فيه ! ولا يعلم أنه قد ضلَّ بفعله وأضل . . لرجا المولى تعالى أن تعود تلك العصبيَّة لهذه المدرسة العريقة ، وأن يؤول الأمر إلى ما كان عليه ، والشكوى إلى المولى العظيم
They study the creed of al-Sanusi in tablets, reciting it aloud. And the standing of the 'Sanusiyyat' in general and the 'Sharh al-Sughra' in particular reached a level that caused some verifying scholars to be annoyed by that, not from the perspective of objecting to the abundant knowledge they contain, but that other works are deemed mistaken merely for opposing them, until the eminent scholar Shaykh Hasan al-‘Attar said in his gloss on al-Mahalli's commentary on Jam‘ al-Jawami‘: 'If only this speaker had lived until now, to see what teachers say in their lessons, and what authors in our time who are connected to the science of kalam transmit! For they have taken al-Sughra and the glosses and commentaries written upon it as their mainstay and imam, and their souls do not aspire to what the verifiers of this art have established in their books, to the extent that if one of them were brought a luminous transmission or a decisive proof, he would not deviate from what has settled in his mind that is contrary to the truth, and he would say: I will not deviate from what I have seen in that book.' This statement from the Shaykh of al-Azhar, the eminent scholar al-‘Attar, is appreciated at its true value by the knowledgeable and the successful. Far be it from him that he should be understood as impugning the 'Sanusiyyat'; rather, it is a warning not to make—especially al-Sughra and its commentaries—everything in this far-reaching science. For you may find a verification in one or more issues that you do not find, or find its contrary, in them. And if the eminent scholar al-‘Attar had lived until now, and seen the state of students of the science of doctrines and kalam, and their concern with quick academic snacks and the deficient modern abridgements that are unverified, and the flimsy compositions in which their authors flattered the people of innovation and avoided prudence, and others in which their partisans became fanatical, narrowing what was broad, and made Paradise exclusive to Ahl al-Sunnah to the exclusion of others, and seen in addition to that the conditions of the teachers of this great science, most of whose tongues rattle with the terms of this science without insight, and some of whom have hidden hostility towards it while studying it, thinking they are earning reward by correcting errors and repelling innovations that have spread in it, not knowing that they have gone astray and led others astray... he would have besought the Most High Lord that that fanaticism for this noble school should return, and that the matter should revert to what it was. And the complaint is to the Most High.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by the editor on the widespread practice of making the Sanusi works—especially al-Sughra and its commentaries—the exclusive reference in kalam. It begins by noting that students recite the Sanusi creed aloud from tablets. The editor then remarks that the prominence of these works has annoyed some scholars, not because of their content, but because other works are dismissed simply for contradicting them. To illustrate, he quotes Shaykh Hasan al-‘Attar, who lamented that contemporary teachers and authors have made al-Sughra and its glosses their sole authority, to the point that they reject any other evidence, no matter how strong. Al-‘Attar clarifies that this is not a criticism of the Sanusi works themselves, but a warning against treating them as the entirety of the science, since important verifications may be found elsewhere. The editor then amplifies this concern, imagining how al-‘Attar would react if he saw the current state: students seeking quick, shallow knowledge, relying on deficient abridgements, and partisanship that either flatters innovators or narrows truth. Teachers often use kalam terminology without understanding it, and some even hide hostility while claiming to correct errors, inadvertently leading others astray. The editor concludes by wishing for a return to the balanced methodology of the original school.

Correction: Al-‘Attar explicitly says he is not objecting to the knowledge they contain, but rather warning against making them the sole criterion for truth, to the exclusion of other verified scholarly works.

Al-Ghazali's Three Qualities for a Student of Tawhid
Al-Ghazali enumerates three necessary qualities in a student of tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three essential qualities that Al-Ghazali requires in a student of the science of tawhid and the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Studying tawhid requires devotion, intelligence, and piety — not just anyone may learn it.

Al-Ghazali, in his book Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id, sets forth three prerequisites for anyone who wishes to study the science of tawhid. These qualities ensure that the student can properly understand, engage with, and remain steadfast upon the knowledge. The passage emphasizes that kalam is not for everyone; it requires specific preparation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Ghazali uses a three-part structure introduced by 'ثلاث خصال'.
2
Identify each quality
Find each of the three qualities: devotion, intelligence/eloquence, piety/righteousness. Note how each is defined and the reasoning given.
The first is عن التجرد للعلم والحرص عليه, the second is الذكاء والفطنة والفصاحة, the third is الصلاح والديانة والتقوى.
3
Understand the reasoning behind each
For each quality, consider why Al-Ghazali considers it necessary. The commentary below will help explain the dangers if these qualities are lacking.
For example, the dullard may be harmed by kalam, and the immoral person may abandon religion at the slightest doubt.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: Why would Al-Ghazali restrict the teaching of tawhid to such students? How does this reflect the seriousness of the science?
This connects to the broader methodology of ilm al-kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإليك هذه الذكرى في صفات القاصد لتعلم علم التوحيد والعقائد، وهي صفات ذكرها حجة الإسلام إمامنا الغزالي في كتابه قواعد العقائد من كتب إحياء علوم الدين حيث قال: العالم به ينبغي أن يخص بتعليم هذا العلم مَنْ فيه ثلاث خصال: إحداها: التجرد للعلم والحرص عليه؛ فإنَّ المحترف يمنعه الشغل عن الاستتمام وإزالة الشكوك إذا عرضت. والثانية: الذكاء والفطنة والفصاحة؛ فإنَّ البليد لا ينتفع بفهمه، والفدم لا ينتفع بحجاجه، فيخاف عليه من ضرر الكلام، ولا يرجى فيه نفعه. والثالثة: أن يكون في طبعه الصلاح والديانة والتقوى، ولا تكون الشهوات غالبة عليه؛ فإنَّ الفاسق بأدنى شبهة ينخلع عن الدين؛ فإن ذلك يحلُّ عنه السد الحجر ويرفع الملاذ. وهذه الثالثة يدخل فيها الفاسق بالاعتقاد كما لا يخفى.
And here is this reminder concerning the qualities of the one who intends to learn the science of tawhid and doctrines—qualities mentioned by the Proof of Islam, our Imam al-Ghazali, in his book Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id from the books of Ihya' 'Ulum al-Din, where he said: 'The scholar of it should single out for teaching this science whoever has three characteristics: The first: devotion to learning and eagerness for it; for the professional is prevented by occupation from completing it and removing doubts when they arise. The second: intelligence, acumen, and eloquence; for the dullard does not benefit from understanding it, and the boor does not benefit from argumentation against it, so it is feared for him from the harm of kalam, and no benefit is hoped for in him. The third: that there be in his nature righteousness, religiosity, and piety, and that desires not be dominant over him; for the immoral person is stripped of religion at the slightest doubt; for that removes from him the barrier and raises the pleasures.' And this third category includes the one who is immoral by belief, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from al-Ghazali's 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id' listing three prerequisites for studying the science of tawhid. It is introduced by the editor as a reminder. Al-Ghazali states that a teacher should only teach this science to someone who possesses three characteristics. First: complete devotion to learning and eagerness, because a person occupied with a profession cannot fully pursue the completion of knowledge nor resolve doubts that arise. Second: intelligence, acumen, and eloquence; a dull person (al-balīd) cannot understand, and a boorish person (al-fadm) cannot engage in argumentation, so instead of benefiting, kalam may harm him. Third: righteousness, religiosity, and piety, with desires not dominating him; a wicked person (al-fāsiq) will abandon his religion at the slightest doubt because he lacks the barrier of piety. The editor adds that this third category includes those who are immoral in belief (al-fāsiq bi-l-i‘tiqād) as well.

Key Terms
التجرد للعلم
الذكاء والفطنة والفصاحة
الصلاح والديانة والتقوى
الفاسق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any person can study kalam regardless of their character or intellect.
Correction: Al-Ghazali insists that only those with devotion, intelligence, and piety should be taught tawhid, as those lacking these qualities may be harmed by the study.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, why is it dangerous for a dullard (البليد) to study kalam?
A) He will not understand the arguments.
B) He will not benefit from understanding it, and it is feared that kalam may harm him.
C) He will become a professional debater.
D) He will lose his faith.

Key Takeaway
Studying tawhid requires a student to be devoted, intelligent, and pious — these qualities protect and benefit the learner.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
As you read this lesson, which of the three qualities are you most lacking? What specific step will you take this week to cultivate it before or while studying tawhid?
?
Al-Ghazali warns that the immoral person abandons religion at the slightest doubt because the barrier of piety is removed. Is there a doubt or desire lurking in your heart that you have not confronted because you know it could shake your faith? Will you continue to avoid it, or will you strengthen the barrier of taqwā today?
?
You claim to seek knowledge of tawhid—but are you truly 'devoted to learning and eager for it,' or is your study of this science competing with worldly concerns? Where does the pursuit of truth sit among your daily priorities?

Ghazali's Three Conditions for Studying Kalam
Ghazali enumerates three qualities for the student of kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ghazali's three prerequisites for studying kalam and the rationale behind each.

Core Idea
Devotion, intelligence, and piety guard the student of kalam from harm.

Al-Ghazali, the great renewer, laid down essential conditions for those who seek to learn the science of tawhid. His criteria ensure that kalam strengthens faith rather than undermining it. This passage from Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id presents these three qualities as a safeguard.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the three conditions.
Notice the repetition of 'فإنّ' for each rationale.
2
Identify each quality and its purpose
For each of the three, note what al-Ghazali says about why it is necessary.
3
Reflect on the third quality's scope
The editor adds that the third includes the immoral in belief. Think about what this means for who can benefit from kalam.
4
Consider the misconception
Understand that these conditions are for the student, not the teacher, and that the third is about general disposition, not perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقائد والكلام ، وما هم عليه من اهتمام بالوجبات العلمية السريعة ، والاختصارات المحدثة المخلَّة وغير المحققة المحققة ، والتأليفات الهشة التي داهن فيها مؤلفوها أهل البدع وجانبوا المداراة ، وأخرى تعصّب فيها أصحابها فضيقوا واسعاً ، وجعلوا الجنةَ وَقْفاً على أهل السنة دون غيرهم ، ورأى إلى ذلك أحوال مدرّسي هذا العلم العظيم ، والذين عامتهم تلقلقُ ألسنتهم بمصطلحات هذا العلم دون دراية ، وبعضُهم قد أضمر الخصومة له وهو يدرسه محتسباً بزعمه ليصحح الأخطاء ويردَّ (۱) البدع التي انتشرت فيه ! ولا يعلم أنه قد ضلَّ بفعله وأضل (١) . . لرجا المولى تعالى أن تعود تلك العصبيَّة لهذه المدرسة العريقة ، وأن يؤول الأمر إلى ما كان عليه ، والشكوى إلى المولى العظيم . (۱) وإليك هذه الذكرى في صفات القاصد لتعلم علم التوحيد والعقائد ، وهي صفات ذكرها حجة الإسلام إمامنا الغزالي في كتابه ) قواعد العقائد ) من كتب إحياء علوم الدين ) ( ١ / ٣٦٠ ) حيث قال : ( العالم به ينبغي أن يخص بتعليم هذا العلم مَنْ فيه ثلاث خصال : إحداها : التجرد للعلم والحرص عليه ؛ فإنَّ المحترف يمنعه الشغل عن الاستتمام وإزالة الشكوك إذا عرضت . والثانية : الذكاء والفطنة والفصاحة ؛ فإنَّ البليد لا ينتفع بفهمه ، والفدم لا ينتفع بحجاجه ، فيخاف عليه من ضرر الكلام ، ولا يرجى فيه نفعه . والثالثة : أن يكون في طبعه الصلاح والديانة والتقوى ، ولا تكون الشهوات غالبة عليه ؛ فإنَّ الفاسق بأدنى شبهة ينخلع عن الدين ؛ فإن ذلك يحلُّ عنه السد الحجر ويرفع الملاذ )، وهذه الثالثة يدخل فيها الفاسق بالاعتقاد كما لا يخفى .
The creeds and kalam, and their concern with fast scientific obligations, and the modern deficient abbreviations that are unverified, and the flimsy compositions whose authors flattered the people of innovation and avoided gentleness, and others whose owners became fanatical so they restricted what was broad, and made Paradise a waqf exclusively for Ahl al-Sunna to the exclusion of others. He saw also the conditions of the teachers of this great science, whose tongues mostly rattle with the terminology of this science without knowledge, and some of them have harbored hostility to it while studying it, self-righteously claiming to correct errors and refute innovations that have spread in it! He does not know that he has gone astray and led others astray... May Allah Almighty return that zeal to this venerable school, and may matters return to what they were, and complaint is to the Almighty. (1) And these is a reminder regarding the qualities of the seeker to learn the science of tawhid and creeds, qualities mentioned by Hujjat al-Islam our Imam al-Ghazali in his book Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id from the books of Ihya' 'Ulum al-Din (1/360), where he said: 'The one who knows it should specify for teaching this science those who possess three characteristics: First: Devotion to learning and eagerness for it; for the professional is prevented by occupation from completing it and removing doubts that arise. Second: Intelligence, perspicacity, and eloquence; for the dull-witted does not benefit from understanding it, and the stupid does not benefit from argumentation, so it is feared for him from the harm of kalam, and its benefit is not hoped for him. Third: That his nature be characterized by righteousness, religiosity, and piety, and that desires not be predominant over him; for the immoral person falls away from religion at the slightest doubt; for that removes the barrier and lifts the pleasures.' And this third includes the immoral person in belief, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is an editorial introduction lamenting the decline of proper scholarship in kalam and then citing a classical authority, al-Ghazali, on the prerequisites for teaching kalam. The editor first criticizes contemporary scholars for their superficiality, sectarianism, and unverified compilations. He then notes that many teachers of kalam merely recite terminology without understanding, and some even teach it with hidden animosity. He hopes for a return to the sound methodology of the early school. To clarify the proper qualities of a student of tawhid, he quotes al-Ghazali's three conditions: first, total devotion to learning so that doubts can be resolved; second, intelligence and eloquence so that the student can understand and argue; third, piety and righteousness so that they are not swayed by desires or fall away at the slightest doubt. The editor adds that the third quality covers even the immoral in belief.

الذكاء والفطنة والفصاحة
الصلاح والديانة والتقوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three conditions are for the teacher, not the student.
Correction: Al-Ghazali explicitly says 'the one who knows it should specify for teaching this science those who possess three characteristics'—the conditions describe the student being taught.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, which of the following is NOT one of the three qualities required in a student of kalam?
A) Devotion to learning
B) Wealth and social status
C) Intelligence and eloquence
D) Righteousness and piety

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's three conditions—devotion, intelligence, and piety—are essential for a student to benefit from kalam without falling into error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Are you truly free from preoccupations when you sit to learn, or is your mind divided? What worldly distraction must you set aside this week to seek knowledge with full devotion?

Taqlid and Hypocrisy in Sharh al-Wusta
Sanusi's emphasis on taqlid and hypocrisy in Sharh al-Wusta

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Sanusi intensified his condemnation of taqlid in Sharh al-Wusta, linking it to hypocrisy and asserting the disbelief of the blind follower.

Core Idea
Blind following is a hidden hypocrisy that destroys true knowledge.

This lesson focuses on one of the most striking features of Sharh al-Wusta: Sanusi's renewed and sharpened attack on taqlid (blind following). He not only warns against it but equates it with hypocrisy (nifaq) and declares the muqallid a disbeliever, following the majority of theologians. Let's explore his argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on taqlid
Carefully read the Arabic passage about taqlid. Notice how Sanusi cites Ibn Dahhaq's two types of hypocrisy and his criticism of contemporaries who downplay taqlid.
The passage includes a nuanced shift from his earlier position in Sharh al-Kubra.
2
Identify the two types of hypocrisy
Find where Sanusi narrates from Ibn Dahhaq that hypocrisy is of two types: the muqallid and the second in fundamentals. Understand how this distinguishes blind followers from actual hypocrites.
3
Analyze Sanusi's stance on the muqallid
Note that Sanusi now attributes to the majority of theologians the view that the muqallid is a kafir. Reflect on why he insists that knowing without knowing how is not true knowledge.
He refutes those who minimize the danger of taqlid.
4
Consider the practical impact
Think about what this means for a believer: one must have evidence-based certainty, not mere conjecture. The Salaf are presented as models of knowledge, far from taqlid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ماذا في ( شرح العقيدة الوسطى ) ؟ كانت عناية الإمام السنوسي منصرفةً إلى شرح « العقيدة الوسطى » دون الوقوف على كل جملة من جملها أو كلمة من كلماتها ، وقد انفرد هذا الشرح بإشباع مسائل عرض لها الإمام في غيره إيجازاً ولو كان هذا العرض في ( شرح العقيدة الكبرى ) ، وبإيراد مسائل لا تجدها في غيره ؛ فمن ذلك : - تجديد تشديده في مسألة التقليد ، مع بيان خطورة الأخذ وإلحاقه برتبة النفاق ؛ إذ نقل عن الإمام ابن دهاق بأنَّ النفاق نفاقان ، المقلّد والثاني هو في أصول الدين ، إلا أنَّه غيَّر يسيراً عما ذهب إليه في شرح العقيدة الكبرى » ؛ إذ جعل مذهب جمهور المتكلمين على رأي بعضهم هو القول بكفر المقلد ، وكذا تعييبُهُ على مَنْ أراد أن يهوّنَ شأن هذه المسألة من معاصريه كالعلامة ابن زكري ، ومن سبقه كالعلامة الشريف الإدريسي ، وتأكيده أنَّ مَنْ عَلِمَ ولم يعلم كيف عَلِمَ . . فلم يعلم ؛ إِنَّما توهّم نفسَهُ عالمةً ، ورتبتها الظن ، ودليلها التقليد ، وباختصار : لم يرجع الإمام في شرح العقيدة الوسطى » عن رأيه في مسألة المقلد دفاعه عن علم الكلام ، وبيان أن الصحابة ومن جاء بعدهم من السلف كانوا في أرفع درجة المعرفة ، وأبعد الناس عن التقليد ، وردُّه على الحشوية الذاهلين عن هذه الحقيقة . ذلك في وصف - تحريره للمطالب السبعة بطريقة رشيقة ، وقال عن عقيدته هذه : ( وذلكَ مِنْ محاسنِها ، وكم فيها مِنْ محاسنَ ! ) . - التوقف في الحكم على الجوهر المجرد ، تبعاً للعلامة الإمام المقترح - إثباته القول بالأحوال ، تبعاً للقاضي وإمام الحرمين . - ميله إلى القول بالتفويض في مسائل الصفات الزائدة على السبعة أو الثمانية ، تبعاً لأحد قولي الشيخ أبي الحسن وإمام الحرمين بأخرة . - عنايته البالغةُ بالنقل عن علمين جبلين في هذا العلم ؛ الإمام ابن دهاق ، فقد أكثر النقل عن كتابه « نكت الإرشاد » ، والإمام السعد
What is in (Sharh al-Aqida al-Wusta)? The attention of Imam al-Sanusi was directed to explaining al-Aqida al-Wusta without stopping at every sentence or word, and this commentary was unique in elaborating on issues that the Imam presented briefly elsewhere, even if this presentation was in (Sharh al-Aqida al-Kubra), and in presenting issues not found elsewhere. Among that: - Renewed emphasis on the issue of taqlid, with an explanation of the danger of accepting it and attaching it to the rank of hypocrisy; as he narrated from Imam Ibn Dahhaq that hypocrisy is of two types: the muqallid and the second is in the fundamentals of religion, except that he slightly changed what he had gone to in Sharh al-Aqida al-Kubra; as he made the school of the majority of theologians on the opinion of some of them the statement of the disbelief of the muqallid, and likewise his criticism of those who wanted to downplay the importance of this issue among his contemporaries like al-Allama Ibn Zakari, and those before him like al-Allama al-Sharif al-Idrisi, and his assertion that whoever knows but does not know how he knows... then he does not know; he only imagines himself knowledgeable, and his rank is conjecture, and his evidence is taqlid. In short: the Imam did not retract in Sharh al-Aqida al-Wusta from his opinion concerning the muqallid, his defense of the science of kalam, and his statement that the Companions and those after them from the Salaf were at the highest degree of knowledge and the farthest people from taqlid, and his refutation against the Hashwiyya who are heedless of this reality. That is in description. - His editing of the seven demands in an elegant manner, and he said about this creed of his: 'That is among its beauties, and how many beauties it has!' - The suspension of judgment on the abstract substance, following the Imam al-Muqtarih. - His affirmation of the doctrine of states, following al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn. - His inclination to the doctrine of delegation in issues of attributes beyond the seven or eight, following one of the two opinions of Shaykh Abi al-Hasan and Imam al-Haramayn eventually. - His great attention to quoting from two eminent scholars in this field: Imam Ibn Dahhaq, as he frequently quoted from his book 'Nukat al-Irshad', and Imam al-Sa'd.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is an editorial list of distinctive features of Sharh al-Aqida al-Wusta. The editor begins by noting that Sanusi's aim was to explain the creed without being overly wordy, and that this commentary uniquely elaborates on issues he treated briefly elsewhere, even in his own Sharh al-Kubra, and includes issues not found elsewhere. Then the editor lists six features: (1) Renewed emphasis on taqlid. Sanusi here intensifies his warning against taqlid, linking it to hypocrisy, and narrates from Ibn Dahhaq that hypocrisy has two types: the muqallid (blind follower) and the second type (actual hypocrisy in fundamentals). He slightly changes his earlier position in Sharh al-Kubra, now attributing to the majority of theologians the view that the muqallid is a disbeliever, and criticizes contemporaries who downplay this issue. He insists that one who knows but does not know how he knows is not truly knowledgeable, and that his rank is only conjecture based on taqlid. The editor notes that Sanusi did not retract his stance on the muqallid. (2) Elegant editing of the seven demands (al-matalib al-sab'a), and Sanusi's own remark that this is one of the beauties of his creed. (3) Suspension of judgment regarding the abstract substance (jawhar mujarrad), following al-Muqtarih. (4) Affirmation of the doctrine of states (ahwal), following al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn. (5) Inclination to tafwid (delegation of meaning) regarding attributes beyond the seven or eight, following one of the two opinions of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and Imam al-Haramayn in his later view. (6) Extensive reliance on two great scholars: Ibn Dahhaq (especially his book Nukat al-Irshad) and al-Sa'd. Each point shows a unique aspect of this commentary.

Key Terms
تقليد
نفاق
مقلد
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think taqlid is acceptable in matters of creed as long as one follows a scholar.
Correction: Sanusi shows that taqlid is a form of hypocrisy and can lead to disbelief; true faith requires evidence-based certainty.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does Sanusi attribute to the majority of theologians regarding the muqallid?
A) The muqallid is a hypocrite but not a disbeliever.
B) The muqallid is a disbeliever.
C) The muqallid is excused due to ignorance.
D) The muqallid is a believer with weak faith.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi equates taqlid with hypocrisy and insists the muqallid is a disbeliever, elevating the need for evidence-based faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your knowledge of Allah's attributes is simply what you were taught as a child without understanding the evidence, are you any different from the muqallid Sanusi condemns? What will you do today to replace conjecture with certainty?
?
Sanusi equates taqlid with hypocrisy. Have you ever felt satisfied with 'just believing' without knowing how you know? Can you honestly say your faith is built on evidence, or are you relying on the authority of others without personal conviction?
?
The Salaf were at the highest degree of knowledge and farthest from taqlid. Are you closer to their path or to those who blindly follow? What step will you take this week to move from being a muqallid to a muhaqqiq (one who verifies)?
