Sanusi on the Corruption of Crooked Minds
Sanusi describes corrupt individuals spreading falsehood and invokes Quran and poetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi describes the spread of falsehood by corrupt individuals and the importance of verification according to the Quran and the example of Zayn al-Abidin.

Core Idea
Crooked minds spread harmful lies; believers must verify reports and guard sacred knowledge.

Sanusi opens his commentary by painting a stark picture of his time: people with twisted understanding spread false words that harm both soul and religion. He invokes a Quranic command to verify reports from the wicked and quotes a poem by Zayn al-Abidin, who concealed knowledge to protect it from fools. This lesson sets the stage for Sanusi's justification of his work despite widespread corruption.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on false words
Read from 'عنا بحسب فهمه الأعوج' to the end of the poem. Notice how Sanusi characterizes the spreaders of falsehood and the Quranic cure.
The Quran verse (49:6) is central.
2
Understand the Quranic command
Reflect on the verse: verify reports from a wicked person to avoid harming others from ignorance. This is the standard Sanusi holds against the corrupt.
3
Analyze the poem of Zayn al-Abidin
The poem expresses the dilemma of concealing knowledge to prevent it from being misused and leading to persecution. Consider why a scholar might hide knowledge.
The poem uses a powerful example: people would call him an idol worshipper.
4
Connect to Sanusi’s context
Sanusi says such behavior is not surprising in his time. This sets up the problem that the following lesson addresses.
The first part ends with 'يرون أقبح ما يأتونه حسنا'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عنَّا بحسَبِ فهمِهِ الأعوج ودينه الأعرج مِنَ الكلماتِ الكاذبة ما يُوجِبُ الإذايةَ في النفسِ والدينِ ، ويَقْبَلُ ذلكَ منهُ ويُشِيعُهُ مَنْ هو على شاكلتِهِ ؛ ممَّنْ لم يمتثل قولَ اللهِ تعالى : يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِن جَاءَكُمْ فَاسِقٌ بِنَبَإٍ فَتَبَيَّنُوا أَن تُصِيبُوا قَوْمًا بِجَهَالَةٍ فَتُصْبِحُوا عَلَى مَا فَعَلْتُمْ نَادِمِينَ [الحجرات : ٦] . والله تعالى دَرُّ زين العابدين علي بن الحسين رضي الله عنهما حيثُ أنشد : [ من البسيط] إِنِّي لَأَكْتُمُ مِنْ عِلْمِي جَوَاهِرَهُ كَيْ لَا يَرَى ذَاكَ ذُو جَهْلِ فَيَفْتَتِنَا يَا رُبَّ جَوْهَرِ عِلْمٍ لَوْ أَبُوحُ بِهِ لَقِيلَ ذَلِكَ مِمَّنْ يَعْبُدُ الْوَثَنَا وَلا سْتَحَلَّ رِجَالٌ مُسْلِمُونَ دَمِي يَرَوْنَ أَقْبَحَ مَا يَأْتُونَهُ حَسَنَا لكن مثل هذا وأكثرُ منهُ لا يُستغرَبُ في هذا الزمان الذي نحنُ فيه ، وهو أواخر القرن التاسع ؛ الذي صار المعروفُ فيهِ مُنكَراً ، والمنكر فيه معروفاً ، وتعذَّرَ فيهِ معرفةُ الحقِّ لندورِ أهلِهِ ، واتَّسَعَ الخَرْقُ فيهِ جداً على الراقع ، فلم يبقَ فيه للعاقل إلا التحصُّنُ بالسكوت ، وملازمة البيوتِ ، والرضا في المعاش بأدنى القوتِ ولولا أنَّ الله سبحانَهُ لم يزل يتفضَّلُ في هذا الزمان الذي الشرُّ فيهِ منكشف عُرْيانٌ على نادرٍ مِنَ الناسِ ؛ بأن يشرح صدورهم لفهم الحقِّ وتمييزِهِ مِنَ الباطل ، وينور قلوبَهم بحسْنِ النيَّة ، وحب الخير وأهله ، وعدم إصغائهم - في اقتباس ما لاحَ لقلوبهم مِنَ النورِ إلى عدلِ كلِّ عاذل . . لكنتُ أقولُ : إِنَّ إبداء العلم في زماننا هذا يحرم بالكلية ويجب تجرُّدُ الإنسان لما يخصُّهُ في نفسِهِ مِنْ حفظ الجوارح وتحسينِ أمر الطويَّةِ ، لكن هذا النادرُ مِنَ الناسِ هو الذي أوقف العقل عنِ الجزم في هذا الزمانِ بهذا الحكم الظاهرِ فيه الريب، وإلى اللهِ سبحانه الرغبة في التوفيق إلى أرشدِ الأمور وأحمدها عاقبة ، وهو السميع المجيب . وهذا أوان الشروع في الشرح ، وبالله التوفيق
From us, according to his crooked understanding and his deviant religion, are false words that cause harm to soul and religion, and he accepts that from him and spreads it those who are like him; from those who have not complied with the saying of Allah Almighty: 'O you who have believed, if there comes to you a wicked person with a report, investigate, lest you harm a people out of ignorance and become regretful over what you have done' [Al-Hujurat: 6]. May Allah Almighty bless Zayn al-Abidin Ali ibn al-Husayn, may Allah be pleased with them both, when he recited: [Al-Baseet] 'Indeed I conceal the jewels of my knowledge, lest a person of ignorance see them and be tempted. Oh, how many a jewel of knowledge, if I were to disclose it, would be said: That is from one who worships idols. And Muslim men would deem my blood lawful, seeing what is ugliest among their deeds as good.' But like this and more is not surprising in this time in which we are, which is the end of the ninth century; the time in which what is good has become evil, and what is evil has become good, and knowledge of the truth has become difficult due to the scarcity of its people, and the tear has become very wide on the patch. So nothing remains for the intelligent person except fortifying himself with silence, staying at home, and being content with the least sustenance in livelihood. And were it not that Allah Almighty continues to bestow favor in this time, in which evil is bare uncovered, upon a rare type among people – by opening their chests to understanding the truth and distinguishing it from falsehood, and illuminating their hearts with good intention, love of good and its people, and their lack of listening – in acquiring what has shone upon their hearts of light – to the blame of every blamer – I would have said: Indeed, revealing knowledge in our time is absolutely forbidden, and a person must devote himself to what concerns his own self in guarding the limbs and improving the matter of the inner self. But this rare type among people is what stopped the intellect from decisively affirming this judgment, which is apparent in it doubt. And to Allah Almighty is the desire for success to the most guided of affairs and the most praiseworthy in outcome. He is the All-Hearing, the All-Answering. This is the time to begin the commentary. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is an introductory justification by Sanusi for writing his commentary despite the corruption of his time. He begins by describing how some people with crooked understanding and deviant religion spread harmful falsehoods, and are accepted by those like them who ignore the Quranic command to verify reports from a wicked person (Quran 49:6). He then quotes a poem by Zayn al-Abidin expressing the necessity of hiding knowledge to protect it from fools who would misuse it and even deem the scholar's blood lawful. Sanusi states that such behavior is unsurprising in his era—the end of the ninth century—when good and evil are reversed, truth is scarce, and the situation is beyond repair. In such conditions, the only prudent course for a rational person is silence, seclusion, and minimal living. However, he emphasizes that Allah has preserved a rare few who genuinely seek truth and good, and their presence prevents him from declaring that teaching knowledge is absolutely forbidden. He thus concludes that he will proceed with the commentary, seeking divine guidance. The passage serves as a methodological framing of his work, explaining why he writes despite the prevalent corruption.

Key Terms
الخبر الكاذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sanusi is simply complaining without purpose.
Correction: Sanusi's description grounds his decision to write; he uses the Quran and poetry to show the severity of corruption.

Checkpoint
What does the Quranic verse (49:6) command believers to do when a wicked person brings news?
A) Accept it immediately
B) Investigate it
C) Spread it further
D) Ignore it

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's first response to corruption is to recall the Quranic duty of verification and the tragic necessity of concealing knowledge from fools.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of the last piece of news you shared without verifying its source. Have you ever harmed someone's reputation or caused division by spreading a false report? Will you commit today to investigate before passing on any report, especially about a fellow Muslim?
?
The poet hides his knowledge because the ignorant would misuse it. Is there a truth you know—about your own sins, about a person, or about Islam—that you share freely without considering who is listening? Could your words cause harm? Ask Allah for wisdom to know when to speak and when to remain silent.
?
Sanusi warns of crooked minds that normalize ugly deeds. Look at your own heart: are there sins you have begun to see as 'good' because of the people around you? What false 'jewels' have you accepted from those who ignore Allah's command? Repent and seek the truth from the Quran and the righteous.

Silence, Scarcity, and the Decision to Teach
Sanusi discusses the state of the age, counsel of silence, and exception of sincere seekers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi navigates the dilemma between recommending silence in a corrupt age and continuing to teach due to the presence of a rare few sincere seekers.

Core Idea
Even in a time of reversed values, the existence of truth-seekers permits the scholar to teach.

Having described the corruption, Sanusi now paints the condition of his age—the end of the ninth century—where good and evil are reversed and truth is scarce. He argues that the only prudent course for a rational person is silence, seclusion, and minimal living. However, he cannot declare teaching absolutely forbidden because Allah has preserved a rare group of sincere seekers. Their presence stops him from certainty on that prohibition, so he proceeds to write. This lesson explains that crucial turn.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of the age
From 'لكن مثل هذا' to 'الرضا في المعاش بأدنى القوت'. Notice how Sanusi characterizes his time: good become evil, truth scarce, situation beyond repair.
The 'tear on the patch' metaphor means the problem is too big to fix.
2
Identify the counsel for the intelligent
Sanusi states that nothing remains for the intelligent except silence, staying home, and being content with minimum provision. Why would he say that?
3
Understand the exception
Read from 'ولولا أن الله' to the end. Sanusi says that without a rare few people whom Allah has guided, he would have prohibited teaching absolutely. These people are sincere seekers who understand truth and distinguish it from falsehood.
They are not swayed by critics.
4
Grasp the final decision
Sanusi says this rare group stops him from being certain that teaching is forbidden. Therefore, he proceeds with his commentary, seeking Allah's guidance. This is his justification.

?

?
If you were a teacher of the truth, would you use the corruption of the times as an excuse to withhold knowledge, or would you risk teaching in hope of finding sincere seekers? Which path requires more faith?
?
Sanusi says that the existence of sincere seekers stopped him from declaring teaching absolutely forbidden. Who are the sincere seekers you know? Are you one of them? If not, what must change in your intention and love for the truth to be counted among them?

Why Sanusi Teaches Despite Corrupt Times
Sanusi explains the corruption of his time and why he proceeds with teaching.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's justification for teaching sacred knowledge in a time of widespread corruption and scarcity of truth-seekers.

Core Idea
Truth is rare, but God's grace on a few justifies teaching.

Sanusi opens his commentary with a sobering assessment of his era: evil is rampant, truth is scarce, and the wise would retreat into silence. Yet he proceeds to teach. This lesson explores his reasoning—how the existence of a rare few sincere seekers, favored by God, prevents him from absolutely forbidding the transmission of knowledge. It reveals the balance between caution and duty in a scholar's heart.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice Sanusi's vivid imagery (the tear, the patcher) and his conditional reasoning.
Pay attention to the contrast between the general state of people and the rare exceptions.
2
Identify Sanusi's dilemma
The passage presents a tension: the rational response to corruption is silence, yet Sanusi does not remain silent. Find the 'if not' clause (لولا) that shifts his conclusion.
This is a key rhetorical move in classical scholarly prefaces.
3
Understand the role of the rare few
Sanusi points to a select group whom God has favored. Note the qualities listed: open chests, illuminated hearts, sincere intention, disregard for blame. These are the justification for teaching.
These qualities mirror the ideal student of knowledge.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Ask yourself: How does this preface frame the entire commentary? What does it say about the responsibility of a scholar in difficult times?
This connects to the learning objective about Sanusi's methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمنكر فيه معروفاً ، وتعذَّرَ فيهِ معرفةُ الحقِّ لندورِ أهلِهِ ، واتَّسَعَ الخَرْقُ فيهِ جداً على الراقع ، فلم يبقَ فيه للعاقل إلا التحصُّنُ بالسكوت ، وملازمة البيوتِ ، والرضا في المعاش بأدنى القوتِ ولولا أنَّ الله سبحانَهُ لم يزل يتفضَّلُ في هذا الزمان الذي الشرُّ فيهِ منكشف عُرْيانٌ على نادرٍ مِنَ الناسِ ؛ بأن يشرح صدورهم لفهم الحقِّ وتمييزِهِ مِنَ الباطل ، وينور قلوبَهم بحسْنِ النيَّة ، وحب الخير وأهله ، وعدم إصغائهم - في اقتباس ما لاحَ لقلوبهم مِنَ النورِ إلى عدلِ كلِّ عاذل . . لكنتُ أقولُ : إِنَّ إبداء العلم في زماننا هذا يحرم بالكلية ويجب تجرُّدُ الإنسان لما يخصُّهُ في نفسِهِ مِنْ حفظ الجوارح وتحسينِ أمر الطويَّةِ ، لكن هذا النادرُ مِنَ الناسِ هو الذي أوقف العقل عنِ الجزم في هذا الزمانِ بهذا الحكم الظاهرِ فيه الريب ، وإلى اللهِ سبحانه الرغبة في التوفيق إلى أرشدِ الأمور وأحمدها عاقبة ، وهو السميع المجيب . وهذا أوان الشروع في الشرح ، وبالله التوفيق :
And the disapproved therein is recognized, and knowledge of the truth became difficult due to the scarcity of its people, and the tear became very wide for the patcher. So nothing remained for the rational person except to fortify himself with silence, to stay at home, and to be content with the least sustenance in livelihood. Were it not that God, exalted is He, continues to bestow favor in this time, in which evil is openly exposed, upon a rare few of the people — by expanding their chests for understanding the truth and distinguishing it from falsehood, and illuminating their hearts with good intention, love of good and its people, and their not listening, in quoting the light that shone upon their hearts, to the blame of every blamer — I would have said: Indeed, revealing knowledge in our time is absolutely forbidden, and a person must devote himself to what concerns him regarding the preservation of his limbs and improving his inner intention. But this rare few is what stopped the intellect from decisively ruling, in this time, with this judgment that is apparently dubious. And to God, exalted, is the desire for success in guiding to the most right of matters and the most praiseworthy in outcome. And He is the All-Hearing, the Responsive. And this is the time to begin the commentary, and success is from God:
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a personal reflection by Sanusi on the state of his era. He describes a time when evil is so prevalent that it is considered good, and truth is rare. He says the only sensible course for a rational person would be to remain silent and isolated. However, because God still favors a few people by opening their hearts to truth and protecting them from the blame of critics, Sanusi cannot absolutely forbid the teaching of knowledge. He then decides to proceed with his commentary. The passage shows his caution and piety, acknowledging the difficulty but also the necessity of guiding those rare seekers.

Key Terms
ندور (scarcity)
الخَرْق (tear)
الراقع (patcher)
التحصُّن (fortification)
الطويَّة (inner intention)
العقل (intellect)
الحكم (judgment)
الريب (doubt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sanusi completely forbids teaching knowledge in corrupt times.
Correction: Sanusi says he would have forbidden it if not for the rare few whom God has favored with understanding and sincerity. Their existence prevents him from making an absolute prohibition.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi say would have been his ruling on teaching knowledge in his time if not for the rare people?
A) It would be absolutely forbidden
B) It would be recommended only for scholars
C) It would be permissible but disliked
D) It would be obligatory upon everyone

Key Takeaway
Even in the darkest times, the presence of sincere seekers compels a scholar to continue teaching.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you one of those 'rare few' whose heart God has expanded for understanding truth? What evidence in your life supports your answer?
?
Sanusi taught despite the blame of blamers. What truth are you afraid to speak because of what others might say?
?
If you were to judge your own inner intention, would you qualify as the kind of seeker that would make a scholar risk teaching in corrupt times? Why or why not?

Method of Learning
beginner
5 min
Purpose of the Book: From Taqlīd to Naẓar
Sanusi states the book's purpose and the hadith about beginning with praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's stated purpose for writing this book and the importance of beginning with praise.

Core Idea
Sound consideration (naẓar) is superior to imitation (taqlīd) in matters of faith.

Sanusi begins his text with a clear goal: to provide concise points that lead the reader from disputed imitation to universally accepted sound reasoning. This sets the methodological foundation for the entire work. The lesson also explains why he starts with praise, citing a prophetic hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening lines
Begin with the basmallah and the purpose statement. Notice the contrast between taqlīd (imitation) and al-naẓar al-ṣaḥīḥ (sound consideration).
The phrase 'takhruju al-mukallaf' shows the goal of the book.
2
Understand the hadith reference
Sanusi justifies beginning with praise using a hadith. Reflect on why starting with praise is important in Islamic tradition.
3
Connect to your own learning
Consider how this purpose shapes the rest of the book: you are being trained to think, not just imitate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمد لله رب العالمين، والصلاة والسلام على سيدنا ومولانا محمد خاتم النبيين وإمام المرسلين، ورضي الله تعالى عن أصحاب رسول الله أجمعين، ومن تبعهم بإحسان إلى يوم الدين.

وبعد: فهذه جمل مختصرة تخرج المكلف بفهمها إن شاء الله تعالى من التقليد المختلف في إيمان صاحبه إلى النظر الصحيح المجمع على إيمان صاحبه.

بدأ بالحمد امتثالاً لحديث: «كل أمر ذي بال لا يبدأ فيه بالحمد فهو أقطع»، ويروى (أجدم)؛ أي: ناقص البركة غير نام.

والحمد في المشهور: هو الثناء باللسان على المحمود بجميل الصفات، سواء تعلق بالفضائل أو بالفواضل.

وإن شئت قلت: هو الثناء بالكلام... إلى آخره، وهو أحسن؛ ليتناول التعريف الحمد القديم والحادث، بخلاف الأول؛ فإنه لا يتناول إلا الحمد الحادث؛ إذ اللسان لا يكون إلا للحوادث.

والشكر: هو الثناء باللسان أو بغيره إذا كان ذلك الثناء مقابلاً للنعم.

فالحمد أخص من الشكر بحسب محله، وأعم بحسب متعلقه، والشكر على العكس.

واستدل كثير من الأئمة على كون الشكر أعم بحسب المحل بقول الشاعر:

أفادتكم النعماء مني ثلاثة يدي ولساني والضمير المحجبا

وفي الاستدلال به نظر؛ إذ لم يطلق الشاعر لفظ الشكر على الثلاثة حتى يستدل بلفظه.
Praise be to God, Lord of the worlds, and blessings and peace upon our master and lord Muhammad, the seal of the prophets and the imam of the messengers, and may God be pleased with all the companions of the Messenger of God, and those who follow them in excellence until the Day of Judgment. To proceed: These are concise sentences whose understanding brings the legally responsible person out, God willing, from imitation (taqlīd) concerning which there is disagreement about the faith of its practitioner, to sound consideration (al-naẓar al-ṣaḥīḥ) concerning which there is consensus about the faith of its practitioner. He began with praise (al-ḥamd) in compliance with the hadith: 'Every matter of importance that does not begin with praise is cut off,' and it is also narrated as 'ajdam,' meaning deficient in blessing, not growing. And praise (al-ḥamd) according to the well-known definition is: laudation (al-thanā') by the tongue of the one praised for beautiful attributes, whether it pertains to inherent excellences (al-faḍā'il) or transitive virtues (al-fawāḍil). And if you wish, you may say: it is laudation by speech... etc., and this is better, because the definition encompasses eternal praise (al-ḥamd al-qadīm) and temporal praise (al-ḥamd al-ḥādith), unlike the first definition, for it encompasses only temporal praise, since the tongue exists only for temporal things. And gratitude (al-shukr) is: laudation by the tongue or by other than it, when that laudation is in return for blessings. So praise (al-ḥamd) is more specific than gratitude (al-shukr) with respect to its locus, and more general with respect to its object, and gratitude is the opposite. And many of the imams adduced as evidence that gratitude is more general with respect to its locus the saying of the poet: 'Your bounty has caused me three things: my hand, my tongue, and the hidden conscience.' But there is consideration regarding adducing this as evidence, since the poet did not apply the word 'gratitude' to these three things, so that one could adduce his word.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to Sanusi's work. It begins with the standard praise formula and prayer upon the Prophet, then states the purpose of the book: to provide concise points that will lead the legally responsible person from imitation (taqlīd) – which is disputed concerning the validity of the practitioner's faith – to sound consideration (al-naẓar al-ṣaḥīḥ) – which is agreed upon. The author then explains why he began with praise, citing the hadith that every important matter not begun with praise is cut off or deficient. He then defines 'praise' (al-ḥamd) in two ways: first, as verbal laudation by the tongue for beautiful attributes, whether inherent or transitive; second, more broadly as laudation by speech, which includes eternal praise and thus is superior because it covers both God's eternal praise and temporal praise. He contrasts this with the first definition, which only covers temporal praise since the tongue is temporal. Next, he defines 'gratitude' (al-shukr) as laudation by tongue or other means in return for blessings. He then compares the two: praise is more specific in locus (only tongue) but more general in object (not only for blessings), while gratitude is the opposite – more general in locus (includes heart, limbs) but more specific in object (only for blessings). Finally, he critiques a poetic proof used by some imams to show that gratitude is more general in locus, pointing out that the poet did not explicitly use the word 'shukr' for the three things mentioned, so the proof is invalid.

Key Terms
التقليد
النظر الصحيح
الحمد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that imitating scholars (taqlīd) is always sufficient and that reasoning (naẓar) is unnecessary.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that taqlīd is disputed in its validity, while sound reasoning brings consensus. Thus, reasoning is preferred.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the concise sentences mentioned in the introduction?
A) To provide easy memorization of theological facts.
B) To bring the legally responsible person from imitation to sound consideration.
C) To collect all opinions of scholars.
D) To refute heretical sects.

Key Takeaway
The book’s goal is to replace imitation with sound rational consideration, beginning with praise as taught by the Prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you content to imitate the beliefs of your family or community without personal investigation, knowing that there is disagreement about the validity of such faith? What step will you take today to engage in sound consideration of at least one core belief?
?
You read that the Prophet commanded beginning every important matter with praise. In which area of your life do you neglect to begin with gratitude to God? How will you change that starting tomorrow?
?
This lesson claims that sound consideration yields consensus on faith. What is one belief you hold that you have not personally verified through reasoning? Will you set aside time this week to examine it with the intention of certainty?

Method of Learning
intermediate
6 min
Defining Al-Ḥamd: Two Versions and Their Scope
Sanusi defines al-ḥamd in two ways and explains why the second is better.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of al-ḥamd and why the second is preferred.

Core Idea
True praise encompasses both eternal praise by God and temporal praise by humans.

Sanusi gives two definitions of praise (al-ḥamd). The first restricts it to verbal praise by the tongue for beautiful attributes. The second broadens it to any speech, allowing it to cover God’s eternal praise of Himself. This lesson explains the nuance and why the second definition is superior.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-ḥamd
Read the first definition carefully. Note the condition: by the tongue, for beautiful attributes, whether faḍā'il or fawāḍil.
2
Compare the second definition
See that the second expands 'by the tongue' to 'by speech' (bi al-kalām). Understand why this inclusion matters.
The second definition is 'ahsan' (better).
3
Reflect on the terms 'qadīm' and 'ḥādith'
The second definition includes eternal praise (like God's praise of Himself) which is not by a tongue. The first limits to temporal praise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمد لله رب العالمين، والصلاة والسلام على سيدنا ومولانا محمد خاتم النبيين وإمام المرسلين، ورضي الله تعالى عن أصحاب رسول الله أجمعين، ومن تبعهم بإحسان إلى يوم الدين.

وبعد: فهذه جمل مختصرة تخرج المكلف بفهمها إن شاء الله تعالى من التقليد المختلف في إيمان صاحبه إلى النظر الصحيح المجمع على إيمان صاحبه.

فالحمد أخص من الشكر بحسب محله، وأعم بحسب متعلقه، والشكر على العكس.

He began with praise (al-ḥamd) in compliance with the hadith: 'Every matter of importance that does not begin with praise is cut off,' and it is also narrated as 'ajdam,' meaning deficient in blessing, not growing. And praise (al-ḥamd) according to the well-known definition is: laudation (al-thanā') by the tongue of the one praised for beautiful attributes, whether it pertains to inherent excellences (al-faḍā'il) or transitive virtues (al-fawāḍil). And if you wish, you may say: it is laudation by speech... etc., and this is better, because the definition encompasses eternal praise (al-ḥamd al-qadīm) and temporal praise (al-ḥamd al-ḥādith), unlike the first definition, for it encompasses only temporal praise, since the tongue exists only for temporal things. And gratitude (al-shukr) is: laudation by the tongue or by other than it, when that laudation is in return for blessings. So praise (al-ḥamd) is more specific than gratitude (al-shukr) with respect to its locus, and more general with respect to its object, and gratitude is the opposite. And many of the imams adduced as evidence that gratitude is more general with respect to its locus the saying of the poet: 'Your bounty has caused me three things: my hand, my tongue, and the hidden conscience.' Commentary

It begins with the standard praise formula and prayer upon the Prophet, then states the purpose of the book: to provide concise points that will lead the legally responsible person from imitation (taqlīd) – which is disputed concerning the validity of the practitioner's faith – to sound consideration (al-naẓar al-ṣaḥīḥ) – which is agreed upon. Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi consider the second definition of al-ḥamd better than the first?
A) It is shorter and easier to remember.
B) It includes both eternal and temporal praise.
C) It mentions both faḍā'il and fawāḍil.
D) It is supported by the hadith.

Key Takeaway
The better definition of praise is laudation by speech, which covers both God's eternal praise and human temporal praise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Alḥamdulillāh', do you mean it only with your tongue, or does your heart truly join in that praise? How often do you praise Allah without being fully present?
?
God's self-praise is eternal and perfect, yet you limit your praise to fleeting moments. If your praise were judged by its completeness, would it be worthy of the One who praises Himself eternally? Reflect on one concrete change you can make today to align your praise with the second definition's broader scope.
?
The second definition encompasses both eternal and temporal praise. Do you live as though your praise fits only the first definition? How can you let your praise of Allah extend beyond specific times to become a constant attitude, even in difficulty?

Method of Learning
intermediate
5 min
Defining Al-Shukr and Comparing It with Al-Ḥamd
Sanusi defines al-shukr and contrasts it with al-ḥamd in terms of specificity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-shukr and how it differs from al-ḥamd in locus and scope.

Core Idea
Gratitude is broader in means but narrower in occasion than praise.

After defining praise, Sanusi turns to gratitude (al-shukr). He defines it as laudation in return for blessings, by tongue or other means. Then he compares praise and gratitude, showing that praise is more specific in locus (only tongue) but more general in object (not limited to blessings), while gratitude is the opposite. This completes the conceptual pair.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-shukr
Note that shukr is laudation by tongue or other than tongue, specifically in return for blessings. It can be by heart, tongue, and limbs.
2
Study the comparison
Sanusi gives a two-way comparison: praise is more specific in locus, more general in object; gratitude is the opposite. Grasp the logic.
Locus: means by which it is expressed. Object: what it is for.
3
Internalize the relationship
Think of examples: praising someone for their character (not necessarily a blessing) vs. thanking someone for a gift (only for blessings).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The author then explains why he began with praise, citing the hadith that every important matter not begun with praise is cut off or deficient. He then defines 'praise' (al-ḥamd) in two ways: first, as verbal laudation by the tongue for beautiful attributes, whether inherent or transitive; second, more broadly as laudation by speech, which includes eternal praise and thus is superior because it covers both God's eternal praise and temporal praise. He contrasts this with the first definition, which only covers temporal praise since the tongue is temporal. Next, he defines 'gratitude' (al-shukr) as laudation by tongue or other means in return for blessings. He then compares the two: praise is more specific in locus (only tongue) but more general in object (not only for blessings), while gratitude is the opposite – more general in locus (includes heart, limbs) but more specific in object (only for blessings). Finally, he critiques a poetic proof used by some imams to show that gratitude is more general in locus, pointing out that the poet did not explicitly use the word 'shukr' for the three things mentioned, so the proof is invalid.

Key Terms
الشكر
الحمد
المحل
المتعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think praise and gratitude are synonyms or that gratitude can only be verbal.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies their distinct scopes: praise is verbal only, but for any good; gratitude is broader in means but tied to blessings.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, how are al-ḥamd and al-shukr different in their loci?
A) Both have the same locus.
B) Praise has a more general locus than gratitude.
C) Praise has a more specific locus (only tongue) while gratitude has a more general locus (tongue and other).
D) Gratitude has a more specific locus than praise.

Key Takeaway
Gratitude is broader in expression (locus) but narrower in reason (blessings) compared to praise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever praised Allah for a blessing you didn't recognize until later? Which is more natural for you: shukr (gratitude for a specific blessing) or hamd (praise regardless of circumstance)? What does this lesson reveal about the state of your heart towards Allah's favors?
?
When you thank Allah, do you limit it to your tongue, or does your gratitude move your heart and actions? The source says shukr can be 'by other than the tongue'—what is one concrete way you can express gratitude with your limbs today, beyond just saying 'alhamdulillah'?
?
Allah says in the Quran, 'If you are grateful, I will surely increase you' (14:7). How does understanding that shukr is specific to blessings change your motivation to be grateful? Will you commit to consciously identifying one specific blessing each hour today and thanking Allah for it with your heart, tongue, and actions?

Critique of Poetic Evidence for Al-Shukr
Sanusi critiques a poetic proof for the generality of shukr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi finds the poetic proof for the generality of gratitude (shukr) weak.

Core Idea
A valid proof must use the term in question explicitly.

Some scholars cited a line of poetry to show that gratitude can be expressed by hand, tongue, and heart. Sanusi critiques this because the poet did not actually use the word 'shukr' for those actions. This lesson sharpens your critical eye for evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic proof
Read the line: 'afādatkum al-na‘mā’ minni thalāthatan / yadī wa-lisānī wa-ḍ-ḍamīru al-muḥajjabā.' Understand that it lists three things: hand, tongue, hidden conscience.
2
Identify the issue
Sanusi says you cannot use this as evidence for shukr because the poet did not explicitly call these acts 'shukr'. The proof assumes the connection.
The poet is describing gratitude implicitly but not naming it.
3
Reflect on the principle
This shows the importance of precise terminology in theological arguments. A proof must be explicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمد لله رب العالمين، والصلاة والسلام على سيدنا ومولانا محمد خاتم النبيين وإمام المرسلين، ورضي الله تعالى عن أصحاب رسول الله أجمعين، ومن تبعهم بإحسان إلى يوم الدين.

وبعد: فهذه جمل مختصرة تخرج المكلف بفهمها إن شاء الله تعالى من التقليد المختلف في إيمان صاحبه إلى النظر الصحيح المجمع على إيمان صاحبه.

بدأ بالحمد امتثالاً لحديث: «كل أمر ذي بال لا يبدأ فيه بالحمد فهو أقطع»، ويروى (أجدم)؛ أي: ناقص البركة غير نام.

والحمد في المشهور: هو الثناء باللسان على المحمود بجميل الصفات، سواء تعلق بالفضائل أو بالفواضل.

وإن شئت قلت: هو الثناء بالكلام... إلى آخره، وهو أحسن؛ ليتناول التعريف الحمد القديم والحادث، بخلاف الأول؛ فإنه لا يتناول إلا الحمد الحادث؛ إذ اللسان لا يكون إلا للحوادث.

والشكر: هو الثناء باللسان أو بغيره إذا كان ذلك الثناء مقابلاً للنعم.

فالحمد أخص من الشكر بحسب محله، وأعم بحسب متعلقه، والشكر على العكس.

واستدل كثير من الأئمة على كون الشكر أعم بحسب المحل بقول الشاعر:

أفادتكم النعماء مني ثلاثة يدي ولساني والضمير المحجبا

وفي الاستدلال به نظر؛ إذ لم يطلق الشاعر لفظ الشكر على الثلاثة حتى يستدل بلفظه.
Praise be to God, Lord of the worlds, and blessings and peace upon our master and lord Muhammad, the seal of the prophets and the imam of the messengers, and may God be pleased with all the companions of the Messenger of God, and those who follow them in excellence until the Day of Judgment. To proceed: These are concise sentences whose understanding brings the legally responsible person out, God willing, from imitation (taqlīd) concerning which there is disagreement about the faith of its practitioner, to sound consideration (al-naẓar al-ṣaḥīḥ) concerning which there is consensus about the faith of its practitioner. He began with praise (al-ḥamd) in compliance with the hadith: 'Every matter of importance that does not begin with praise is cut off,' and it is also narrated as 'ajdam,' meaning deficient in blessing, not growing. And praise (al-ḥamd) according to the well-known definition is: laudation (al-thanā') by the tongue of the one praised for beautiful attributes, whether it pertains to inherent excellences (al-faḍā'il) or transitive virtues (al-fawāḍil). And if you wish, you may say: it is laudation by speech... etc., and this is better, because the definition encompasses eternal praise (al-ḥamd al-qadīm) and temporal praise (al-ḥamd al-ḥādith), unlike the first definition, for it encompasses only temporal praise, since the tongue exists only for temporal things. And gratitude (al-shukr) is: laudation by the tongue or by other than it, when that laudation is in return for blessings. So praise (al-ḥamd) is more specific than gratitude (al-shukr) with respect to its locus, and more general with respect to its object, and gratitude is the opposite. And many of the imams adduced as evidence that gratitude is more general with respect to its locus the saying of the poet: 'Your bounty has caused me three things: my hand, my tongue, and the hidden conscience.' But there is consideration regarding adducing this as evidence, since the poet did not apply the word 'gratitude' to these three things, so that one could adduce his word.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to Sanusi's work. It begins with the standard praise formula and prayer upon the Prophet, then states the purpose of the book: to provide concise points that will lead the legally responsible person from imitation (taqlīd) – which is disputed concerning the validity of the practitioner's faith – to sound consideration (al-naẓar al-ṣaḥīḥ) – which is agreed upon. The author then explains why he began with praise, citing the hadith that every important matter not begun with praise is cut off or deficient. He then defines 'praise' (al-ḥamd) in two ways: first, as verbal laudation by the tongue for beautiful attributes, whether inherent or transitive; second, more broadly as laudation by speech, which includes eternal praise and thus is superior because it covers both God's eternal praise and temporal praise. He contrasts this with the first definition, which only covers temporal praise since the tongue is temporal. Next, he defines 'gratitude' (al-shukr) as laudation by tongue or other means in return for blessings. He then compares the two: praise is more specific in locus (only tongue) but more general in object (not only for blessings), while gratitude is the opposite – more general in locus (includes heart, limbs) but more specific in object (only for blessings). Finally, he critiques a poetic proof used by some imams to show that gratitude is more general in locus, pointing out that the poet did not explicitly use the word 'shukr' for the three things mentioned, so the proof is invalid.

Key Terms
الشكر
الاستدلال
الشعر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any action indicating gratitude can be cited as proof for the definition of shukr.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the term must be explicitly mentioned; mere implication is insufficient.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi consider the poetic evidence for shukr's generality in locus to be problematic?
A) The poet was not a reliable authority.
B) The poem does not mention the word 'shukr' explicitly.
C) The hand, tongue, and heart are not valid means of gratitude.
D) The poem contradicts a hadith.

Key Takeaway
A proof lacking explicit terminology cannot establish a technical definition, even if the meaning is implied.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend your beliefs to yourself or others, do you ever use arguments that sound good but lack a solid textual basis? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the foundations of what you claim to know?
?
This lesson shows that even respected scholars can make mistakes in proof. In your own journey of learning, how have you been guilty of accepting a claim without checking if the evidence is explicit? What is one area of your faith where you need to verify your proofs?
?
Sanusi warns against using a poem that doesn't even contain the key word. When you give dawah or discuss Islam, are you sometimes persuaded by eloquent words that lack the essential Qur'an or Sunnah text? How can you train yourself to be more rigorous?

The Linguistic Nature of al-Alam and the Need for Plural
Sanusi explains the linguistic reason for the plural in 'Rabbi al-Alamin'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the singular 'al-Alam' cannot refer to all creation and why the plural 'al-Alamin' is used in the Quran.

Core Idea
The singular 'world' points to one genus; the plural includes all.

The phrase 'Rabbi al-Alamin' uses the plural form 'alamin' rather than the singular 'al-alam'. Sanusi explains the linguistic reason: the singular 'alam' in Arabic refers to each distinct genus of creation, not the totality. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and shows how the plural solves the problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow. Then read the translation and note how Sanusi builds his argument.
2
Understand the meaning of 'alam'
Notice that the singular 'alam' in Arabic is used for each genus of creation on an alternative basis, not for the whole.
Examples: world of angels, world of humans, etc.
3
See why singular would mislead
If God had said 'Rabbi al-Alam', the default understanding would be that He is Lord of only one genus, likely the visible world.
4
Grasp how the plural solves it
The plural 'al-Alamin' includes every genus called 'alam', showing that His lordship covers all creation.
Also note that the plural is not a dual (which would mean two worlds).
5
Reflect on the key takeaway
Ask yourself: why is this linguistic detail important for theology? It ensures that the first verse of the Quran establishes Allah's universal lordship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يقل : رب العالم ليفيد الجمع شمول الربوبية جميع أجناس العالم؛ إذ العالم المفرد إنما يطلق في اللغة على كل جنس من تلك الأجناس على البدل، وليس هو في اللغة اسماً لمجموع سوى الله تعالى؛ بحيث لا يكون له أفراد بل أجزاء، فيمتنع. ولأجل أن العالم في اللغة موضوع لكل جنس من المخلوقات على البدل، يقال: عالم الملك، وعالم الإنس، وعالم الجن، وكذا عالم الأفلاك، وعالم النبات، وعالم الحيوان، فلو أفرد لربما تبادر إلى الفهم أنه إشارة إلى الجنس والحقيقة على ما هو الظاهر في تعريف المفرد عند عدم العهد، فجمع ليشمل كل جنس يسمى بالعالم، وفي هذا الجمع دلالة على أن القصد إلى الأفراد دون نفس الحقيقة والجنس. والذي يجمع هو العالم لا العالمين. وما يزعمه بعض الأصوليين من أن مثله يكون للجنس أيضاً، وتبطل الجمعية.. فإنما هو حيث لا يصح الاستغراق. وأشار الزمخشري في قوله تعالى: {الحمد لله رب العالمين} إلى أنه جمع؛ لئلا يتبادر إلى الفهم أنه إشارة إلى هذا العالم المشاهد بشهادة العرف. ورد عليه التفتازاني في حواشيه: بأن المقابل للعالم المشاهد هو العالم الغائب، فإذا كان الإفراد موهماً أن المقصود هو الأول فقط.. ناسب أن يثنيا ليتناولهما معاً؛ فإن الكل مندرج فيهما قطعاً. فإن قلت: العالم لا يطلق على واحد من جنس المسمى به كزيد مثلاً، فإذا جمع امتنع استغراقه لأفراد جنس واحد؛ لأن الجمع إنما يعم الآحاد التي يطلق مفرده عليها.
And He did not say 'Lord of the world' to indicate that the plural includes all genera of the world in lordship; since the singular 'the world' is only used in language for each genus of those genera on an alternative basis, and it is not in language a name for a totality other than God, such that it has no individuals but parts, thus it is impossible. And because 'the world' in language is assigned to each genus of creation on an alternative basis, one says: the world of angels, the world of humans, the world of jinn, likewise the world of celestial spheres, the world of plants, the world of animals. If He had used the singular, it would likely have been understood as a reference to the genus and essence, as is apparent in the definition of the singular in the absence of a previous agreement. Therefore He used the plural to include every genus called 'world', and in this plural there is an indication that the intention is the individuals, not the essence and genus itself. That which is pluralized is 'world', not 'two worlds'. And what some usuliyyun claim, that such (a plural) could also indicate the genus and invalidate the collective meaning, is only valid where comprehensive coverage is not possible. And al-Zamakhshari pointed out in His saying: 'Praise be to God, Lord of the worlds' that it is plural, so that it would not be understood as a reference to this visible world, by testimony of common usage. And al-Taftazani objected to him in his glosses: that the corresponding opposite to the visible world is the unseen world. So if the singular would lead to the false assumption that only the first is intended, it would be appropriate to use the dual to include both together, for all are certainly included in them. If you say: 'world' is not used for a single individual of the genus named by it, like Zayd for example. So if it is pluralized, it is impossible for it to cover the individuals of a single genus, because the plural only generalizes over the singulars to which its singular form applies.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic-theological argument explaining why the Quran uses the plural 'رب العالمين' instead of the singular 'رب العالم'. It begins by stating the problem: the singular 'العالم' in Arabic does not refer to a single collection of all creation but rather is applied to each distinct genus of created beings (e.g., the world of angels, the world of humans) on an alternative basis. Therefore, if God had said 'رب العالم', the immediate understanding—given no prior context—would be that He is Lord of only one genus, most likely the visible world. To avoid this, the plural is used, signaling that every genus called 'عالم' is intended. The author then clarifies that the pluralized word is 'عالم' (making it a sound masculine plural) and not 'عالمين' as a dual, though the latter might appear similar. Next, he refutes a claim by some usul scholars that such a plural construction could also be interpreted as signifying the genus itself, thereby undermining the collective meaning. He argues that this interpretation only applies where comprehensive coverage of individuals is impossible—which is not the case here. After establishing his own reasoning, the author cites al-Zamakhshari, who supported the plural reading to prevent limiting the verse to the visible world. This is followed by al-Taftazani's objection: since the opposite of the visible world is the unseen world, the dual 'العالمين' would have been more suitable to include both. Finally, the passage presents a hypothetical question: if 'عالم' cannot be applied to a single individual of a genus (like 'Zayd'), how can its plural cover all individuals of a genus? The answer is that the plural form generalizes over the singulars to which the singular form applies—thus it is valid for 'عالم' to mean 'worlds' as collections of individual members.

Key Terms
العالم
العالمين
رب
جمع
إفراد
جنس
أفراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the singular 'رب العالم' would be sufficient because 'العالم' means all creation in one totality.
Correction: In Arabic, the singular 'العالم' refers to a specific genus, not the totality. The plural is necessary to cover all genera.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does God use the plural 'رب العالمين' instead of the singular 'رب العالم'?
A) It is for exaltation and respect.
B) To include all genera of creation.
C) To refer to the visible and unseen worlds only.
D) Because the singular does not exist in Arabic.

Key Takeaway
The plural 'العالمين' in 'رب العالمين' is used to encompass all genera of creation, preventing the limitation that the singular would imply.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'the world', do you unconsciously limit it to your own experience, ignoring the vastness of other creation like angels, jinn, and celestial realms? How would your understanding of Allah's lordship change if you actively remembered all these worlds?
?
In your daily life, do you sometimes assume that Allah's care and provision are limited to your own small world? How does knowing that He is Lord of every genus—every category of creation—challenge you to expand your view of His mercy?
?
The singular form could cause misunderstanding—are there areas in your faith where you are content with a limited understanding of Allah's attributes? Will you commit to studying the names of Allah more deeply to avoid such limitations?

Scholarly Discussions on the Plural 'Alamin'
Sanusi addresses scholarly objections and alternative views on the plural form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections and responses regarding the plural form 'alamin', including views of Zamakhshari and Taftazani.

Core Idea
The plural 'alamin' is defended against claims that it could indicate genus or should be dual.

After establishing the basic linguistic reason, Sanusi addresses additional scholarly points: a claim by some usul scholars that such a plural could indicate the genus, the interpretation of Zamakhshari favoring plural to avoid limiting to the visible world, and Taftazani's objection that the dual would be more appropriate. He also resolves a hypothetical linguistic puzzle. This lesson examines these nuanced discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, paying attention to the arguments of the usul scholars, Zamakhshari, and Taftazani.
2
Understand the usul claim
Some usuliyyun claimed that the plural could also indicate the genus, thereby invalidating the collective meaning. Sanusi refutes this by saying it only applies when comprehensive coverage is not possible.
Here, comprehensive coverage of all worlds is possible, so the claim fails.
3
Analyze Zamakhshari's view
Zamakhshari supported the plural to avoid misunderstanding that the verse refers only to the visible world.
4
See Taftazani's objection
Taftazani argued that since the opposite of the visible world is the unseen world, a dual would have been more appropriate to include both.
5
Grasp the final hypothetical and resolution
A hypothetical objection: if 'عالم' cannot be applied to a single individual of a genus, how can its plural cover all individuals? The answer: the plural covers the singulars to which the singular form applies (i.e., the different genera).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Correction: The passage shows that Taftazani's dual suggestion is an objection, not the accepted view. The plural is used to cover all genera.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did al-Zamakhshari support the plural reading?
A) To indicate respect.
B) To avoid limiting the verse to the visible world.
C) To follow the usul scholars.
D) To use the dual form.

Key Takeaway
The plural 'العالمين' is defended against various objections, confirming its role in encompassing all creation.

Plural Generality by Inclusion
Objection and answer on plural generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a comprehensive plural in Arabic can cover individuals by inclusion (تضمن), not only by exact correspondence (مطابقة).

Core Idea
A plural can cover by inclusion, not just by exact match.

The verse {رب العالمين} uses the plural 'عالمون', which al-Zamakhshari argued avoids limiting to the seen world. But al-Taftazani counters with a deeper point: the plural covers all worlds, visible and invisible, through inclusion. This lesson examines the objection and answer that clarify this rule of Arabic semantics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the flow from Zamakhshari's point to Taftazani's rebuttal, then the objection and answer.
Pay attention to the analogy with 'أقاويل' and 'أقوال'.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that 'عالم' is not applied to individuals like Zayd, so its plural cannot cover individuals of a single genus. Recognize this objection clearly.
3
Understand the answer
The answer asserts that the comprehensive plural covers by exact correspondence or by inclusion. The example of 'أقاويل' shows that a plural of many can include items not covered by the plural of few.
4
Reflect on the principle
Understand how this principle resolves the original issue: 'عالمون' covers all worlds, whether the singular 'عالم' applies to each individually or not, because it covers by inclusion.
This shows linguistic depth in kalam argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار الزمخشري في قوله تعالى: {الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ رَبِّ الْعَالَمِينَ} إلى أنه جمع؛ لئلا يتبادر إلى الفهم أنه إشارة إلى هذا العالم المشاهد بشهادة العرف. ورد عليه التفتازاني في حواشيه: بأن المقابل للعالم المشاهد هو العالم الغائب، فإذا كان الإفراد موهما أن المقصود هو الأول فقط، ناسب أن يثنيا ليتناولهما معا؛ فإن الكل مندرج فيهما قطعا. فإن قلت: العالم لا يطلق على واحد من جنس المسمى به كزيد مثلا، فإذا جمع امتنع استغراقه لأفراد جنس واحد؛ لأن الجمع إنما يعم الآحاد التي يطلق مفرده عليها. قلت: لا نسلم أن الجمع المستغرق إنما يعم الآحاد التي يطلق مفرده عليها فقط، بل يعم كل ما دل عليه مفرده مطابقة أو تضمنا. فكما أن لفظة الأقاويل تتناول كل واحد من آحاد الأقوال وإن لم يصدق لفظ الأقوال عليه، كذلك العالمون يتناول كل واحد من آحاد الأجناس وإن لم يصدق لفظ العالم عليه.
And al-Zamakhshari pointed out in His saying, Exalted is He: {Praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds} that it is plural; so that it does not leap to the mind that it refers to this witnessed world by the testimony of custom. And al-Taftazani countered him in his glosses: that the counterpart of the witnessed world is the absent world; so if the singular gives the illusion that the intended is only the first, it is appropriate to dualize it to encompass both; for the whole is certainly included in them. For you say: The world (العالم) is not applied to a single individual of the genus of what is named by it like Zayd, for example; so if it is pluralized, its comprehending the individuals of a single genus is prevented; because the plural only covers the individuals to which its singular is applied. I say: We do not concede that the comprehensive plural only covers the individuals to which its singular is applied exclusively; rather it covers everything that its singular denotes by way of exact correspondence or by inclusion. So just as the word 'al-aqāwīl' (the sayings [plural of many]) encompasses each one of the individual sayings even if the word 'al-aqwāl' (the sayings [plural of few]) does not apply to it, likewise 'al-ʿālamūn' (the worlds) encompasses each one of the individual genera even if the word 'al-ʿālam' (the world) does not apply to it.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the use of the plural 'عالمون' in the verse 'رب العالمين'. The discussion begins by mentioning al-Zamakhshari's observation that the plural is used to avoid being understood as referring only to the observed world. Then al-Taftazani objects that the counterpart of the observed world is the unseen world, so the dual 'عالمين' (which is actually the plural form) would encompass both. Then a new objection is raised: the word 'عالم' is not applied to an individual like 'Zayd', so when it is pluralized, it cannot encompass individuals of a single genus because the plural only covers individuals that its singular applies to. The answer to this is that the comprehensive plural does not only cover individuals that its singular applies to by exact correspondence (مطابقة), but also covers what its singular denotes by inclusion (تضمن). For example, the plural of many 'أقاويل' encompasses individual 'قول' even though 'أقوال' (the plural of few) is not applied to it. Similarly, 'عالمون' encompasses each individual genus even though 'عالم' is not applied to it. This argument shows that the plural 'عالمون' is comprehensive enough to cover all worlds, both visible and invisible.

Key Terms
استغراق
مطابقة
تضمن
جمع
مفرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the answer, how does the plural 'عالمون' cover individuals of different genera?
A) By exact correspondence (مطابقة) only
B) By inclusion (تضمن) only
C) Both by exact correspondence and inclusion
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
A comprehensive plural in Arabic can denote individuals by inclusion (تضمن) as well as by exact correspondence, allowing terms like 'عالمون' to cover all worlds.

Objection: Singular More Comprehensive than Plural in 'عالم'
An objection that the singular 'عالم' is more comprehensive for generality than the plural 'عالمون'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the singular form 'عالم' is more comprehensive than the plural 'عالمون' and the reasoning behind it.

Core Idea
The plural's comprehensiveness allows exceptions, making the singular more inclusive.

In the broader discussion of the term 'عالم' in the Islamic creed, a linguistic-rational objection arises: the singular form might be more comprehensive than the plural. This lesson presents that objection, which challenges the choice of 'عالمون' in the creed. Understanding this nuance deepens your grasp of how kalam engages with Arabic rhetoric.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice how it presents an objection using 'فإن قلت' (for you say) style.
This is a common dialectical format in kalam.
2
Understand the reasoning
The objection argues that the plural's comprehensiveness (استغراق الجمع) means inclusion of the whole (المجموع), which does not preclude the exclusion of one or two individuals. This makes the plural less comprehensive than the singular.
The singular's comprehensiveness is claimed to be more absolute.
3
Identify the implication
If the singular is more comprehensive, then using 'عالم' would be better for expressing complete inclusion. The objection suggests the creed should have used the singular instead of 'عالمون'.
This is a linguistic technicality within the larger argument.
4
Note that this is an objection
The passage ends with the objection; the answer (to the objection) is not included in this segment. This is a typical dialectical move where the opposition is stated before being refuted.
Look out for the answer in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: قد ذكروا أن استغراق المفرد أشمل؛ بناء على أن معنى استغراق الجمع شموله المجموع، وهو لا ينافي خروج فرد أو فردين، فقد يقال: يترجح هنا التعبير بالمفرد لهذا
For you say: They have mentioned that the comprehensiveness of the singular is more inclusive; based on the meaning of the comprehensiveness of the plural being its inclusion of the whole, and that does not preclude the exclusion of one or two individuals; so it may be said: the expression with the singular is preferable here for this reason.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection. The objector argues that the singular form 'عالم' is more comprehensive than the plural 'عالمون' according to the scholars. The reasoning is that the plural's comprehensiveness covers the whole group, which can still allow the exclusion of one or two individuals. Therefore, the singular would be better to express complete inclusion. The text cuts off before the answer, but the objection sets up a traditional debate in Arabic rhetoric about the scope of singular vs. plural generics.

Key Terms
استغراق المفرد
استغراق الجمع
شمول المجموع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a plural always indicates complete generality without exception.
Correction: This objection notes that plural comprehensiveness (استغراق الجمع) covers the whole but may still allow exceptions of one or two individuals, so the singular is more comprehensive.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why is the singular considered more comprehensive than the plural?
A) Because the plural only covers individuals that its singular applies to.
B) Because the plural's comprehensiveness covers the whole, which may still allow exception of some individuals.
C) Because the singular cannot be used for multiple genera.
D) Because the plural is used exclusively for visible worlds.

Key Takeaway
The objection claims the singular 'عالم' is more comprehensive because the plural's inclusiveness allows exceptions of individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Generality of 'al-'Alamin' and Refutation of Ibn Malik
Sanusi discusses the generality of 'al-'alamin' and refutes Ibn Malik's claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definite plural 'al-'alamin' is considered general and includes all creatures, and how Sanusi refutes Ibn Malik's claim that it is specific to rational beings.

Core Idea
The definite plural 'al-'alamin' is general, not limited to rational beings.

In classical Arabic, there is a well-known principle that a definite plural includes every individual of its kind. However, the renowned grammarian Ibn Malik challenged this regarding the word 'al-'alamin'. In this passage, Sanusi defends the generality of 'al-'alamin' and refutes Ibn Malik's argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the two positions and the author's conclusion.
Notice that the passage begins with a reference to earlier scholars.
2
Identify Ibn Malik's claim
Ibn Malik argued that 'al-'alamin' is specific to rational beings because it is a plural of 'alam', and the singular is more general than its plural.
3
Analyze Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi rejects Ibn Malik's claim, stating that 'al-'alamin' is not specific to rational beings and is an irregular plural of 'alam'. Reflect on the consensus of scholars that supports this view.
The refutation is based on linguistic consensus and counterexample.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلت : هذا رأي سلكه السكاكي وتبعه عليه جلال الدين القزويني واعترضه عليهما التفتازاني في مطوله بما هو مشهور ولئن سلم لهما ذلك فإنما يصح ذلك في مثل : لا رجل ولا رجال وأما شمول الجمع المعرف باللام لكل فرد فمما اتفق عليه أئمة التفسير والأصول والنحو وما زعمه ابن مالك : أن العالمين ليس جمعا للعالم بناء على أن العالم المفرد عام والعالمون الجمع خاص بالعقلاء والمفرد لا يكون أعم من جمعه .. غير مسلم والحق : أن العالمين غير خاص بالعقلاء وأنه جمع للعالم على غير قياس
I said: This is an opinion adopted by al-Sakaki, and followed by him by Jalal al-Din al-Qazwini, and objected to them by al-Taftazani in his 'Mukhtasar' with what is well-known. And if it is conceded to them that, that would only be correct in something like 'la rajul' and 'la rijal'. As for the inclusion of the definite plural with 'al' for every individual, that is agreed upon by the imams of tafsir, usul, and nahw. And what Ibn Malik claimed: that 'al-`alamin' is not a plural of 'al-`alam' based on the fact that 'al-`alam' singular is general and 'al-`alamin' plural is specific to rational beings, and the singular cannot be more general than its plural... is not conceded. The truth is that 'al-`alamin' is not specific to rational beings, and that it is a plural of 'al-`alam' irregularly.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic discussion about the word 'al-`alamin'. The author begins by citing the opinion of al-Sakaki and al-Qazwini on a point of Arabic grammar, and notes that al-Taftazani objected to them. The point is about the generality of the definite plural. The author then states that the generality of the definite plural for every individual is agreed upon by experts, and refutes Ibn Malik's claim that 'al-`alamin' is not a genuine plural of 'al-`alam' because the singular is general and the plural is specific to rational beings. The author rejects this, affirming that 'al-`alamin' is not specific to rational beings and is an irregular plural of 'al-`alam'.

Key Terms
العالمين
جمع معرف باللام
عموم
ابن مالك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'العالمين' refers only to humans or rational beings.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that 'العالمين' is not specific to rational beings; it is a plural of 'العالم' that includes all creatures, even though it is an irregular plural.

Checkpoint

B) It is not specific to rational beings and is an irregular plural of 'العالم'
C) It is a singular noun with general meaning
D) It is a plural that cannot be used generically

Key Takeaway
The word 'al-'alamin' is an irregular plural of 'alam' and includes all creatures, not just rational beings.

Definition of a Sahabi (Companion)
Definitions of the term sahabi with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition and conditions required to be considered a companion (sahabi) of the Prophet.

Core Idea
A companion is one who met the Prophet while Muslim and died Muslim.

Before we discuss theological arguments, it's important to know what we mean by 'sahabi'. This passage clarifies the term used by scholars. It gives a concise definition that will help you understand references to the companions in later lessons.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key terms: 'Ashab', 'Sahb', 'Sahib'.
The commentator explains the plural form before defining the term.
2
Identify the definition
Locate the exact definition of a sahabi. It contains two conditions: meeting the Prophet and dying as a Muslim.
The definition is after 'innahu'.
3
Note the conditions
The definition has two conditions: 1) met the Prophet while being a Muslim and alive, 2) died as a Muslim. Notice also that 'living Muslim' is applied to the person at the time of meeting.
There is no mention of hearing hadith or fighting alongside.
4
Understand the qualification
The commentator says detailed conditions belong to Hadith science. This means the basic definition is given here, but deeper discussion is elsewhere.
This shows the boundary between kalam and hadith studies.

Understanding Over Memorization in Kalam
The commentator explains the indefinite 'juman' and the emphasis on understanding over memorization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator emphasizes understanding over memorization, and how the indefinite 'juman' signals the unique value of these sentences.

Core Idea
Understanding the sentences elevates you from taqlid; memorization aids but does not replace understanding.

In this passage, the commentator digs into the words of the author, explaining the significance of the indefinite 'juman' (sentences) and why the author specified 'by understanding it' rather than 'by memorizing it'. This lesson will help you grasp the methodological priority of comprehension in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Parse the grammatical context
Read the opening line about 'wa ba'd' and see how the commentator sets up the sentence structure. Notice the implied condition verb.
This is a technical grammatical point that prepares for the main discussion.
2
Understand the indefinite 'juman'
The commentator gives two possible meanings for the indefinite: paucity or glorification. Reflect on how each interpretation affects the meaning of the passage.
Both interpretations highlight the special status of these sentences.
3
Grasp the emphasis on understanding
The commentator explicitly states that the author said 'by understanding it' not 'by memorizing it' to show that comprehension is what matters. However, he then qualifies this by noting the benefits of memorization.
This is the central methodological lesson: understanding is primary, but memorization is a valuable aid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وبعد ) هو ظرفٌ مقطوع عن الإضافة لفظاً دون معنى، والعاملُ فيهِ فعلُ شرطٍ حُذِفَ، والتقديرُ : ( مهما يكنْ مِنْ شيءٍ بعد هذا الكلام السابق ... ) فهذهِ جُمَلٌ ( وهي جمع جملةٍ ؛ وهي هنا ما تضمَّنَ إسناداً مفيداً )، وتنكيرُ ( جملٌ ) إمَّا للتقليل ؛ تنبيهاً على أنَّها جُمَلٌ قليلةً العددِ، ثم هي معَ ذلكَ تُخرِجُ مَنْ فهمها عن درجة التقليد إلى مرتبة عالية في المعارفِ، وإما للتعظيم ؛ تنبيهاً على عِظَم شأنها لعظم ما جَمَلَتْهُ مِنْ نفيس العلم باللهِ تعالى، وتأييده بقواطع البراهين. وإنَّما قالَ : ( تُخرِجُ المكلَّفَ بفهمها إِنْ شاءَ اللهُ تعالى )، ولم يقل : ( بحفظها ) إشارةً إلى أنَّ فهم معانيها هو المعتبر، لا مجرَّدُ حفظ حروفها، إلا أنَّ حفظ اللفظ مِنْ أعونِ الأمور على فهم المعنى، وعلى التعبير عنه، والترقي في مراتب الفهوم، ولهذا كثيراً ما يترقى بالحفظِ مَنْ لم يُعهَدْ بدقَةِ النظرِ إلى درجةِ مَنْ عُرِفَ بها، بل وكثيراً ما يزيد عليه بمراتب بعيدة. وإنَّما اقتصر على المكلَّف - وإِنْ كانَ غيرُ المكلف ؛ كالصبي المميّز مثلاً يخرجُ أيضاً بفهمها مِنَ التقليد - للتنبيه على أنَّ المكلَّفَ هو الذي يتأكَّدُ في حقِّهِ أنْ يتفهم ما يُخلِّصُهُ مِنَ التقليدِ، لئلا يفجأَهُ الموتُ قبل حصولِ المعرفة
His statement: 'And after' is a circumstance severed from addition in form but not in meaning, and the operative factor in it is a deleted condition verb, and the estimated phrase is: 'Whatever is after this previous speech...' So these are sentences (the plural of sentence; and here it is what contains a meaningful predication), and the indefinite form of 'sentences' is either for paucity, indicating that they are few in number, yet they elevate the one who understands them from the level of taqlid to a high rank in knowledge, or for glorification, indicating the greatness of their status due to the noble knowledge of Allah they contain, and its support by decisive proofs. And he only said: 'it brings out the mukallaf by understanding it, if Allah wills', and he did not say: 'by memorizing it', as an indication that understanding its meanings is what matters, not merely memorizing its letters. However, memorizing the wording is among the most helpful things for understanding the meaning, and for expressing it, and for rising in the levels of understanding. For this reason, many who are not known for sharpness of insight ascend by memorization to the level of those who are known for it, and even often exceed them by many degrees. And he only restricted it to the mukallaf - even though the non-mukallaf, such as the discerning child, also comes out of taqlid by understanding them - to indicate that the mukallaf is the one for whom it is emphasized to comprehend what delivers him from taqlid, so that death does not suddenly overtake him before attaining knowledge.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the author's wording. It begins by parsing the grammatical structure of 'wa ba'd' as a circumstantial phrase with an implied condition. Then it discusses the indefinite 'juman' (sentences), explaining that the indefinite can indicate either the small number of these sentences or their great importance. The commentator stresses that the author said 'by understanding it' not 'by memorizing it' to show that comprehension is what matters. However, he qualifies this by noting that memorization aids understanding and can even elevate a person to higher levels. Finally, he explains why the author specified 'mukallaf' (the morally responsible person): although a non-mukallaf also benefits, the mukallaf must make greater effort because death could come at any moment, and they need to attain knowledge to avoid dying in a state of taqlid.

Key Terms
جمل
فهم
حفظ
مكلَّف
تقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Memorization alone is sufficient for theological knowledge.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that understanding the meanings is what matters; memorization is merely a helpful tool.

Checkpoint
Why did the author say 'by understanding it' instead of 'by memorizing it'?
A) Because memorization has no value in kalam
B) To indicate that understanding is what matters, not mere memorization
C) Because only the mukallaf can understand
D) To show that understanding is easier than memorization

Key Takeaway
In kalam, understanding the meanings of the sentences is paramount, while memorization is a supportive tool.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the shahada or other creedal statements, do you truly understand the meanings behind each word, or are you simply repeating sounds you have memorized?
?
If death came upon you this night, would your understanding of Allah's oneness be deep enough to save you from being a blind follower?
?
You have memorized many proofs, but have you allowed their meanings to penetrate your heart and transform your relationship with Allah, or are they just stored in your memory?

Memorization Aids Understanding
Sanusi extols the benefits of memorizing the wording of texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how memorization of wording helps in understanding meaning, expression, and intellectual advancement, and why it can even surpass pure intellectual insight.

Core Idea
Memorization is not just rote—it is a powerful tool that can elevate understanding.

In this passage, Sanusi makes a surprising claim: memorizing the wording of theological texts is among the most helpful things for understanding their meaning. He argues that memorization can even surpass deep intellectual insight in some cases. This lesson unpacks that claim and its implications for the student of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and reflect
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi presents the benefits of memorization.
The passage begins with a concession about memorizing letters before stating the main point.
2
Identify the benefits listed
Sanusi lists three benefits: understanding meaning, expressing it, and ascending in ranks of understanding. Find these in the text.
3
Consider the claim about surpassing insight
Sanusi adds that memorization can elevate a person without deep insight to the level of one with it, or even surpass them. Reflect on why this might be true.
This connects to the learning objective about memorization's value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حفظ حروفها ، إلا أنَّ حفظ اللفظ مِنْ أعونِ الأمور على فهم المعنى ، وعلى التعبير عنه ، والترقي في مراتب الفهوم ، ولهذا كثيراً ما يترقى بالحفظِ مَنْ لم يُعهَدْ بدقَةِ النظرِ إلى درجةِ مَنْ عُرِفَ بها ، بل وكثيراً ما يزيد عليه بمراتب بعيدة

قبل حصولِ المعرفة
Memorizing its letters, but memorizing the wording is among the most helpful things for understanding the meaning, and for expressing it, and for ascending in the ranks of understandings. For this reason, often one who is not known for precision of insight ascends through memorization to the degree of one who is known for it, nay, often surpasses him by many degrees. And he only restricted to the legally responsible person - although the non-legally responsible person, such as the discerning child for example, also emerges from blind imitation by understanding it - to indicate that the legally responsible person is the one for whom it is emphasized to comprehend what will rescue him from blind imitation, lest death overtake him before attaining knowledge.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a methodological note. The author first defends the value of memorizing the wording (lafẓ) of texts, arguing that it greatly aids understanding of meaning (ma‘nā), expression, and intellectual advancement. Often, someone not known for deep insight can reach the level of a recognized scholar through memorization, or even surpass him. The author then explains why he restricts his discussion to the mukallaf (the legally responsible person): although a non-mukallaf like a discerning child can also escape taqlid through understanding, the mukallaf is the one for whom it is most urgent to seek knowledge that frees him from blind imitation, lest death surprise him before attaining knowledge. This highlights the practical urgency of the work.

Key Takeaway
Memorization of the wording of texts is a powerful tool that can lead to deep understanding and even surpass natural intellectual insight.

?

?

Focus on the Mukallaf
Sanusi explains his focus on the mukallaf to highlight the urgency of escaping taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi restricts his discussion to the legally responsible person (mukallaf) and why this is urgent.

Core Idea
The mukallaf must prioritize knowledge that rescues from blind imitation before death.

After extolling memorization, Sanusi explains a key methodological choice: he addresses only the mukallaf, though others can benefit too. He does this to emphasize the urgency for those who are legally responsible to attain certain knowledge. This lesson explores that reasoning.

Guided Path

2

3

The reason is 'lest death overtake him before attaining knowledge.' Consider how this motivates the mukallaf to seek knowledge promptly.
This connects to the learning objective about the importance of focusing on the mukallaf.
