Types of Reflection: Initial, Reminiscent, and Reminding
A note classifies reflection into three types and mentions the Mu'tazila position on the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of reflection (naẓar) in Ashʿarī theology and the disputed nature of initial reflection with the Muʿtazila.

Core Idea
Reflection's types depend on prior cognition, not on reasoning method.

Sanusi introduces a classification of reflection that is foundational to the Ashʿarī theory of knowledge. He distinguishes three types based on whether the soul has prior acquaintance with the subject matter. This classification also serves to clarify the theological disagreement with the Muʿtazila over the source of initial reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the three types and their defining conditions.
The passage is a marginal note, so it is concise.
2
Identify each type and its condition
List the three types: initial, reminiscent, reminding. For each, note the condition about prior cognition in the soul.
Initial means no prior cognition; reminiscent means active reconsideration; reminding means knowledge comes without active retrieval.
3
Understand the theological dispute
Compare the Ashʿarī view (all three created by Allah) with the Muʿtazila view (initial reflection by human power and its resulting knowledge by generation).
This highlights a core difference in causality between the two schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم: أن الأنظار ثلاثة؛ ابتدائي: وهو الذي لم يتقدم للنفس، وتذكري: وهو الذي تقدم لها واسترجعته، وذكري: وهو الذي تقدم لها ثم أتاها من غير استرجاع. والثلاثة عندنا بخلق الله تعالى، وخالف المعتزلة في الابتدائي؛ فهو عندهم بالقدرة الحادثة، ونتيجته التي هي العلم بالتولد.
Know that reflections are three: initial (ibtidāʾī) — which has not preceded for the soul; reminiscent (tadhkurī) — which has preceded for it and it reconsiders it; and reminding (dhikrī) — which has preceded for it and then it comes to it without reconsideration. And all three, in our view, are by the creation of Allah Most High, and the Mu'tazila differed concerning the initial; for it is, in their view, by created power, and its result which is knowkledge is by generation (tawallud).
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This marginal note provides a classification of reflection (naẓar) into three types based on the soul's prior relation to the subject matter. The first is initial reflection (ibtidāʾī), where the soul has no prior cognition of the matter. The second is reminiscent reflection (tadhkurī), where the soul has prior cognition and reconsiders it. The third is reminding reflection (dhikrī), where the soul has prior cognition and the knowkledge comes to it without active reconsideration. The note affirms that all three types are created directly by Allah, countering the Muʿtazilī claim that initial reflection is produced by the servant's created power (qudra ḥāditha) and that its resulting knowkledge comes through generation (tawallud). This classification reinforces the Ashʿarī rejection of any creaturely causality in reflection and knowkledge.

Key Terms
ابتدائي
تذكري
ذكري
النفس
استرجاع
بخلق الله
بالقدرة الحادثة
التولد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the three types of reflection refer to different reasoning methods or that the Muʿtazila agree with the Ashʿarīs on all three types.
Correction: The classification is based solely on the soul's prior relation to the subject, not on the reasoning method. The Muʿtazila only differ on the initial type, claiming it is by human power.

Checkpoint
According to the note, which type of reflection does the Mu'tazila say is produced by the servant's power?
A) All three
B) The reminding (dhikrī)
C) The initial (ibtidāʾī)
D) The reminiscent (tadhkurī)

Key Takeaway
Reflection is categorized by its relation to prior knowledge, and all reflection is directly created by Allah, contrary to the Muʿtazila view on initial reflection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you first encounter a new Islamic concept, do you actively recognize that your very ability to think and reflect about it is created by Allah? How does this awareness (or lack thereof) affect your humility and reliance on Him?
?
The Muʿtazila claimed that initial reflection is within human power. In your own journey of seeking knowledge, have you ever felt a sense of pride in your own reasoning? What would change in your heart if you truly believed that every thought you have is directly created by Allah?
?
If all reflection is from Allah, then your successes in understanding theology are His gift and your struggles are His test. Will you today, in your next moment of reflection, pause and thank Allah for the very act of thinking, and ask Him to guide your thoughts?

Imam al-Haramayn on the Rational Connection Between Nazar and Ilm
Imam al-Haramayn's rational connection between nazar and ilm with obstacle distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Haramayn's doctrine that the connection between valid reasoning and knowledge is rationally necessary, and how he accounts for obstacles that might prevent knowledge.

Core Idea
Reasoning and knowledge are tied by a rational bond, not by chance or convention.

Imam al-Haramayn, a leading Ash'ari theologian, presents a key principle about how we come to know things through reasoning. He argues that when reasoning is valid and no external obstacles exist, knowledge necessarily follows. This idea establishes the reliability of rational inquiry as a path to knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice that Imam al-Haramayn makes a strong claim about the relationship.
The Arabic has some textual issues we'll note.
2
Identify the two types of obstacles
Imam al-Haramayn distinguishes between general obstacles (like death) and specific obstacles (like ignorance). See how he treats them differently.
3
Consider the analogy
He compares the connection to that between a substance and its accident. Think about what that analogy implies about necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال إمام الحرمينِ : الربط بينهما عقلي ، كارتباط الجوهر بمطلقِ ، فلا يمكن عندَهُ أنْ يحصل نظر صحيح ولا يُوجَدَ العلمُ [العرض؟] بالمدلول عقيبه إذا انتفت عن الناظر الناظر الآفات العامَّةُ المضادَّةُ للعلم كالموت ونحوه ، وأمَّا الآفاتُ الخاصَّةُ ؛ كالجهل ونحوه .. فوجود النظرِ الصحيح يدفعُها ، فلا يُحتاجُ إلى اشتراطِ نَفْيها
And Imam al-Haramayn said: The connection between them is rational, like the connection of the substance to the absolute [?]. So according to him, it is not possible that a correct reasoning occur and [nor] is knowledge of the [?] by the indicated found after it, if the general obstacles contrary to knowledge, like death and the like, are removed from the thinker. And as for the specific obstacles, like ignorance and the like – the existence of correct reasoning repels them, so there is no need to stipulate their negation.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrine of Imam al-Haramayn regarding the relationship between valid reasoning (nazar sahih) and the knowledge (ilm) that results from it. He asserts that the connection between them is rational (aqli), not conventional. He likens it to the connection between a substance and its accident – that is, just as a substance necessarily possesses an accident under certain conditions, so too correct reasoning necessarily yields knowledge under certain conditions. He then qualifies this by distinguishing two types of obstacles: general obstacles (afat 'amma) like death which preclude knowledge entirely, and specific obstacles (afat khassa) like ignorance which are actually removed by the reasoning itself. Therefore, he concludes that only the negation of general obstacles is required; specific obstacles are automatically negated by the presence of correct reasoning.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
علم (ilm)
آفات (afat)
ربط عقلي (rational connection)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Imam al-Haramayn denies that nazar always produces ilm.
Correction: He affirms it under normal conditions; only general obstacles (like death) can prevent it.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Haramayn, what is the relationship between correct reasoning and knowledge?
A) conventional
B) rational
C) arbitrary
D) doubtful

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Haramayn teaches that valid reasoning necessarily yields knowledge as long as no general obstacles like death are present; specific obstacles like ignorance are overcome by the reasoning itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in reasoning about your faith, what 'general obstacles' might be present in your life that prevent the knowledge from settling in your heart? Are you merely going through intellectual exercises while ignoring death and its reality? Commit to one action today to remove that obstacle.
?
You claim to seek knowledge, but do you truly trust that correct reasoning will remove ignorance? What specific doubts or ignorances are you holding onto that you have not subjected to sound reasoning? What step will you take this week to apply nazar to that area?
?
Imam al-Haramayn says the connection between reasoning and knowledge is rational, not accidental. Do you approach your study of aqeedah as a necessary path to certainty, or do you treat it as optional or coincidental? What does your daily practice reveal about your belief?

Classification of Nazar into Three Types and the Mu‘tazila Opposition
Sanusi lists three types of nazar and contrasts Ash'ari and Mu'tazila views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of reasoning (nazar) in kalam and the theological dispute over whether the initial type is created by Allah or produced by human power.

Core Idea
Reasoning is either initial, recollective, or reminder—and who creates it matters.

This passage from Sanusi's footnote provides a concise classification of reasoning (nazar) into three types, each defined by the soul's prior engagement. It then immediately introduces a key theological disagreement: are all three directly created by Allah, or does human power produce the initial type as the Mu'tazila claim? Understanding this classification is essential for grasping the Ash'ari position on human agency and causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the three types
Read the Arabic passage once and identify the three categories of nazar. Notice the specific terms: ibtida'i, tadhakkuri, and dhikri.
The passage begins with 'اعلم: أن الأنظار ثلاثة'.
2
Understand the definitions of each type
Examine how Sanusi defines each type based on whether the soul has previously engaged in the reasoning and whether it recalls it.
Ibtida'i is new reasoning; tadhakkuri is recalled; dhikri comes without recall.
3
Identify the Ash'ari claim
Notice the phrase 'والثلاثة عندنا بخلق الله تعالى' – all three are created by Allah. This is the Ash'ari position.
This asserts direct divine creation of all acts of reasoning.
4
Recognize the Mu'tazila opposition
The text says 'وخالف المعتزلة في الابتدائي' – the Mu'tazila opposed on the initial type. Understand their view: ibtida'i is by human power and its result (knowledge) is generated (tawallud).
This highlights a core difference in causality between the two schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أن الأنظار ثلاثة ؛ ابتدائي : وهو الذي لم يتقدم للنفس ، وتذكري : وهو الذي تقدم لها واسترجعته ، وذكري : وهو الذي تقدم لها ثم أتاها من غير استرجاع . والثلاثة عندنا بخلق الله تعالى ، وخالف المعتزلة في الابتدائي ؛ فهو عندهم بالقدرة الحادثة ، ونتيجته التي هي العلم بالتولد
Know that the reasonings (anzar) are three: al-ibtida'i (initial): that which has not preceded for the soul; al-tadhakkuri (recollective): that which has preceded it and it recalled it; and al-dhikri (reminder): that which has preceded it then it came to it without recall. And all three according to us are by the creation of Allah, may He be exalted. And the Mu'tazila opposed in the initial (ibtida'i); so it is according to them by originated power, and its result, which is knowledge, by generation (tawallud).
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage, taken from a footnote, first provides a classification of reasoning (nazar) into three types: (1) ibtida'i: reasoning that has not been previously engaged in by the soul; (2) tadhakkuri: reasoning that the soul has done before and then recalls; (3) dhikri: reasoning that the soul has done before but then encounters again without an act of recall. The author then asserts that according to the Ash'aris all three types are directly created by Allah. However, the Mu'tazila disagreed specifically regarding the ibtida'i type, claiming it is produced by the servant's originated power (qudra haditha), and its resulting knowledge is generated (mutawallid).

Key Terms
أنظار (anzar/nazar types)
ابتدائي (ibtida'i)
تذكري (tadhakkuri)
ذكري (dhikri)
خلق (creation)
تولد (tawallud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume the Mu'tazila agreed with Ash'aris on all three types being created by Allah.
Correction: The Mu'tazila only opposed on the ibtida'i type; they agreed on the other two.

Checkpoint
Which type of reasoning did the Mu'tazila claim is produced by human power?
A) tadhakkuri
B) dhikri
C) ibtida'i
D) all three

Key Takeaway
The three types of reasoning differ based on the soul's prior engagement, and the Ash'ari-Mu'tazila dispute centers specifically on the causation of the initial type.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in initial reasoning (ibtida'i) – for example, figuring out a new concept or solving a problem for the first time – do you consciously acknowledge that this ability is created by Allah, or do you take credit for it? How would your mental posture shift if you truly internalized that every fresh insight is a divine gift? This week, before you begin a challenging intellectual task, pause and say: 'O Allah, create in me the reasoning I need.'
?
Think of a time when a profound truth suddenly came back to you without effort (dhikri). Did you recognize it as a reminder from Allah, or did you dismiss it as a random thought? What if every spontaneous moment of clarity is actually a direct act of divine creation – would that change how you value and respond to such moments? Commit to thanking Allah the next time you experience a sudden insight.
?
The Mu'tazila believed that initial reasoning is produced by your own power and the resulting knowledge is generated from it. Examine your heart: do you genuinely attribute your intellectual achievements to your own ability, or to Allah? Search for any subtle pride in your intelligence or learning. If you find it, repent and affirm that 'there is no power nor strength except by Allah' – not as a slogan but as a lived conviction.

The Differing Views on the First Obligation
Multiple opinions on the first obligatory act for a legally responsible person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the major scholarly opinions on the first obligation (awwal wajib) upon a legally responsible person, and how some of these views can be reconciled.

Core Idea
The first obligation is debated, but many views converge on correct reasoning with proper intent.

This passage presents a rich discussion among Sunni theologians about what a person must do first when they become accountable. Al-Ash'ari, al-Juwayni, al-Baqillani, and others each propose a different starting point: reasoning, intention, knowledge, doubt, or affirmation. Understanding these views clarifies the foundations of Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first view
Read the opening statement about al-Ash'ari's view that correct reasoning (al-nazar al-sahih) is the first obligation. Note that this is the main Ash'ari position.
2
Examine the second view
Notice the view of al-Ustadh and Imam al-Haramayn that the first obligation is the intent (qasd) to reason, which includes purifying the heart from pride, envy, etc.
This purification is considered the first guidance from Allah.
3
Reconcile the knowledge view
Understand the view that knowledge (ma'rifa) is the first obligation, attributed to al-Ash'ari as well. The author reconciles it by distinguishing between intention and performance.
Intention vs. performance: one looks at what is intended, the other at what is enacted.
4
Contrast the Mu'tazila and final views
Learn the Mu'tazila view that doubt (shakk) is the first obligation, and another view that it is affirmation (iqrar). Compare these to the earlier views.
These views differ significantly from the mainstream Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيانُ أَوَّلِ واجب على المُكلَّف وهذا النظرُ الصحيح هو أوَّلُ واجب على المكلف عند الشيخ الأشعري . وذهب الأستاذ وإمام الحرمين إلى أنَّ أوَّلَ واجب القصد إلى النظر ؛ أي : توجيه القلْبِ إليه بقطع العلائق المنافية له ، ومنها : الكِبْرُ ، والحسد ، والبِغْضَةُ للعلماء الداعين إلى الله سبحانه وتعالى ، وتطهيرُ القلبِ مِنْ هذه الأخلاقِ هو أوَّلُ هدايةِ اللهِ تعالى للعبدِ وقال القاضي : أوَّلُ واجب أوَّلُ جزء مِنَ النظر . وقيل : أَوَّلُ واجب المعرفةُ ، ويُعزى للشيخ أيضاً ، وهو في الحقيقة غيرُ مخالف لما قبله ؛ لأنَّهُ نظرَ إلى أَوَّلِ واجب مقصداً ، وغيرَه نظر إلى أول واجب امتثالاً وأداء . وقالَتِ المعتزلة - وعُزِيَ أيضاً للأستاذ ابنِ فُورَكَ - : إِنَّ أَوَّلَ واجبٍ الشقُ . وقيل : أَوَّلُ واجب الإقرار باللهِ تعالى وبرُسُلِهِ عَن عَقْدِ مطابق وإِنْ كان جاهلاً بهما
Statement of the first obligation upon the legally responsible person. And this correct reasoning is the first obligation upon the legally responsible person according to al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari. And al-Ustadh and Imam al-Haramayn held that the first obligation is the intent (qasd) to reason; that is, directing the heart towards it by cutting off the ties that are contrary to it, among which are: pride, envy, and hatred of the scholars who call to Allah, may He be exalted and high; and purification of the heart from these moral traits is the first guidance of Allah, may He be exalted, to the servant. And al-Qadi said: the first obligation is the first part of reasoning. And it was said: the first obligation is knowledge (ma'rifa), and it is attributed to al-Shaykh as well, and it is in fact not contradictory to what preceded; because he looked to the first obligation intentionally, while others looked to the first obligation in terms of compliance and performance. And the Mu'tazila – and it is also attributed to al-Ustadh Ibn Furak – said: indeed the first obligation is doubt (shakk). And it was said: the first obligation is affirmation (iqrar) of Allah, may He be exalted, and His messengers, with a corresponding resolve, even if he is ignorant of them.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed discussion of the first obligation (awwal wajib) upon a legally responsible person (mukallaf). It begins by stating that according to al-Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, the first obligation is correct reasoning (nazar sahih). Then it presents the view of al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini and Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni: the first obligation is the intention (qasd) to reason, which involves directing the heart towards reasoning by cutting off obstacles like pride, envy, and hatred of scholars, and purifying the heart – which is itself the first guidance from Allah. Next, al-Qadi (Abu Bakr al-Baqillani) held that the first obligation is the very first part of reasoning. Another view, attributed to al-Ash'ari as well, is that the first obligation is knowledge (ma'rifa). The author reconciles this by noting that al-Ash'ari looked at the first obligation in terms of intention (maqsadan) while others looked at it in terms of actual performance (imtithalan). Then the Mu'tazila, and also attributed to al-Ustadh Ibn Furak, held that the first obligation is doubt (shakk). Finally, another opinion is that the first obligation is affirmation (iqrar) of Allah and His messengers with a heartfelt resolution, even if one is ignorant of the details.

Key Terms
أول واجب (first obligation)
مكلف (mukallaf)
قصد (intent)
معرفة (knowledge)
شق (shakk/doubt)
إقرار (affirmation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The different opinions on the first obligation are contradictory and cannot be reconciled.
Correction: The knowledge view attributed to al-Ash'ari is reconciled with his view of correct reasoning by distinguishing between intention (maqsadan) and actual performance (imtithalan), showing that the differences are often a matter of perspective.

Checkpoint
According to al-Qadi (al-Baqillani), what is the first obligation?
A) Correct reasoning
B) Intent to reason
C) The first part of reasoning
D) Knowledge

Key Takeaway
The first obligation reflects different scholarly perspectives, but all emphasize the necessity of correct reasoning and purity of intention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage mentions that purifying the heart from pride, envy, and hatred of scholars is the first guidance from Allah. Which of these traits have you allowed to linger in your heart, and what is one concrete action you will take this week to uproot it?
?
Some scholars held that the first obligation is doubt. But when you face doubt about your faith, do you use it as a stepping stone to seek certainty, or does it lead you to procrastinate and avoid the truth? What is your heart's honest response?
?
Al-Ash'ari viewed the first obligation as an intention, while others saw it as an act of performance. In your relationship with Allah, do you often go through the motions without the sincere intention behind them? What would change if you prioritized correcting your intention first?

Opinions on the First Obligation of the Mukallaf
Sanusi enumerates six opinions on the first obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different scholarly opinions on what constitutes the first obligation for a legally responsible person (mukallaf) and how they can be reconciled.

Core Idea
The first obligation varies depending on whether we consider intention or performance.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a range of scholarly views on the very first duty that falls upon a person who has reached the age of accountability. From the primacy of reflection to the necessity of intention, these opinions reflect different perspectives on the starting point of religious obligation. Understanding this diversity is crucial for grasping the foundations of Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the opinions
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that Sanusi lists six distinct views. Try to list them in order.
The first opinion is attributed to al-Ash'ari, the last to the Mu'tazila.
2
Understand the reconciliation
Sanusi notes that some opinions are not contradictory but look at the 'first' in terms of intention versus performance. Reflect on how this resolves apparent disagreement.
3
Consider the Mu'tazila view
The Mu'tazila opinion mentions 'al-shaqq' (likely 'al-shakk' meaning doubt). Why might doubt be considered the first obligation? Think about the role of doubt in seeking truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيانُ أَوَّلِ واجب على المُكلَّف] وهذا النظرُ الصحيح هو أوَّلُ واجب على المكلف عند الشيخ الأشعري. وذهب الأستاذ وإمام الحرمين إلى أنَّ أوَّلَ واجب القصد إلى النظر؛ أي: توجيه القلْبِ إليه بقطع العلائق المنافية له، ومنها: الكِبْرُ، والحسد، والبِغْضَةُ للعلماء الداعين إلى الله سبحانه وتعالى، وتطهيرُ القلبِ مِنْ هذه الأخلاقِ هو أوَّلُ هدايةِ اللهِ تعالى للعبدِ. وقال القاضي: أوَّلُ واجب أوَّلُ جزء مِنَ النظر. وقيل: أَوَّلُ واجب المعرفةُ، ويُعزى للشيخ أيضاً، وهو في الحقيقة غيرُ مخالف لما قبله؛ لأنَّهُ نظرَ إلى أَوَّلِ واجب مقصداً، وغيرَه نظر إلى أول واجب امتثالاً وأداء. وقالَتِ المعتزلة - وعُزِيَ أيضاً للأستاذ ابنِ فُورَكَ -: إِنَّ أَوَّلَ واجبٍ الشقُ. وقيل: أَوَّلُ واجب الإقرار باللهِ تعالى وبرُسُلِهِ عَن عَقْدِ مطابق وإِنْ لم يكن علماً، وإبطال هذا القول بإبطال القول بصحة التقليد، وقد تقدَّمَ ما فيهِ مِنَ الخلافِ.
Exposition of the first obligation on the mukallaf. This correct reflection (al-nazar) is the first obligation on the mukallaf according to al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari. Al-Ustadh and Imam al-Haramayn held that the first obligation is the intention (al-qasd) to reflect; i.e., directing the heart towards it by cutting off hindering attachments, among which are: pride, envy, and hatred of the scholars who call to Allah Almighty, and purifying the heart from these traits is the first guidance of Allah Almighty to the servant. Al-Qadi said: The first obligation is the first part of reflection. It was said: The first obligation is knowledge (al-ma'rifa), and it is attributed to the Shaykh as well, and it is in reality not contrary to what preceded; because he looked at the first obligation in terms of intention, while others looked at the first obligation in terms of compliance and performance. The Mu'tazila said—and it is also attributed to al-Ustadh Ibn Furak—that the first obligation is the [؟] (al-shaqq). And it was said: The first obligation is affirmation (al-iqrar) of Allah and His messengers with a corresponding resolve, even if it is not knowledge, and the refutation of this statement is by refuting the statement of the validity of imitation (taqlid), and the disagreement about it has preceded.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the various scholarly opinions regarding the first obligation (awwal wajib) upon the mukallaf (the legally responsible person). The author lists five positions: 1) Al-Ash'ari: the first obligation is correct reflection (al-nazar). 2) Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq and Imam al-Haramayn: it is the intention (al-qasd) to reflect, which involves directing the heart and purifying it from negative traits like pride and hatred. 3) Al-Qadi: it is the first part of reflection itself. 4) Some say it is knowledge (al-ma'rifa), and this is reconciled with the first view by noting that 'first intention' vs. 'first performance' differ. 5) The Mu'tazila (and also Ibn Furak): the first obligation is doubt (al-shakk, though written as al-shaqq in the text). 6) Another opinion: it is affirmation (al-iqrar) of Allah and His messengers even without knowledge, but this is refuted by rejecting the validity of taqlid. The passage thus presents a range of views and hints at their reconciliation.

Key Terms
أول واجب
نظر
القصد إلى النظر
المعرفة
التقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The different opinions on the first obligation are contradictory and irreconcilable.
Correction: Sanusi explains that some opinions look at intention (qasd) while others look at performance (imtithal), so they are not truly opposed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which view on the first obligation is attributed to al-Qadi?
A) The first obligation is correct reflection (nazar).
B) The first obligation is the intention to reflect (qasd al-nazar).
C) The first obligation is the first part of reflection.
D) The first obligation is knowledge (ma'rifa).

Key Takeaway
There are multiple valid perspectives on the first obligation of the mukallaf, and they can be reconciled by distinguishing between intention and performance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The second opinion emphasizes cleansing the heart of pride, envy, and hatred toward scholars before seeking truth. Examine your own heart: is there any resentment or arrogance that blocks your learning? How will you remove it?
?
The passage reconciles opinions by distinguishing between intention and performance. In your worship and study, do you focus more on the intention behind your actions or their correct execution? Where is your imbalance, and what one change can you make to bring them together?

Usul al-Fiqh Requires Knowledge Like Usul al-Din
Sanusi declares usul al-fiqh requires knowledge like usul al-din.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the study of usul al-fiqh, like usul al-din, requires certain knowledge ('ilm) and that blind imitation (taqlid) is not sufficient in these foundational disciplines.

Core Idea
Usul al-fiqh demands knowledge, not mere imitation.

This brief passage from Sanusi's commentary makes a critical methodological point: the discipline of usul al-fiqh is treated like usul al-din in requiring certain knowledge. The author emphasizes that mere imitation (taqlid) is not sufficient in these foundational sciences. This lesson explains the reasoning behind this alignment and its implications for Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, noting that it is incomplete due to a page break. Then read the English translation to grasp the main idea.
The passage ends abruptly; the intended completion is that taqlid is not sufficient.
2
Identify the central claim
The author claims that usul al-fiqh is 'annexed' to usul al-din in requiring knowledge. Ask yourself: what does this annexation imply about the epistemic standard?
Both disciplines are foundational and thus demand certainty, not conjecture or blind following.
3
Reflect on the importance
Consider why requiring knowledge rather than taqlid matters for Islamic jurisprudence. How does this principle affect the way scholars derive rulings?
It elevates usul al-fiqh to the level of creedal foundations, requiring rigorous reasoning.

Source Text

He means: The scholars said: 'And the principles of jurisprudence are annexed to the principles of religion' (2); - and Allah knows best -: in that what is required in them is knowledge, and there is not sufficient in them

Commentary
This brief passage states that the principles of jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh) are treated like the principles of religion (usul al-din) in that what is required in them is knowledge ('ilm), and imitation (taqlid) is not sufficient. The exact phrase 'ولا يكفي فيها' is cut off, but the intended meaning is that taqlid is not sufficient in usul al-fiqh, just as it is not sufficient in usul al-din. This is a methodological point aligning the two disciplines in terms of epistemic requirement.

Annexation of Usul al-Fiqh and the True Status of Nazar
Sanusi states the annexation of usul al-fiqh to usul al-din, contrasts furu', and refutes al-Sharif's view on nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why usul al-fiqh is annexed to usul al-din in requiring certain knowledge, how the Lawgiver pardons ignorance in furu', and why nazar is an original obligation rather than a subsidiary one.

Core Idea
Nazar is an original obligation because it is the means to iman, not a subsidiary duty.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary addresses a key methodological issue: the relationship between usul al-fiqh and usul al-din, and the nature of nazar (reflection). After establishing that usul al-fiqh is annexed to usul al-din in requiring certain knowledge, Sanusi contrasts this with the branches (furu') where ignorance is excused. He then critiques al-Sharif's view that nazar is merely a subsidiary obligation, arguing instead that it is an original obligation because iman depends on it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the annexation claim
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi begins by stating that usul al-fiqh is annexed to usul al-din, and that knowledge is required, not mere effort.
The annexation extends even to takfir for ignorance.
2
Contrast the treatment of furu' with usul
Find the part about furu' where the Lawgiver pardons ignorance. List the examples given: eating impure food, impure water, judge with false witnesses. Notice the reasoning: hardship in repeated examination.
This contrast shows the severity of usul compared to furu'.
3
Analyze the critique of al-Sharif and the conclusion on nazar
Read the author's refutation of al-Sharif. Understand why considering nazar as a subsidiary obligation is unsound. Grasp the final point: nazar is a means to iman, thus it is an original obligation like iman itself.
The key is that whatever iman cannot be attained without is itself original (wajib asli).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال العلماء : ويُلحَقُ بأصول الدين أصول الفقه ; - والله تعالى أعلمُ - : في أنَّ المطلوب فيها العلم ، ولا يكفي فيها غير؛ لأنها تُلحَقُ بأصول الدين في تكفيرِ مَنْ لم يعلمها . قالَ : ( وأما الفروع فقد عفا صاحب الشرع عن ذلكَ ، فَمَنْ بذل جهده في الفروع فأخطأ فله أجرٌ ، وإن أصابَ فَلَهُ أَجْرانِ ، ومَنْ أكل طعاماً نجساً يظنُّه طاهراً فهذا جهل يُعفى عنه ; لما في تكرر الفحص ذلكَ مِنَ المشقَّةِ ، وكذلك المياه النجسة والأشربة النجسة لا حرج على الجاهل بها ، وكذا الحاكم يقضي بشهودِ الزورِ معَ جهلِهِ بحالهم لا إثم عليهِ في ذلكَ ; لتعذُّرِ الاحترازِ مِنْ ذلكَ عليهِ ) انتهى . وأما ما اقتصر عليهِ الشريف مِنَ المعصية فقط في حق المتمكِّنِ مِنَ النظر .. فدعوى منه لا دليل عليها ، وما يمكنُ أنْ يُستَدَلَّ لَهُ بأنَّ النظر واجب فرعيٌّ كالصلاة ونحوها .. فمصادرة لا تصح ، بل الحقُّ : أنه وسيلة إلى الإيمان الذي هو المعرفة ، أو حديث النفس التابع لها ، وما لا يحصلُ الإيمانُ إلا به فهو واجبٌ أصلي كالإيمان
The scholars said: And usul al-fiqh is annexed to usul al-din – and Allah the Exalted knows best – in that knowledge is required in it, and [mere effort] is not sufficient in it; because it is annexed to usul al-din in declaring as disbeliever the one who does not know it. He said: 'As for the branches (furu'), the Lawgiver has pardoned concerning that. So whoever exerts his effort in the branches and errs, he has one reward; and if he is correct, he has two rewards. And whoever eats impure food thinking it pure, this is ignorance that is excused, because of the hardship in repeated examination of that. Likewise, impure waters and impure drinks – no blame upon the ignorant person regarding them. Similarly, the judge who judges based on false witnesses while ignorant of their condition – there is no sin upon him in that, because of the impossibility of guarding against that for him.' End. As for what al-Sharif confined to sin only regarding the one capable of reflection (nazar)... it is a claim from him with no evidence for it. And what could be used as evidence for him – that nazar is a subsidiary obligation like prayer and so on – is an unsound petitio principii. Rather, the truth is that it is a means to iman, which is knowledge (ma'rifa), or the inner speech (hadith al-nafs) that follows it. And whatever iman is not attained except through it is an original obligation like iman.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage begins with a statement from the scholars: usul al-fiqh is annexed to usul al-din in that knowledge is required, and mere effort without attaining knowledge is insufficient; this annexation extends even to declaring the one who does not know these matters a disbeliever (takfir). The author then quotes al-Qarafi again, who distinguishes the branches (furu') from usul: in furu', the Lawgiver has pardoned ignorance. For example, if someone exerts ijtihad in furu' and errs, he gets one reward; if correct, two. If someone eats impure food thinking it pure, he is excused due to the hardship of constant checking. Similarly, ignorance of impure water and drinks is excused, and a judge who unknowingly accepts false witnesses bears no sin. This contrasts sharply with usul. Then the author critiques al-Sharif, who claimed that only sin (not disbelief) applies to one capable of nazar (reflection) who does not perform it. The author says this is a claim without evidence. If al-Sharif argued that nazar is a subsidiary obligation (fardh far'i) like prayer, that is a petitio principii (مصادرة). The correct view is that nazar is a means (wasila) to iman, which is knowledge (ma'rifa) or the inner speech (hadith al-nafs) that follows knowledge. Whatever iman cannot be attained without is itself an original obligation (wajib asli), just like iman.

Key Terms
أصول الفقه
الفروع
النظر
الإيمان
المعرفة
واجب أصلي
واجب فرعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nazar is a subsidiary obligation, so its neglect only incurs sin, not disbelief.
Correction: Nazar is an original obligation because it is the means to iman; without it, iman cannot be attained, and thus its neglect is like neglecting iman itself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct ruling on nazar (reflection) for one capable of performing it?
A) It is a subsidiary obligation, with sin only if neglected.
B) It is an original obligation, as it is a means to iman.
C) It is recommended but not obligatory.
D) It is obligatory only for scholars.

Key Takeaway
Nazar is an original obligation because it is the means to iman, and usul al-fiqh is annexed to usul al-din in requiring certain knowledge, unlike the branches where ignorance is excused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that nazar (reflection) is an original obligation because it is the means to iman. Have you truly engaged in profound reflection about the foundational truths of your faith, or do you rely on inherited belief? What specific step will you take this week to practice nazar with sincerity?
?
The passage warns that ignorance in usul matters can lead to takfir. Consider your own knowledge of the fundamentals of iman. Is there any area where you have been complacent, assuming that good intentions excuse lack of knowledge? Will you commit to studying the essentials of usul al-din and usul al-fiqh until you have certainty?
?
Al-Qarafi mentions that in furu', errors are pardoned due to hardship, but in usul, no such excuse applies. Have you ever treated a core belief as if it were a minor branch, excusing yourself from deep understanding? Are you ready to take the full weight of responsibility for your iman and its foundations?

Why the Companions Did Not Engage in Kalam
Author explains why Companions did not engage in kalam and later developments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main reasons the Companions did not engage in kalam and how later scholars revived it when circumstances changed.

Core Idea
The Companions had no need for kalam because truth was clear and heresies absent.

This passage answers a key question: Why did the early Muslim community not engage in speculative theology? The author explains that the Companions found the Qur'an sufficient and faced no doctrinal threats. Their later silence gave way to the emergence of kalam when understanding declined and heresies appeared.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's argument.
Notice the opening word 'نعم' – this is answering an implied question.
2
Identify the implied question and response
The author is answering: 'Why didn't the Companions speak about kalam?' He responds with two reasons. Find them in the text.
The reasons begin with 'لما كانت عقائد التوحيد' and 'ولم تكن في أزمنتهم بدع'.
3
Understand the two reasons
First, the doctrines of tawhid do not admit of much disagreement. Second, there were no innovations in their time. Thus, they relied on the clear Qur'an.
The Companions were content with the proofs of the Qur'an.
4
See the later development
Later, when people became ignorant of Arabic and the prophetic era faded, Ahl al-Sunnah wrote kalam books to clarify and added refutations of innovations.
The hadith about the splitting of Israelites is cited as prophecy.
5
Reflect on the misconception
The passage shows that the Companions' silence was deliberate due to lack of need, not inability. Consider how this corrects a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم (۲) (١) لما كانت عقائد التوحيد لا تقبل من الخلاف بين أهل الحق ما تقبله الفروع ، ولم تكن في أزمنتهم بدع يحتاجون إلى ردها .. لم يتكلموا رضي الله عنهم في هذا العلم ما تكلموا في الفروع ؛ اكتفاء منهم بأدلة القرآن الواضحة لكل موفق ، وإياها اعتمد أهل السنة رضي الله تعالى عنهم في كتبهم الكلامية ، وزادوها إيضاحاً وشرحاً لما عجز الناس عن فهمها ؛ من أجل استيلاء الجهل بعلم اللسان عليهم ، وغَيبة شموس النبوة وأنجمها عنهم. وضم إلى ذلك بعضهم ذكر حجج الرد على شبه المبتدعة التي دبت الناس بعد موتهم دبيب النمل ، وقد أخبر بها النبي صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ في قوله : ( إن بني إسرائيل افترقوا على اثنتين وسبعين فرقة (۱)
Yes (2)(1): Because the doctrines of tawhid do not admit of disagreement among the people of truth as the branches (furuʿ) admit, and there were not in their times innovations that needed refutation... They (may Allah be pleased with them) did not speak about this science as they spoke about the branches; contenting themselves with the proofs of the Qur'an that are clear for every person who is granted success, and it is these that the people of the Sunnah (may Allah be pleased with them) relied upon in their kalam books, and they increased them in clarification and explanation when people became unable to understand them, due to the prevalence of ignorance of the science of language among them, and the disappearance of the suns of prophethood and its stars from them. And some of them added to that the mention of the arguments of refutation against the doubts of the innovators that crept among the people after their deaths like the creeping of ants, and the Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) informed about them in his saying: 'Indeed, the Children of Israel split into seventy-two sects (1).'
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage explains why the Companions did not engage in kalam. The author begins with 'نعم' (Yes), answering an implied question: 'Why didn't the Companions speak about kalam?' He gives two reasons: First, the doctrines of tawhid do not admit of the same level of disagreement as the furuʿ (legal branches) do; thus there was no need for extensive discussion. Second, in their time there were no innovations (bidaʿ) that required refutation. Consequently, the Companions did not speak about this science (i.e., kalam) as they spoke about furuʿ; they were content with the clear proofs of the Qur'an. Later, when people became unable to understand those proofs due to ignorance of Arabic language and the disappearance of the prophetic era, the Ahl al-Sunnah wrote kalam books to clarify and explain. Some of them also added refutations against the doubts of the innovators that spread after the Companions' time, as the Prophet had foretold the splitting of the Israelites into seventy-two sects, implying similar splits in his own community.

أهل السنة
كتب كلامية
علم اللسان
المبتدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Companions' silence on kalam implies they were unaware of its importance or incapable of engaging in it.
Correction: The passage shows their silence was deliberate due to lack of need: the doctrines were clear and there were no heresies. They were content with the Qur'an's clear proofs.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why did the Companions not speak about kalam (this science)?
A) They were not knowledgeable in it.
B) They were forbidden from it.
C) There were no innovations and the Quran was clear enough.
D) They were too busy with political matters.

Key Takeaway
The Companions did not engage in kalam because the core doctrines were clear and heresies absent; later scholars revived it to defend the faith when understanding declined and heresies appeared.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a doubt about your faith, do you instinctively turn to the clear proofs of the Quran, or do you rely on intellectual arguments and scholars first? Which approach did the Companions model, and what does your current habit reveal about your relationship with Allah's Book?
?
The passage links ignorance of the Arabic language to a loss of understanding of divine proofs. How much have you invested in learning Arabic to access the Quran directly? If you rely entirely on translations, are you content with that, or does it stir a desire to connect with Allah's speech as it was revealed?
?
The Prophet foretold that his community would split into sects. In your daily life, what subtle influences—social media, friends, popular speakers—might be pulling you away from the clear path of Ahl al-Sunnah? What concrete step will you take today to insulate yourself from those influences and anchor yourself in the Quran and the way of the Companions?

Why the Sahaba Did Not Speak Much on Kalam
Sanusi explains that the Sahaba did not need to discuss kalam due to lack of innovations and clear Quranic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical and doctrinal reasons why the Sahaba did not extensively engage in kalam, and how later scholars built upon Quranic proofs when needed.

Core Idea
The Sahaba didn't need kalam because they had clear Quranic proofs and no innovations.

This passage from Sanusi addresses a common question: why didn't the Sahaba produce many theological works? Some might assume that kalam is inherently blameworthy. Sanusi explains that the reason was practical—there was simply no need. The Quranic proofs were clear, and no innovations had yet appeared. Only later, when linguistic understanding declined and the prophetic era ended, did scholars expand these proofs into formal kalam works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument sink in.
Notice the opening word 'نعم' which confirms a previous point.
2
Identify the main reason for limited kalam
The passage gives two reasons: beliefs of tawhid do not admit of the same kind of disagreement as furu', and there were no innovations needing refutation. This is why the Sahaba did not speak much on kalam.
3
Contrast with the later need for kalam
Later Ahl al-Sunnah wrote theological books because people lost the ability to understand the Quran directly due to ignorance of Arabic linguistics and the absence of the prophetic presence. They expanded and clarified the Quranic proofs.
This shows that later kalam is a necessary elaboration, not an innovation.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that the Sahaba avoided kalam because it is blameworthy. This passage shows it was simply unnecessary at the time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم لمَّا كانَتْ عقائدُ التوحيدِ لا تَقبَلُ مِنَ الخلافِ بينَ أهل الحقِّ ما تقبله الفروع ، ولم تكن في أزمنتهم بدع يحتاجون إلى ردها .. لم يتكلموا رضي الله عنهم في هذا العلم ما تكلموا في الفروع ؛ اكتفاء منهم بأدلَّةِ القرآنِ الواضحة لكلِّ مُوفَّقٍ ، وإيَّاها اعتمد أهلُ السنَّةِ رضي الله تعالى عنهم في كتبهم الكلاميَّةِ ، وزادوها إيضاحاً وشرحاً لمَّا عجز الناسُ عن فهمها ؛ مِنْ أجل استيلاء الجهل بعلم اللسانِ عليهم ، وغَيبة شموس النبوَّةِ وأنجمها عنهم
Indeed, since the beliefs of tawhid do not admit of disagreement among Ahl al-Haqq as the branches (furu') admit, and there were no innovations in their times that required refutation, they (may Allah be pleased with them) did not speak about this science as they spoke about the branches; contenting themselves with the clear proofs of the Quran for every person who is granted success. And Ahl al-Sunnah (may Allah be pleased with them) relied upon those proofs in their theological books, and increased them in clarification and explanation when people became unable to understand them, due to the prevalence of ignorance of the science of language (ilm al-lisan) among them, and the absence of the suns of prophethood and its stars from them.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage explains the reason for the limited production of theological works by the Sahaba. It states that the beliefs of tawhid (divine unity) do not admit of the same kind of disagreement among the people of truth as do the subsidiary branches of law (furu'). Additionally, during their time there were no innovations (bida') that required refutation. Therefore, they did not engage in theological discourse as extensively as they did in jurisprudence. They sufficed with the clear proofs of the Quran. Later, when people lost the ability to understand the Quran due to ignorance of Arabic linguistics (ilm al-lisan) and the absence of the prophetic presence, Ahl al-Sunnah wrote theological works to clarify and expand upon those Quranic proofs. This demonstrates that later kalam works are not novel innovations but necessary elaborations.

الفروع
بدع
أدلة القرآن

شموس النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sahaba avoided kalam because it is blameworthy or forbidden.
Correction: The passage shows they avoided it only because there was no need—clear Quranic proofs and no innovations—not because kalam is inherently evil.

Checkpoint
Why did the Sahaba not produce many works on ilm al-tawhid?
A) Because it is forbidden.
B) Because they relied on the clear proofs of the Quran and there were no innovations.
C) Because they were not knowledgeable in theology.
D) Because they only focused on fiqh.

Key Takeaway
The Sahaba's limited engagement in kalam was due to sufficiency of Quranic proofs and absence of innovations, not because kalam is forbidden.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Sahaba were content with the clear proofs of the Quran, why do I often neglect the Quran and turn to secondary sources first? Am I truly content with Allah's book as sufficient guidance, or do I rely on human explanations out of laziness or doubt?
?
When I face confusion about my faith, do I first open the Quran and seek clarity from its verses, or do I immediately search for a scholar's opinion? What does this reveal about my trust in the Quran's clarity?
?
Sanusi notes that later scholars had to elaborate because ignorance of Arabic spread. Have I made any serious effort to learn Arabic so I can access the Quran directly, or have I been complacent in relying on translations? What am I doing to remedy that ignorance?

The Condemnation of Disparaging Tawhid Study
Sanusi condemns those who despise theological reflection as human devils who obstruct Allah's path.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi considers condemnation of theological reflection (nazar) in tawhid as a satanic act that leads to misguidance.

Core Idea
To condemn theological reflection is to become a human devil.

Sanusi delivers a sharp condemnation of those who discourage the study of tawhid. He identifies them as human devils who block the path to Allah. This passage exposes the grave spiritual danger of rejecting rational theological inquiry, linking it directly to the desires of Iblis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the strong language: 'shaytan ins' (human devil) and 'qati' li tariq Allah' (blocker of Allah's path).
2
Identify the accusations
List the three ways Sanusi describes those who condemn nazar: human devil, blocker of Allah's path, one who desires to keep the ignorant in confusion.
Also note the consequences: innovation (bida'), deviation (ilhad), and literal interpretation despite consensus on ta'wil.
3
Connect to Iblis's desire
Understand that Sanusi says this attitude is exactly the desire of Iblis. Then ask: what does it mean to become his companion and deputy?
This shows the high stakes of rejecting theological reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة : فلا يشكُ عاقل أنَّ مَنْ يذمُّ النظر في علم التوحيد فهو شيطان إنس ، قاطع لطريق الله تعالى ، مريد أن يبقى الجاهل على تخليطات في عقائدِهِ ، وبدع وإلحادٍ في فهم أشياءَ مِنَ الكتاب والسنَّةِ على ظواهرها معَ الإجماع على تأويلها ، وهذه شهوة إبليس بلا شكٍّ ، ومَنْ سعى في تحصيل شهوته فهو صاحبه وخليفته في الفسادِ والعياذ بالله تعالى
In summary: no rational person doubts that whoever condemns reflection (nazar) in the science of tawhid is a human devil, a blocker of the path of Allah Most High, desiring to keep the ignorant upon confusion in his beliefs, and innovations and deviation in understanding some things from the Book and the Sunnah according to their apparent meanings despite the consensus on their interpretation. And this is the desire of Iblis without doubt. And whoever strives to fulfill his desire is his companion and his deputy in corruption. May Allah protect us.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a strong warning against those who disparage the study of theological reflection (nazar) in the science of tawhid. The author states that such a person is a 'human devil' (shaytan ins), because he blocks the path to Allah by preventing people from engaging in necessary theological reflection. His goal is to keep the ignorant in confusion (takhlimat) and to lead them into innovations (bida') and deviation (ilhad) by interpreting the Quran and Sunnah literally in matters where there is consensus on symbolic interpretation (ta'wil). This, the author says, is exactly the desire of Iblis. Anyone who works to fulfill that desire becomes a companion and deputy of Satan in corruption. The passage underscores that studying kalam is essential to avoid misguidance.

Misconception: Some think that criticizing theological reflection is a form of piety, avoiding innovation.
Correction: Sanusi shows that such criticism actually serves Iblis's desire, leading to confusion, innovation, and deviation, and is the act of a human devil.

Checkpoint

A) He becomes a disbeliever.
B) He becomes a companion and deputy of Iblis in corruption.
C) He is excused as ignorant.
D) He is still a Muslim but sinful.

Key Takeaway
Condemning theological reflection in tawhid is a satanic act that makes one a companion of Iblis in corruption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Malik ibn Anas and the Science of Usul al-Din
Sanusi cites al-Zubaydi to show Malik's study of usul al-din.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that early scholars like Imam Malik studied what is essentially kalam under the name 'usul al-din' for defending orthodox belief.

Core Idea
Imam Malik studied usul al-din to refute deviation, showing the early roots of kalam.

This short passage from Sanusi cites a historical report to establish the noble status of ilm al-kalam. By showing that the great Imam Malik dedicated years to studying the science of usul al-din, Sanusi argues that kalam is not a later innovation but was practiced by the earliest authorities. Let’s see what this report tells us and how it connects to the broader defense of Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice that the passage begins with the phrase 'فضل علم الكلام' – 'Virtue of ilm al-kalam' – setting the context for why this report is cited.
The text is a short report from al-Zubaydi's biographical work on grammarians.
2
Identify what Malik studied
The passage states that Malik studied with Ibn Hurmuz for years. What did the scholars consider that knowledge to be? Look for the phrase 'علم أصول الدين' – the science of the principles of religion.
This science later became known as ilm al-kalam.
3
Connect to the purpose of this knowledge
The passage adds that this knowledge is what 'تُرَدُّ به مقالة أهل الزيغ والضلال' – by which the statements of deviation and misguidance are refuted. What does this tell you about the purpose of usul al-din?
It shows that theological study was seen as a defensive tool for preserving correct belief.
4
Address the common misconception
Some people think early imams rejected kalam entirely. How does this report challenge that view? Think about how the science was named and who studied it.
The common misunderstanding is that early scholars avoided theological discussion; this passage shows the opposite under the label usul al-din.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ فضل علم الكلامِ ] وقد ذكرَ الزُّبيدي في « طبقاتِ النحاة » : أنَّ العلم الذي كان يختلف فيه مالك بن أنس إلى ابن هرمز سنين كانوا يرون أنَّهُ علم أصول الدين وما تُرَدُّ به مقالة أهل الزيغ والضلال
[ Virtue of ilm al-kalam ] And al-Zubaydi mentioned in his 'Tabaqat al-Nuhat' that the knowledge which Malik ibn Anas used to frequent Ibn Hurmuz for years, they considered it to be the science of the principles of religion and what refutes the statements of the people of deviation and misguidance.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a short report from al-Zubaydi's biographical work on grammarians, Tabaqat al-Nuhat. It relates that Imam Malik ibn Anas regularly attended the lessons of Ibn Hurmuz for many years. The scholars of that time considered the subject of those lessons to be 'ilm usul al-din (the science of the foundations of religion), which is essentially what later came to be called 'ilm al-kalam. Furthermore, they regarded this knowledge as the means by which the arguments of the people of deviation (zaygh) and misguidance (dalal) are refuted. This demonstrates that early authorities like Malik engaged in theological study and considered it valuable for defending orthodox belief.

Key Terms
فضل علم الكلام
طبقات النحاة
مالك بن أنس
ابن هرمز
أصول الدين
أهل الزيغ والضلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Early imams like Malik were completely opposed to kalam and never studied it.
Correction: This passage shows that under the name 'usul al-din', Malik actively studied the principles of religion to refute deviations, which is essentially the same as kalam.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the scholars consider the knowledge that Malik studied with Ibn Hurmuz to be?
A) Grammar.
B) Fiqh.
C) Usul al-Din.
D) Hadith.

Key Takeaway
Imam Malik studied usul al-din (the basis of kalam) for years in order to refute deviations, demonstrating that early scholars valued and engaged in theological defense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The contemporaries of Malik saw this knowledge as essential to refute 'the people of deviation and misguidance'. In your life, are you confident that you can identify falsehoods that contradict Islamic teachings? If not, how will you acquire that ability?
?
Imam Malik sought out knowledge of usul al-din despite his already great stature. What does your current level of commitment to learning the principles of deen reveal about your priorities? Will you let this lesson move you to action or remain a passive admirer of the scholars?

The Virtue of Kalam According to Malik and the Early Scholars
The author uses historical reports and poetry to demonstrate the early endorsement of kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how early scholars like Imam Malik endorsed the science of kalam and why it was considered essential for defending Islamic doctrine.

Core Idea
Kalam is not an innovation; it was practiced and endorsed by the early imams like Malik.

This lesson examines a passage from the author's work that defends the science of kalam using historical evidence. We will see how Imam Malik studied kalam, wrote about it before al-Ash'ari, and set conditions for debating heretics. The passage also includes poetry praising kalam as the highest science. By engaging with this text, you will appreciate the early roots of kalam and its role in preserving correct belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the names and terms used.
The passage contains a poem in the meter al-khafif.
2
Identify Malik's involvement
Note that Malik studied with Ibn Hurmuz for years, and that the subject was considered 'usul ad-din.' Also note that Malik authored a treatise before al-Ash'ari's birth.
This shows kalam predates al-Ash'ari.
3
Understand Malik's condition for debate
Malik allowed debate only for the 'mustabhir' (well-versed scholar). Why would he restrict it?
This emphasizes the need for expertise.
4
Reflect on the poetry
The first poem declares that all sciences are servants to kalam. The second poem (incomplete) was a response to critics. What message do they convey?
Poetry was a common medium for scholarly praise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ فَضْلُ عِلْمِ الْكَلَامِ ] وَقَدْ ذَكَرَ الزُّبَيْدِيُّ فِي « طَبَقَاتِ النُّحَاةِ » لَهُ أَنَّ الْعِلْمَ الَّذِي كَانَ يَخْتَلِفُ مَالِكٌ فِيهِ لِابْنِ هُرْمُزَ سِنِينَ كَانُوا يَرَوْنَ أَنَّهُ عِلْمُ أُصُولِ الدِّينِ وَمَا تُرَدُّ بِهِ مَقَالَةُ أَهْلِ الزَّيْغِ وَالضَّلَالِ. وَقَدْ قَالَ مَالِكٌ: كَانَ ابْنُ هُرْمُزَ بَصِيرًا بِالْكَلَامِ، وَكَانَ يَرُدُّ عَلَى أَهْلِ الْأَهْوَاءِ. وَقَدْ سُئِلَ مَالِكٌ عَنْ مُنَاظَرَةِ أَهْلِ الْأَهْوَاءِ، فَقَالَ: أَمَّا الْمُسْتَبْحِرُ فَنَعَمْ، وَأَمَّا غَيْرُهُ فَلَا؛ لِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ وَهَنٌ فِي الدِّينِ. وَقَدْ أَلَّفَ مَالِكٌ رَضِيَ اللهُ عَنْهُ فِي هَذَا الْعِلْمِ رِسَالَتَهُ قَبْلَ أَنْ يُولَدَ الشَّيْخُ الْأَشْعَرِيُّ، ثُمَّ إِنَّهُ بَيَّنَ مَنَاهِجَ الْأَوَّلِينَ، وَلَخَّصَ مَوَادَّ الْبَرَاهِينِ، وَلَمْ يُحْدِثْ فِيهِ بَعْدَ السَّلَفِ إِلَّا مُجَرَّدَ الْأَلْقَابِ وَالِاصْطِلَاحَاتِ، وَقَدْ حَدَثَ مِثْلُ ذَلِكَ فِي كُلِّ فَنٍّ مِنَ الْعُلُومِ. وَقَدْ أَنْشَدَ الْأَشْيَاخُ فِي فَضْلِ هَذَا الْعِلْمِ: [ مِنَ الْخَفِيفِ ] أَيُّهَا الْمُعْتَدِي لِتَطْلُبَ عِلْمًا كُلُّ عِلْمٍ عَبْدٌ لِعِلْمِ الْكَلَامِ تَطْلُبُ الْفِقْهَ كَيْ تُصَحِّحَ حُكْمًا ثُمَّ أَغْفَلْتَ مُنْزِلَ الْأَحْكَامِ. وَقِيلَ لِلْقَاضِي ابْنِ الطَّيِّبِ رَحِمَهُ اللهُ: إِنَّ قَوْمًا يَذُمُّونَ عِلْمَ الْكَلَامِ، فَأَنْشَدَ: [ مِنَ الْبَسِيطِ ]
[ The Virtue of the Science of Kalam ] And az-Zubaydi mentioned in his 'Tabaqat an-Nuhat' that the science for which Malik used to frequent Ibn Hurmuz for years was considered by them to be the science of the principles of religion (usul ad-din) and that by which the statement of the people of deviation and misguidance is refuted. And Malik said: Ibn Hurmuz was knowledgeable in kalam, and he used to refute the people of desires. And Malik was asked about debating with the people of desires, and he said: As for the one who is well-versed (mustabhir), yes; as for other than him, no, because that is a weakness in religion. And Malik, may Allah be pleased with him, authored a treatise on this science before Shaykh al-Ash'ari was born. Then he clarified the methodologies of the early ones and summarized the materials of the proofs, and he did not introduce anything after the predecessors except mere terms and conventions, and similar happened in every field of knowledge. And the shaykhs recited in praise of this science: [in the meter al-khafif] 'O one who strives to seek knowledge, every knowledge is a servant to the science of kalam. You seek fiqh to correct a ruling, then you neglected the Revealer of rulings.' And it was said to Qadi Ibn at-Tayyib, may Allah have mercy on him: Indeed, some people disparage the science of kalam. So he recited: [in the meter al-basit] ...
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage collects historical evidence and authoritative endorsements to defend the science of kalam. It begins by citing az-Zubaydi's biographical work to show that Imam Malik studied kalam with Ibn Hurmuz for years, and that this science was understood as the principles of religion (usul al-din) and the tool for refuting heretical groups. Malik then personally testifies to Ibn Hurmuz's expertise in kalam and his active refutation of the people of desires (ahl al-ahwa'). When asked about engaging in debate with such people, Malik gives a conditional answer: only the 'mustabhir'—one who is thoroughly grounded and well-versed in the discipline—may do so; for others, it would weaken their religion. The passage then notes that Malik himself wrote a treatise on this science before the birth of al-Ash'ari, proving that kalam is not an Ash'ari innovation. The author adds that later scholars only introduced new terminology and organizational labels, not new content—a development common to all sciences. Finally, two pieces of poetry are cited: the first, by the shaykhs, declares that all other sciences are servants to kalam, and that seeking fiqh without kalam is to miss the ultimate source of rulings (the Revealer). The second, by Qadi Ibn at-Tayyib, is cut off but clearly intended as a rejoinder to those who disparage kalam.

Key Terms
علم الكلام
طبقات النحاة
أصول الدين
أهل الأهواء
المستبحر
الاصطلاحات
الألقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the science of kalam was introduced by al-Ash'ari and is therefore a later innovation rejected by early Muslims.
Correction: This passage shows that Imam Malik studied kalam with Ibn Hurmuz years before al-Ash'ari was born, and even wrote a treatise on it. Thus, kalam has roots in the early generations.

Checkpoint
What condition did Malik set for debating with the people of desires?
A) Only if the opponent is willing to listen.
B) Only if the debate is private.
C) Only if one is a well-versed scholar (mustabhir).
D) Only if the ruler permits it.

Key Takeaway
The science of kalam has a pedigree going back to the early imams like Malik, who studied it and used it to defend true doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imam Malik only permitted debate for the 'mustabhir' (deeply knowledgeable). Have you ever spoken about a religious matter without being truly 'mustabhir' in that area? What was the result in your heart? Will you commit to seeking depth before speaking from now on?
?
The poem says every knowledge is a servant to kalam, the science of Allah's attributes and His oneness. How much time do you actually spend studying tawhid and the names of Allah compared to fiqh or other worldly knowledge? Does your schedule reflect that priority? What will you change?
?
Malik studied kalam with Ibn Hurmuz for years to refute deviant sects. How many hours have you invested in learning to defend the correct creed against modern doubts? If you were challenged today, would you be able to answer from sound proofs, or would you be a cause of weakness in the religion?

Imam Malik's Endorsement of Kalam and His Treatise
Reports from Imam Malik about kalam and his treatise before Ash'ari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Imam Malik engaged in kalam and authored a treatise, showing that early scholars did not universally reject theological discourse.

Core Idea
Imam Malik practiced kalam and restricted debate to the profoundly learned.

This passage from a classical kalam text presents historical reports about Imam Malik's stance on theological discourse (kalam). It shows that Malik not only engaged in kalam himself but also set a condition for who may debate innovators. The passage also reveals that Malik authored a treatise on kalam before the birth of Ash'ari, indicating that the Ash'ari school built upon earlier foundations. Understanding this corrects the misconception that early imams entirely opposed kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the three distinct reports about Malik: his praise of Ibn Hurmuz, his condition for debating, and his treatise.
The passage is dense with historical references.
2
Identify Malik's key statements
Identify the two statements attributed to Malik: one praising Ibn Hurmuz for his kalam, and the other answering a question about debating people of desires. Note the condition 'mustabhir' (profound).
The word 'mustabhir' means deeply learned.
3
Understand the significance of Malik's treatise
The passage states Malik authored a treatise on kalam before Ash'ari was born. This shows that kalam existed before the Ash'ari school. Later scholars only added terminology, not new content.
This parallels developments in other sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قال مالك : كانَ ابنُ هُرْمُزَ بصيراً بالكلام ، وكان يردُّ على أهلِ الأهواء (1) . وقد سُئِلَ مالكٌ عن مناظرةِ أهل الأهواء ، فقال : أما المستبحرُ فنعم ، وأمَّا غيرُهُ فلا ؛ لأنَّ ذلك وهن في الدين . وقد ألَّفَ مالك رضيَ الله عنه في هذا العلمِ رسالته قبل أنْ يُولَدَ الشيخُ الأشعري (۲) ، ثم إِنَّهُ بَيَّنَ مناهج الأوَّلين ، ولخص مواد البراهين ، ولم يُحدِث فيهِ بعد السلف إلا مجرَّدَ الألقاب والاصطلاحاتِ ، وقد حدث مثل ذلك في كل فن مِنَ العلوم
Malik said: Ibn Hurmuz was insightful in kalam, and he used to refute the people of desires (1). And Malik was asked about debating the people of desires, so he said: As for the one who is profound, then yes; and as for other than him, then no; because that is weakness in the religion. And Malik, may Allah be pleased with him, authored a treatise on this science before Shaykh al-Ash'ari was born (2). Then he clarified the methodologies of the early ones, summarized the materials of proofs, and did not introduce in it after the Salaf except mere titles and terminologies, and the like of that happened in every field of the sciences.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage reports historical evidence of early engagement with kalam. It begins with Malik praising Ibn Hurmuz for his knowledge in kalam and his refutation of the people of desires. Then it narrates Malik's nuanced view on debating innovators: only a deeply learned person may engage, as debate by others weakens religion. Finally, it states that Malik authored a treatise on kalam before Ash'ari, showing that kalam existed before the classical Ash'ari school; later thinkers only added technical terminology, not new content. This parallels developments in other sciences.

?
Ibn Hurmuz was known for refuting people of desires. Do you shy away from defending your creed when you hear misconceptions? How can you follow his example in a way that is both knowledgeable and humble? Commit to one specific action this week.

The Kalam Method: Quranic Foundation
Definition of the kalam method and its Quranic proof, with refutation of al-Jahiz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the kalam method and its grounding in the Quran, as well as the refutation of the claim that it is foreign to Islam.

Core Idea
The kalam method is rooted in the Quran, not imported from Greek philosophy.

This short passage establishes that the science of tawhid is inherently tied to the Quran and Sunnah, then defines the specific method used by kalam scholars (madhhab kalami). It defends this method by giving a Quranic example and refuting the earliest critic, al-Jahiz. As you read, notice how the definition is both technical and practical.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the flow from general principle (disparaging tawhid = denying Quran/Sunnah) to specific definition of kalam method, to example, to refutation.
2
Understand the definition of the kalam method
The passage defines it as 'presenting a proof for the desired conclusion in the manner of the people of kalam.' This means using logical argumentation derived from revelation, not from pure reason.
3
Reflect on the Quranic example
The verse from Al-Anbiya' 21:22 is cited: if multiple gods existed, the heavens and earth would be corrupted. This is an example of the kalam method: using a logical consequence (corruption) to disprove multiple gods, based on a revealed premise.
4
Note the refutation of al-Jahiz
Al-Jahiz denied the kalam method, but Ibn 'Arafa called him a liar, stating most kalam proofs are derived from the Quran. This shows the method is fully Islamic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال بعض العلماء : من قدحَ في علم التوحيد فقد أنكر القرآن والسنة ؛ إذ أدلته مأخوذة منهما . وقد ذكر أهلُ البيان حقيقة المذهب الكلامي فقالوا : هو إيرادُ حُجَّةٍ للمطلوب على طريقة أهل الكلام ، ومثلوا ذلك بقولِهِ تعالى : ( لَوْ كَانَ فيهِمَا ءَالِمَةٌ إِلَّا اللَّهُ لَفَسَدَنَا) [الأنبياء : ٢٢] . قالوا : وأَوَّلُ مَنْ أنكرَ المذهب الكلامي الجاحظُ مِنَ المعتزلة . قال الشيخ ابن عرفة : ( وكذبَ الجاحظ ؛ إذْ أَكثرُ حُجَجِ أَهلِ الكلام مستنبطةٌ مِنَ القرآن العظيم ) .
Some scholars said: Whoever disparages the science of tawhid has denied the Quran and the Sunnah; because its proofs are taken from them. And the people of expression (ahl al-bayan) mentioned the reality of the kalam method (madhhab kalami) and said: It is presenting a proof for the desired conclusion in the manner of the people of kalam. And they exemplified that by the saying of the Exalted: 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would both have been corrupted' [Quran 21:22]. They said: The first to deny the kalam method was al-Jahiz from the Mu'tazila. Shaykh Ibn 'Arafa said: 'Al-Jahiz lied; indeed most of the proofs of the people of kalam are derived from the Great Quran.'
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage defines the kalam method and defends it. It begins by asserting that criticizing tawhid (theology) is equivalent to rejecting Quran and Sunnah because its proofs come from them. Then it defines the 'kalam method' (madhhab kalami) as presenting a proof for a claim in the style of the kalam scholars, and gives the Quranic example from Surah al-Anbiya' 22 (the argument from the impossibility of multiple gods). It then reports that al-Jahiz was the first to reject this method, but Ibn 'Arafa refutes him, stating that most kalam proofs are derived from the Quran.

Key Terms
المذهب الكلامي
الحجة
الجاحظ
ابن عرفة
الاستنباط
علم التوحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The kalam method is borrowed from Greek philosophy and has no basis in Islam.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that the kalam method is rooted in the Quran, citing a Quranic example (Al-Anbiya' 21:22) and noting that most kalam proofs are derived from the Quran. Ibn 'Arafa explicitly refutes al-Jahiz's denial.

Checkpoint

D) Al-Fatiha 1:1

Key Takeaway
The kalam method is a Quranic method of presenting proofs for theological claims, and its denial by al-Jahiz was refuted by Ibn 'Arafa.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical argument about Allah's attributes, do you instinctively feel it is 'too rational' or 'un-Islamic'? Will you now let Ibn 'Arafa's rebuke of al-Jahiz silence that hesitation?
?
The verse 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would both have been corrupted' is a proof you have likely heard many times. Have you ever let it settle in your heart so that your certainty in tawhid becomes unshakable? If not, what is stopping you from deeply pondering it today?
?
Al-Jahiz denied the kalam method because he thought it was foreign. Are there any aspects of Islamic theology you have dismissed as 'philosophy' without checking whether they are rooted in the Quran? Will you commit to investigating the Quranic basis before claiming something is bid'ah?
