Critique of Neglecting Theology
A scholar criticizes those who only study worldly sciences while ignoring fundamentals of religion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique of those who focus solely on worldly sciences while neglecting the fundamentals of theology, as illustrated by Shaykh 'Abd al-Jalil's vivid depiction of their ignorance and arrogance.

Core Idea
Worldly knowledge without theology leaves one intellectually impotent and spiritually bankrupt.

Shaykh 'Abd al-Jalil presents a powerful critique of people who dedicate themselves exclusively to worldly sciences such as grammar and arithmetic, hoping to gain material benefit and prestige. He shows that when faced with essential theological questions, they are reduced to silence and disgrace. This passage serves as a warning about the consequences of neglecting the core obligations of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the author's vivid language and critique.
Pay attention to the strong imagery used.
2
Identify the two groups
Notice the contrast between those who pursue worldly sciences for gain and the one who is challenged with theological questions.
3
Understand the questions asked
The passage lists three key theological questions: what is the first obligation, when does obligation begin, and what is the evidence for one's beliefs?
These questions are fundamental to Islamic theology.
4
Reflect on the imagery
The author uses striking comparisons (silent as a fish, stuck as a bird) to emphasize the scholar's helplessness.
5
Consider the lesson
Think about the importance of prioritizing theology over worldly pursuits and the spiritual consequences of neglect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو القاسم عبد الجليل في ( عقيدته (۱) : ( اعلم : أنَّ كثيراً من الناس لا يشتغلون إلا بعلم النحو والحساب ، وإصلاح اللفظ وإجادة الكتاب ؛ لما يرجونَ مِنْ كون ذلك لهم بضاعةً وحرفةً يُعوّلونَ عليها وصناعةً ، فتراهم يَجُرُّونَ أذيالهم مِنْ خُيلائِهم ، ويذهبونَ متعاظمينَ في غُلَوائِهم ، يلحظونَ الناسَ بعين الاحتقار ، ويرمقونهم بمقلة الاستصغار ، فإذا قيلَ لهُ : ما أَوَّلُ الواجباتِ ؟ ومتى يجب التكليفُ على الإنسانِ ؟ وما الدليلُ على صحة ما إليه تذهبُ ، وفسادِ ما عنه ترغب ؟ .. بقي أسكتَ مِنْ سمكة ، وأشدَّ وُحُولاً مِنْ طائرٍ في شبكة (٢) ، وصغرَ في همَّتِهِ ما كانَ كبيراً ، وذلَّ في نفسِهِ ما كانَ عزيزاً خطيراً ، ولَبسَ ثوبَ استكانة ، وتسربل بسربال مهانة ، فيا لها مِنْ مصيبة ما أعظمها عليهِ ! وداهية ما أكبرها لديهِ ! ) انتهى (۱) قال العلامة المؤرخ ابن الأبار البلنسي في التكملة لكتاب الصلة » : ( عبد الجليل بن أبي بكر الربعي القروي ، يعرف بالديباجي وبابن الصابوني ، ويكنى أبا القاسم ، يروي عن أبي عمران الفاسي ، وأبي القاسم الخرقي وأبي عبد الله الأزدي صاحب القاضي أبي بكر بن الطيب ، وكان عالماً بالأصول ، مدرساً لها ، وله فيها تصانيف ، منها كتاب « المستوعب في أصول الفقه » ، ومنها كتاب « الانتصار » ، اختصره من كتاب « الانتصار » لأبي بكر بن الطيب ، وله ( رسالة في الاعتقادات » ) ، فهو من أعيان النصف الثاني من القرن الخامس (۲) قوله : ( وحولاً ) من الوَحَل ؛ وهو الطين الرقيق ، يقال : وَحِلَت الدابة في الطين ؛ وقعت فيه فلم تستطع عنه قياماً.
Shaykh Abu al-Qasim 'Abd al-Jalil said in his 'Creed': 'Know that many people occupy themselves only with the science of grammar and arithmetic, correcting pronunciation and perfecting writing, because they hope that this will be for them a commodity and a craft to rely upon and a trade. So you see them dragging their tails in arrogance, and walking in self-conceit in their excess, glancing at people with contempt, and looking at them with disdain. If it is said to him: "What is the first of the obligations? When does obligation become incumbent upon a person? What is the evidence for the correctness of what you hold and the corruption of what you reject?" ... he remains more silent than a fish, and more stuck than a bird in a net (2). And what was great in his aspiration becomes small, and what was noble and valuable in his own eyes becomes low. He puts on the garment of humility and cloaks himself in the cloak of disgrace. What a calamity, how great it is upon him! What a disaster, how great it is before him!' End. (1) The historian scholar Ibn al-Abbar al-Balansi said in the supplement to the book 'al-Sila': 'Abd al-Jalil ibn Abi Bakr al-Rib'i al-Qarawi, known as al-Dibaji and Ibn al-Sabuni, and his kunya is Abu al-Qasim. He narrated from Abu 'Imran al-Fasi, Abu al-Qasim al-Kharqi, and Abu 'Abd Allah al-Azdi, companion of Qadi Abu Bakr ibn al-Tayyib. He was a scholar of fundamentals, a teacher of it, and he has works in it, including the book "al-Mustaw'ab fi Usul al-Fiqh", and the book "al-Intisar", which he abridged from the book "al-Intisar" by Abu Bakr ibn al-Tayyib, and he has "A Treatise on Beliefs". He is among the notables of the second half of the fifth century.' (2) His saying: 'more stuck' (wuhulan) is from al-wahal, which is thin mud. It is said: The beast became stuck (wahilat) in the mud: it fell into it and could not get up.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a critique of those who devote themselves solely to worldly sciences like grammar and arithmetic while neglecting the fundamentals of religion. The author quotes Shaykh Abu al-Qasim 'Abd al-Jalil, who describes such people as arrogant and contemptuous of others. They are unable to answer basic theological questions: the first obligation, when obligation begins, and evidence for their beliefs. They are humbled and silent. The author then provides a biography of the Shaykh and a linguistic note on the word 'wuhulan' (stuck).

Key Terms
علم النحو
علم الحساب
أول الواجبات
التكليف
الاستكانة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mastering worldly sciences like grammar and arithmetic is sufficient for religious practice and honor.
Correction: Without theology, one cannot answer fundamental questions about the first obligation, the beginning of obligation, or the evidence for beliefs, leading to humiliation.

Checkpoint
What questions does the scholar say stump those who neglect theology?
A) What is grammar and arithmetic?
B) What is the first of the obligations, when does obligation begin, and what is the evidence for one's beliefs?
C) How to perfect writing?
D) What is the meaning of fear?

Key Takeaway
Neglecting theology in favor of worldly sciences leads to spiritual and intellectual humiliation, as one is unable to answer fundamental questions of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If someone asked you right now, 'What is the first obligation upon you as a Muslim and what is your evidence?' could you answer? If not, how would you feel standing in the place of the scholar's critique?
?
In your pursuit of education or career, have you ever felt that your knowledge of worldly matters makes you superior? How does this passage reframe that arrogance?
?
The passage says such a person 'drags their tails in arrogance' but becomes 'more silent than a fish' when questioned. Have you ever been humbled by realizing your ignorance of the most important matters? What will you do to ensure you are not caught in such a state?

Critique of Those Who Neglect Theology
Abd al-Jalil criticizes those who neglect theology for worldly sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique of those who prioritize worldly sciences over theology and the importance of knowing foundational religious obligations.

Core Idea
Theology is the first obligation; neglecting it leads to disgrace.

In this passage, Sheikh Abd al-Jalil delivers a powerful critique of people who devote themselves solely to worldly sciences like grammar and arithmetic while neglecting theology. He describes their arrogance and then their utter silence when asked basic religious questions, using vivid similes to underscore the calamity of such neglect. This lesson will help you see why theology must be prioritized.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's vivid imagery come through.
Notice the contrast between worldly pursuits and theological ignorance.
2
Identify the critique
The sheikh criticizes those who only study grammar and arithmetic for worldly gain. They become arrogant.
3
Reflect on the similes
Consider the similes: 'more silent than a fish', 'more stuck in mud than a bird in a net'. What do they convey?
These similes highlight complete helplessness.
4
Connect to your own studies
Reflect on why theology (ilm al-kalam) is considered the first obligation. How does this passage motivate its study?
This passage serves as a warning against neglecting religious knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو القاسم عبد الجليل في ( عقيدته (۱) : ( اعلم : أنَّ كثيراً من الناس لا يشتغلون إلا بعلم النحو والحساب ، وإصلاح اللفظ وإجادة الكتاب ؛ لما يرجونَ مِنْ كون ذلك لهم بضاعةً وحرفةً يُعوّلونَ عليها وصناعةً ، فتراهم يَجُرُّونَ أذيالهم مِنْ خُيلائِهم ، ويذهبونَ متعاظمينَ في غُلَوائِهم ، يلحظونَ الناسَ بعين الاحتقار ، ويرمقونهم بمقلة الاستصغار ، فإذا قيلَ لهُ : ما أَوَّلُ الواجباتِ ؟ ومتى يجب التكليفُ على الإنسانِ ؟ وما الدليلُ على صحة ما إليه تذهبُ ، وفسادِ ما عنه ترغب ؟ .. بقي أسكتَ مِنْ سمكة ، وأشدَّ وُحُولاً مِنْ طائرٍ في شبكة (٢) ، وصغرَ في همَّتِهِ ما كانَ كبيراً ، وذلَّ في نفسِهِ ما كانَ عزيزاً خطيراً ، ولَبسَ ثوبَ استكانة ، وتسربل بسربال مهانة ، فيا لها مِنْ مصيبة ما أعظمها عليهِ ! وداهية ما أكبرها لديهِ ! ) انتهى
Shaykh Abu al-Qasim 'Abd al-Jalil said in his 'Aqida (1): (Know that many people do not occupy themselves except with the science of grammar and arithmetic, and correcting pronunciation and perfecting handwriting, because they hope that this will be for them a commodity and a trade to rely upon and a craft. So you see them dragging their tails out of arrogance, and going about puffed up in their excesses, looking at people with an eye of contempt, and glancing at them with a pupil of belittlement. When it is said to him: What is the first of obligations? And when does obligation become due upon a person? And what is the proof of the correctness of what you hold and the corruption of what you reject? ... he remains more silent than a fish, and more stuck in mud than a bird in a net, and what was great becomes small in his resolve, and what was precious and weighty becomes low in his estimation, and he wears the garment of humility, and clothes himself in a robe of disgrace. Oh what a calamity, how great it is upon him! And what a disaster, how immense it is for him!) End.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is an admonition. Sheikh 'Abd al-Jalil criticizes people who exclusively pursue worldly sciences like grammar (النحو) and arithmetic (الحساب), and the mastery of correct pronunciation and beautiful handwriting. They do this for material gain and social status, and they become arrogant, looking down on others. However, when they are asked fundamental religious questions such as 'What is the first obligation upon a person?', 'When does religious accountability begin?', and 'What is the evidence for what you believe and against what you reject?', they are completely unable to answer. The Sheikh uses vivid similes: they are more silent than a fish, more stuck in mud than a bird in a net. Their pride collapses into humility and disgrace. He concludes by lamenting this as a great calamity and disaster for them. The point is to show the necessity of studying theology (ilm al-kalam) over purely worldly learning.

Key Terms
علم النحو
علم الحساب
أول الواجبات
التكليف
الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Learning grammar and calligraphy is sufficient for religious knowledge.
Correction: Religious knowledge, starting with the first obligations, is essential and cannot be replaced by worldly sciences.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what question are these arrogant scholars unable to answer?
A) What is the capital of Syria?
B) What is the first of obligations?
C) How do you conjugate a verb?
D) What is the population of Mecca?

Key Takeaway
Neglecting theology while mastering worldly skills leads to spiritual failure and disgrace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a skill or worldly knowledge you are proud of. If someone asked you right now, 'What is the first obligation upon you? And what is your proof?', would you fall silent? What does that say about your priorities?
?
When have you looked down on someone because they lacked your worldly skills, while you yourself were spiritually ignorant? How does this passage expose the hypocrisy of that attitude?
?
What is one specific theological question you cannot answer today but feel you should? What step will you take this week to learn it, so you are not like the fish silent in the net?

The Virtue of Proof and the Vice of Taqlid
Ibn Rahhal praises knowledge of evidence and sound reasoning, and condemns blind imitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of proofs and sound reflection is considered the axis of religion, and why blind imitation (taqlid) without proof is condemned in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Knowledge of evidence and sound reasoning is the foundation of true religion, while blind imitation is condemned.

Ibn Rahhal, in his commentary on al-Barawi, opens with a powerful commendation of theological reasoning. He sets the stage for the entire study of kalam by declaring that knowledge of proofs and sound reflection is the very axis of religion. This passage not only praises the path of evidence but also warns against abandoning it for the ease of blind imitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the strong language: 'axis of religion', 'worship of prophets', 'vice of taqlid'.
Pay attention to the transition from praise to condemnation.
2
Identify the two main themes
The passage has two clear parts: first, the praise of knowledge of proofs; second, the condemnation of taqlid. Find where the shift occurs.
The shift happens after 'فلما انقرض الفحول'.
3
Connect to the Quranic proof
Ibn Rahhal supports his condemnation by quoting Quran 43:23. Reflect on why this verse is effective evidence.
The verse shows that following ancestors without proof is blameworthy.
4
Ask yourself the significance
Why is taqlid without proof considered a vice? What does this imply for a student of theology?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالَ ابنُ رحال في شرح البروي (۱) في فضل معرفة الأدلَّةِ وحسن النظر : ( اعلم : أَنَّهُ قطب الدينِ (۲) ، وعبادة الأنبياء والمرسلين ، والأئمَّةِ الراشدين ، وسائرِ علماء المسلمين ، عليها درج السلفُ ، وتبعهم على ذلكَ الخلفُ ، فلما انقرضَ الفحولُ وهلكَتِ الصدور ، وخلفَ مِنْ بعدهم أخلافُ استوعروا طريقهم واستصعبوا سبيلهم ، وأناخوا بفناء الراحاتِ وركبوا مراكب الجهالات . . استوطنوا رذيلة التقليد ؛ وهي مُناخ لكلِّ غبي بليد ، وقد ذم الله قوماً قلدوا آباءهم بغير حُجَّةٍ ، قالوا : ( إِنَّا وَجَدْنَا ءَابَاءَنَا عَلَى أُمَّةٍ وَإِنَّا عَلَى وَاثَرِهِم مُّقْتَدُونَ) [الزخرف : ٢٣) ( انتهى
And Ibn Rahhal said in his commentary on al-Barawi (1) concerning the virtue of knowledge of proofs and sound reflection: (Know: that it is the axis of religion (2), and the worship of the prophets and messengers, and the rightly-guided imams, and all the Muslim scholars. Upon it the predecessors traversed, and the successors followed them in that. When the powerful ones passed away and the leaders died, and there came after them successors who found their path rugged and their way difficult, and they alighted in the courtyard of comforts and rode the mounts of ignorances ... they settled into the vice of taqlid (blind imitation); and it is a stopping place for every stupid, dull person. And God has condemned people who imitated their fathers without proof: they said: 'Indeed, we found our fathers upon a religion, and we are, upon their footsteps, following.' [Zukhruf 43:23]) End.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a commendation of theological reasoning. Ibn Rahhal, in his commentary, explains that knowledge of proofs (معرفة الأدلة) and sound reflection (حسن النظر) is the axis of religion (قطب الدين) and the practice of prophets and the righteous. He then describes how later generations abandoned this path, finding it difficult, and instead took to ease and ignorance. They adopted the vice of taqlid (blind imitation), which he calls a resting place for every stupid and dull person. He supports this with the Quranic verse (43:23) where disbelievers excuse their shirk by saying they follow their fathers. The passage thus establishes the necessity of using evidence and reason in religious matters and condemns uncritical following of tradition without proof.

Key Terms
معرفة الأدلة
حسن النظر
قطب الدين
التقليد
الحجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taqlid (blind imitation) is always acceptable in religious matters.
Correction: The passage shows that taqlid without proof is a vice condemned by God. True religious practice requires knowledge of proofs and sound reflection.

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Rahhal say is the axis of religion (قطب الدين)?
A) Fasting
B) Knowledge of proofs and sound reflection
C) Pilgrimage
D) Wealth

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of proofs and sound reflection is the axis of religion, and blind imitation without evidence is a condemned vice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are not the first to find the path of evidence difficult. The passage describes people who turned to comfort and ignorance instead. When you face a complex theological question, do you choose the easy answer from those around you, or do you persevere in learning the proofs yourself? Which specific belief in your life are you holding onto without having examined its evidence?
?
The prophets and the righteous worshipped Allah through knowledge of proofs and sound reflection. As you stand in prayer today, ask yourself: Is my worship built on a foundation of understanding and conviction, or am I merely going through motions inherited from my family? Make a commitment this week to learn the proof for one act of worship you practice.
?
God condemns those who say, 'We found our fathers upon a religion, and we follow in their footsteps.' Identify one religious practice or belief that you accept simply because it is the tradition of your community, without having personally verified its evidence. Are you willing to put it to the test of the Quran and sound reasoning, even if it requires changing a cherished habit?

Blame of Taqlid and Warning Against Its Proponents
Sanusi concludes his criticism of taqlid and prays for guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi condemns blind imitation (taqlīd) and how its proponents deceive people, and you will appreciate the importance of reflection (naẓar) in attaining knowledge of Allah.

Core Idea
Blind imitation cuts off the path to knowing Allah.

In this concluding passage, Sanusi intensifies his critique of taqlīd and those who promote it. He exposes their deceptive tactics and warns that they spiritually impoverish the believers. He then turns to prayer, seeking divine guidance and protection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the shift from criticism to prayer.
2
Identify the two deceptions
The passage mentions two ways the proponents of taqlīd deceive people. Find them and note the contrast.
One claims superior knowledge, the other claims safety.
3
Reflect on the poetic quote
Sanusi quotes a line of poetry. What does it imply about those who forbid reflection?
The metaphor is about calling the dead.
4
Consider the prayer
Sanusi ends with a personal prayer. What does he ask for? How does this relate to the lesson?
He asks for guidance and protection from evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنقتصر على هذا القدر في الإشارة إلى ذم التقليدِ ، وذم مَنْ يحرِّضُ عليهِ ، ويذمُّ النظر في علم التوحيدِ ويُحرِّمُهُ ، ويقطع طريق المعرفة بالله تعالى على عبادِ اللهِ ، ويغرُّهم تارةً بأنَّهم على أحسنِ معرفة ، وتارةً بأنَّ تقليدهم صحيح ليسَ فِيهِ مَتْلَفَةٌ ، والله سبحانَهُ حسيب شياطين الإنس القاطعين على المساكين طرق معايشهم الدينيَّةِ . لَقَدْ أَسْمَعْتَ لَوْ نَادَيْتَ حَيّاً وَلَكِنْ لا حَيَاةَ لِمَنْ تُنَادِي . والله سبحانَهُ أسألُ أنْ يلهمنا رشدَ أنفسنا ، وأنْ يقينا شرَّ أنفسنا وشرَّ كل ذي شرّ ، بفضلِهِ وكرمِهِ ، وصلَّى الله على سيدنا ومولانا محمد نبيه وعبده
And we will limit ourselves to this much in indicating the blame of blind imitation (al-taqlīd), and the blame of those who incite it, and who blame reflection (al-naẓar) in the science of divine unity (ʿilm al-tawḥīd) and forbid it, and cut off the path of knowledge of Allah Most High from His servants, and deceive them sometimes by saying that they are upon the best knowledge, and sometimes by saying that their blind imitation is correct with no destruction in it. And Allah, glorified is He, is the reckoner of the satans among mankind who cut off from the poor the paths of their religious livelihoods. Indeed you would have made hear if you called a living being, but there is no life for the one you call. And Allah, glorified is He, I ask to inspire us with righteousness of ourselves, and to protect us from the evil of ourselves and the evil of every possessor of evil, by His grace and generosity. And may Allah bless our master and patron Muḥammad, His prophet and servant.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a concluding exhortation in which Sanusi summarizes his criticism of blind imitation (taqlīd) and its proponents. He states that he has said enough to indicate the blameworthiness of taqlīd and of those who encourage it, who also disparage and forbid rational reflection (naẓar) in the science of tawḥīd. These people, he says, cut off the path of maʿrifa (knowledge) of Allah from His servants. They deceive the people in two ways: sometimes by claiming that they already possess the best knowledge, and sometimes by saying that their taqlīd is sound and involves no danger (matlafa). Sanusi then invokes Allah as the reckoner against such 'satans among mankind' who deprive the poor of their religious livelihoods. He quotes a line of poetry: 'You would have made hear if you called a living being, but there is no life for the one you call,' implying that those who forbid reflection are spiritually dead and cannot respond. The passage ends with a prayer for divine guidance, protection from evil, and blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad.

Key Terms
تقليد
نظر
معرفة
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that blindly following religious authorities (taqlīd) is always safe and sufficient for attaining true knowledge.
Correction: Sanusi warns that taqlīd without reflection can cut off the path to knowledge of Allah and deceive people into thinking they are on the right path while they are not.

Checkpoint
What do the proponents of taqlīd say to deceive people according to this passage?
A) That reflection is obligatory upon everyone
B) That their taqlīd is correct and contains no danger
C) That knowledge of Allah is impossible
D) That the poor have no religious duties

Key Takeaway
Blind imitation (taqlīd) that rejects reflection (naẓar) is blameworthy and can cut off the path to true knowledge of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever blindly accepted a belief about Allah without personally reflecting on its truth? How does this passage challenge the comfort of that uncritical acceptance? What will you do to replace that with genuine knowledge?
?
The passage warns of 'satans among mankind' who cut off religious livelihoods. Could you be unintentionally playing that role for someone else by discouraging their questions or deep thinking about faith? How will you change that today?
?
Sanusi prays to be protected from the evil of the self. What specific step will you take this week to ensure your soul does not lead you to prefer comfortable imitation over the hard work of knowing Allah?

Dialectic and Rhetoric: Persuasion and Acceptance
Sanusi defines dialectic and rhetoric and states their purposes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions and purposes of dialectic and rhetoric, and how they differ from demonstration.

Core Idea
Dialectic targets the incapable; rhetoric targets the masses.

Sanusi begins by distinguishing two of the five logical arts: dialectic and rhetoric. Both aim to persuade, but they rely on different premises and address different audiences. Let’s examine his definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on dialectic and rhetoric
Read the Arabic text slowly, noting the structure: first dialectic, then rhetoric. Identify the purpose of each.
The purpose of dialectic is given first, then rhetoric.
2
Understand the types of premises
Notice that rhetoric uses accepted or probable premises, illustrated by the example of a thief at night. Dialectic does not specify premises here.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: Why would Sanusi consider these arts less certain than demonstration? The commentary will clarify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والغرضُ مِنَ الجدلِ : إقناع قاصر عنِ البرهان ، أو إلزام الخصم
والخطابة : هي ما تألَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتٍ مقبولةٍ مِنْ شخص مُعتقَد فيه ؛ لزهد فيه ونحوِهِ ، أو مُقدِّماتٍ مظنونةٍ ؛ كقولنا : هذا يدور في الليل بالسلاح ، وكلُّ مَنْ يدورُ في الليل بالسلاح فهو لص ؛ فهذا لص .
والغرضُ مِنَ الخَطابةِ : ترغيب السامعين .
والشعرُ : هو ما تأَلَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتٍ مُتخيَّلة لترغيب النفس في شيء ، أو تنفيرها عنه ؛ فالأول : كقولِ مَنْ يُرغَبُ في شربِ الخمر : هذه خمرةٌ ، وكلُّ خمرة ياقوتةٌ سيَّالة ؛ فهذهِ ياقوتة سيَّالة ، والثاني : كقولِ مَنْ يُنفِّرُ عن شرب العسل : هذا عسل ، وكلُّ عسل فهو قيء حيوان ؛ فهذا قيء حيوان .
والغرضُ مِنَ الشعرِ : انفعالُ النفسِ ؛ أي : تأثرها وتكيفها بحب شيء أو بغضه .
والمغالطةُ : ما تألَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتِ شبيهة بالحقِّ وليسَتْ بحقِّ ؛ وتُسمَّى سفسطةً ، أو مُقدِّماتِ شبيهة بالمقدِّماتِ المشهورة وليسَتْ بها ؛ وتُسمَّى مشاغبةً ، أو مِنْ مُقدِّماتِ وهميَّةٍ كاذبة
وبه تعلم : أن الوزن الشعري المعروف لا يشترط هنا
مثالُ الأَوَّلِ : قولنا في صورةِ فرس في حائط : هذا فرس ، وكلُّ فرس صمَّالُ ؛ فهذا صهَّالُ
ومثالُ الثاني : قولنا في شخص يَخبِطُ في البحثِ : هذا يُكلَّمُ العلماء بألفاظ العلم حتى يسكتوا ، وكلُّ مَنْ يُكلِّم العلماء بألفاظ العلم فهو عالم ؛ فهذا عالم .
ومثالُ الثالث : قولنا في حَبْلٍ على صورة حيَّةٍ : هذا شكل حيَّةٍ وكلُّ ما كانَ كذلكَ فالحزمُ الفِرارُ منهُ ؛ فهذا الحزمُ الفِرارُ منه والغرضُ مِنَ المغالطة : تغطية الحق ، وتزيين الباطل
والمُعتمد عليهِ مِنْ هذه الصناعات الخمس : البرهان
The purpose of dialectic (jadal) is persuasion of one incapable of demonstration (burhān), or compelling an opponent. Rhetoric (khatāba) is what is composed of premises accepted from a person in whom one has confidence, due to asceticism in him and the like, or probable premises; like our saying: 'This one walks at night with weapons, and whoever walks at night with weapons is a thief; so this is a thief.' The purpose of rhetoric is to persuade the listeners. Poetry (shiʿr) is what is composed of imagined premises to attract the soul to something or to repel it; the first is like the saying of one who encourages drinking wine: 'This is wine, and every wine is a flowing ruby; so this is a flowing ruby.' The second is like the saying of one who discourages drinking honey: 'This is honey, and every honey is animal vomit; so this is animal vomit.' The purpose of poetry is the soul's being affected, i.e., its impression and disposition towards love or hatred of something. Sophistry (mugālaṭa) is what is composed of premises resembling truth but not true; it is called sophistry (sufsaṭa), or premises resembling well-known premises but not well-known; it is called quarrelsomeness (mushāghaba), or from false imaginary premises. By this you know that the well-known poetic meter is not required here. Example of the first: our saying about a picture of a horse on a wall: 'This is a horse, and every horse neighs; so this neighs.' Example of the second: our saying about a person who confuses in discussion: 'This one addresses scholars with terms of knowledge until they become silent, and whoever addresses scholars with terms of knowledge is a scholar; so this is a scholar.' Example of the third: our saying about a rope in the shape of a snake: 'This is the shape of a snake, and everything that is like that, the prudent thing is to flee from it; so the prudent thing is to flee from it.' The purpose of sophistry is to cover truth and adorn falsehood. The relied-upon among these five arts is demonstration (burhān).
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the five logical arts used in argumentation, particularly in kalām. The author defines each art and states its purpose. First, dialectic (jadal) is used to persuade someone incapable of following a demonstration or to force an opponent into a contradiction. Second, rhetoric (khatāba) relies on premises accepted on authority or probability, aiming to move an audience. Third, poetry (shiʿr) uses imagined premises to evoke emotional attraction or repulsion; the author notes that conventional poetic meter is not a requirement here. Fourth, sophistry (mugālaṭa) is divided into three types: (a) arguments resembling truth but false (sufsaṭa), (b) arguments resembling well-known premises but not (mushāghaba), and (c) arguments from false imaginary premises. Examples illustrate each: a picture of a horse, a pretender to learning, and a rope mistaken for a snake. The purpose of sophistry is to conceal truth and decorate falsehood. Finally, the author states that among these five, only demonstration (burhān) is the authoritative method for attaining certainty.

Key Terms
جدل
خطابة
إقناع
مقدمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of dialectic according to this passage?
A) To produce certainty
B) To persuade one incapable of demonstration or compel an opponent
C) To adorn falsehood
D) To affect the soul

Key Takeaway
Dialectic and rhetoric are persuasive arts that do not achieve the certainty of demonstration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a religious lecture, do you accept it because the speaker is respected or because you have verified the evidence? Are you content with rhetoric when you should seek demonstration?
?
Have you ever used rhetoric (appealing to authority or probable reasons) to convince someone of a truth without having certain proof yourself? How does that affect your accountability before Allah?
?
Which of your beliefs about Islam are based on rhetoric (trusted authority) rather than demonstration? Are you willing to examine them and seek certainty, even if it is uncomfortable?

Poetry: Imagined Premises and Emotional Effect
Sanusi defines poetry and gives examples of imagined premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how poetry uses imagined premises to influence the soul toward attraction or repulsion.

Core Idea
Poetry works through imagination, not logic.

Sanusi now turns to poetry, explaining that its premises are imagined rather than real. Its goal is to move the soul emotionally, not to establish truth. Let’s read his definition and examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on poetry
Read the Arabic text. Notice the two examples: one encouraging wine, one discouraging honey.
The examples are analogical but based on imagined comparisons.
2
Identify the purpose
Sanusi states that the purpose is the soul’s being affected – attraction or repulsion.
3
Note the side comment about meter
Later in the text (in the next lesson), he mentions that poetic meter is not required. This is a clarification for the logical definition.
This comment appears in the sophistry section but pertains to poetry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والغرضُ مِنَ الجدلِ : إقناع قاصر عنِ البرهان ، أو إلزام الخصم
والخطابة : هي ما تألَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتٍ مقبولةٍ مِنْ شخص مُعتقَد فيه ؛ لزهد فيه ونحوِهِ ، أو مُقدِّماتٍ مظنونةٍ ؛ كقولنا : هذا يدور في الليل بالسلاح ، وكلُّ مَنْ يدورُ في الليل بالسلاح فهو لص ؛ فهذا لص .
والغرضُ مِنَ الخَطابةِ : ترغيب السامعين .
والشعرُ : هو ما تأَلَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتٍ مُتخيَّلة لترغيب النفس في شيء ، أو تنفيرها عنه ؛ فالأول : كقولِ مَنْ يُرغَبُ في شربِ الخمر : هذه خمرةٌ ، وكلُّ خمرة ياقوتةٌ سيَّالة ؛ فهذهِ ياقوتة سيَّالة ، والثاني : كقولِ مَنْ يُنفِّرُ عن شرب العسل : هذا عسل ، وكلُّ عسل فهو قيء حيوان ؛ فهذا قيء حيوان .
والغرضُ مِنَ الشعرِ : انفعالُ النفسِ ؛ أي : تأثرها وتكيفها بحب شيء أو بغضه .
والمغالطةُ : ما تألَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتِ شبيهة بالحقِّ وليسَتْ بحقِّ ؛ وتُسمَّى سفسطةً ، أو مُقدِّماتِ شبيهة بالمقدِّماتِ المشهورة وليسَتْ بها ؛ وتُسمَّى مشاغبةً ، أو مِنْ مُقدِّماتِ وهميَّةٍ كاذبة
وبه تعلم : أن الوزن الشعري المعروف لا يشترط هنا
مثالُ الأَوَّلِ : قولنا في صورةِ فرس في حائط : هذا فرس ، وكلُّ فرس صمَّالُ ؛ فهذا صهَّالُ
ومثالُ الثاني : قولنا في شخص يَخبِطُ في البحثِ : هذا يُكلَّمُ العلماء بألفاظ العلم حتى يسكتوا ، وكلُّ مَنْ يُكلِّم العلماء بألفاظ العلم فهو عالم ؛ فهذا عالم .
ومثالُ الثالث : قولنا في حَبْلٍ على صورة حيَّةٍ : هذا شكل حيَّةٍ وكلُّ ما كانَ كذلكَ فالحزمُ الفِرارُ منهُ ؛ فهذا الحزمُ الفِرارُ منه والغرضُ مِنَ المغالطة : تغطية الحق ، وتزيين الباطل
والمُعتمد عليهِ مِنْ هذه الصناعات الخمس : البرهان
The purpose of dialectic (jadal) is persuasion of one incapable of demonstration (burhān), or compelling an opponent. Rhetoric (khatāba) is what is composed of premises accepted from a person in whom one has confidence, due to asceticism in him and the like, or probable premises; like our saying: 'This one walks at night with weapons, and whoever walks at night with weapons is a thief; so this is a thief.' The purpose of rhetoric is to persuade the listeners. Poetry (shiʿr) is what is composed of imagined premises to attract the soul to something or to repel it; the first is like the saying of one who encourages drinking wine: 'This is wine, and every wine is a flowing ruby; so this is a flowing ruby.' The second is like the saying of one who discourages drinking honey: 'This is honey, and every honey is animal vomit; so this is animal vomit.' The purpose of poetry is the soul's being affected, i.e., its impression and disposition towards love or hatred of something. Sophistry (mugālaṭa) is what is composed of premises resembling truth but not true; it is called sophistry (sufsaṭa), or premises resembling well-known premises but not well-known; it is called quarrelsomeness (mushāghaba), or from false imaginary premises. By this you know that the well-known poetic meter is not required here. Example of the first: our saying about a picture of a horse on a wall: 'This is a horse, and every horse neighs; so this neighs.' Example of the second: our saying about a person who confuses in discussion: 'This one addresses scholars with terms of knowledge until they become silent, and whoever addresses scholars with terms of knowledge is a scholar; so this is a scholar.' Example of the third: our saying about a rope in the shape of a snake: 'This is the shape of a snake, and everything that is like that, the prudent thing is to flee from it; so the prudent thing is to flee from it.' The purpose of sophistry is to cover truth and adorn falsehood. The relied-upon among these five arts is demonstration (burhān).
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the five logical arts used in argumentation, particularly in kalām. The author defines each art and states its purpose. First, dialectic (jadal) is used to persuade someone incapable of following a demonstration or to force an opponent into a contradiction. Second, rhetoric (khatāba) relies on premises accepted on authority or probability, aiming to move an audience. Third, poetry (shiʿr) uses imagined premises to evoke emotional attraction or repulsion; the author notes that conventional poetic meter is not a requirement here. Fourth, sophistry (mugālaṭa) is divided into three types: (a) arguments resembling truth but false (sufsaṭa), (b) arguments resembling well-known premises but not (mushāghaba), and (c) arguments from false imaginary premises. Examples illustrate each: a picture of a horse, a pretender to learning, and a rope mistaken for a snake. The purpose of sophistry is to conceal truth and decorate falsehood. Finally, the author states that among these five, only demonstration (burhān) is the authoritative method for attaining certainty.

Key Terms
شعر
مقدمات متخيلة
انفعال النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of poetry according to this passage?
A) To produce certain knowledge
B) To persuade an opponent
C) To affect the soul with love or hatred
D) To conceal truth

Key Takeaway
Poetry uses imagined premises to influence emotions, not to establish truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of something you desire that you know is harmful. What imagined premise (like the 'flowing ruby') does your soul use to make it seem attractive? Write it down. Now, are you willing to replace that image with the truth and turn away from that desire?
?
Sanusi says poetry aims to make the soul love or hate something without concern for truth. Consider one deeply held belief you have—is it really based on certain evidence, or have you been persuaded by an emotional image? What would it cost you to examine it honestly?
?
Identify one emotional appeal you encounter daily (an ad, a social media post, a political slogan). Can you see the imagined premise it uses? Commit this week to not letting your soul be swayed by such images without first checking their truth. Will you take that step?

Method of Learning
beginner
6 min
Sophistry: Deceptive Arguments and the Superiority of Demonstration
Sanusi classifies sophistry into three types and declares demonstration the only reliable art.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of sophistry and why demonstration is the only relied-upon art among the five.

Core Idea
Sophistry deceives; demonstration alone delivers certainty.

In this final part, Sanusi describes sophistry (maghalata) with its three subtypes and provides examples. He concludes by stating that among the five arts, only demonstration (burhan) is trustworthy. Let’s read carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on sophistry
Read the Arabic text from 'والغالطة' to the end. Note the three types and their examples.
The text includes a note about poetic meter, which pertains to the previous lesson.
2
Identify the three types
Sophistry uses premises resembling truth (sophism), resembling well-known premises (quarrelsomeness), or false imaginary premises.
3
Study the examples
Each example illustrates one type: horse picture, pretender to learning, rope as snake.
4
Understand the purpose and conclusion
Sophistry aims to cover truth and adorn falsehood. Sanusi ends by affirming that demonstration is the relied-upon art.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والغرضُ مِنَ الجدلِ : إقناع قاصر عنِ البرهان ، أو إلزام الخصم
والخطابة : هي ما تألَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتٍ مقبولةٍ مِنْ شخص مُعتقَد فيه ؛ لزهد فيه ونحوِهِ ، أو مُقدِّماتٍ مظنونةٍ ؛ كقولنا : هذا يدور في الليل بالسلاح ، وكلُّ مَنْ يدورُ في الليل بالسلاح فهو لص ؛ فهذا لص .

ومثالُ الثالث : قولنا في حَبْلٍ على صورة حيَّةٍ : هذا شكل حيَّةٍ وكلُّ ما كانَ كذلكَ فالحزمُ الفِرارُ منهُ ؛ فهذا الحزمُ الفِرارُ منه والغرضُ مِنَ المغالطة : تغطية الحق ، وتزيين الباطل

The purpose of dialectic (jadal) is persuasion of one incapable of demonstration (burhān), or compelling an opponent. Rhetoric (khatāba) is what is composed of premises accepted from a person in whom one has confidence, due to asceticism in him and the like, or probable premises; like our saying: 'This one walks at night with weapons, and whoever walks at night with weapons is a thief; so this is a thief.' The purpose of rhetoric is to persuade the listeners. Poetry (shiʿr) is what is composed of imagined premises to attract the soul to something or to repel it; the first is like the saying of one who encourages drinking wine: 'This is wine, and every wine is a flowing ruby; so this is a flowing ruby.' The second is like the saying of one who discourages drinking honey: 'This is honey, and every honey is animal vomit; so this is animal vomit.' The purpose of poetry is the soul's being affected, i.e., its impression and disposition towards love or hatred of something. Sophistry (mugālaṭa) is what is composed of premises resembling truth but not true; it is called sophistry (sufsaṭa), or premises resembling well-known premises but not well-known; it is called quarrelsomeness (mushāghaba), or from false imaginary premises. By this you know that the well-known poetic meter is not required here. Example of the first: our saying about a picture of a horse on a wall: 'This is a horse, and every horse neighs; so this neighs.' Example of the second: our saying about a person who confuses in discussion: 'This one addresses scholars with terms of knowledge until they become silent, and whoever addresses scholars with terms of knowledge is a scholar; so this is a scholar.' Example of the third: our saying about a rope in the shape of a snake: 'This is the shape of a snake, and everything that is like that, the prudent thing is to flee from it; so the prudent thing is to flee from it.' The purpose of sophistry is to cover truth and adorn falsehood. The relied-upon among these five arts is demonstration (burhān).

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the five logical arts used in argumentation, particularly in kalām. The author defines each art and states its purpose. First, dialectic (jadal) is used to persuade someone incapable of following a demonstration or to force an opponent into a contradiction. Second, rhetoric (khatāba) relies on premises accepted on authority or probability, aiming to move an audience. Third, poetry (shiʿr) uses imagined premises to evoke emotional attraction or repulsion; the author notes that conventional poetic meter is not a requirement here. Fourth, sophistry (mugālaṭa) is divided into three types: (a) arguments resembling truth but false (sufsaṭa), (b) arguments resembling well-known premises but not (mushāghaba), and (c) arguments from false imaginary premises. Examples illustrate each: a picture of a horse, a pretender to learning, and a rope mistaken for a snake. The purpose of sophistry is to conceal truth and decorate falsehood. Finally, the author states that among these five, only demonstration (burhān) is the authoritative method for attaining certainty.

Key Terms
مغالطة
سفسطة
مشاغبة
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think sophistry is a single method; the passage shows it has three distinct types.
Correction: Sophistry includes three subtypes: sophism proper, quarrelsomeness, and imaginary premises.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the five logical arts is the relied-upon one?
A) Dialectic
B) Rhetoric
C) Poetry
D) Demonstration

Key Takeaway
Among all logical arts, only demonstration provides certainty; sophistry deliberately deceives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you were convinced by a smooth talker or a clever story that later turned out to be false. What was the 'premise resembling truth' that deceived you? How will you guard against such sophistry in the future?
?
Sanusi says sophistry aims to 'cover truth and adorn falsehood'. Is there an area of your life where you are covering up a hard truth with comfortable excuses? What would it cost you to face it honestly?
?
The passage declares demonstration as the only relied-upon art. In your pursuit of faith, are you settling for arguments that sound convincing but lack true certainty? What is one belief you hold that you cannot prove with demonstration, and what will you do about it?
